THE RED PAGE.
THOUGH “Lasca” is in America a popular
recitation piece, nothing seems to be known
of its author, nor can any other verses be traced
to him. “Lasca” is said to have originally
appeared in the. English maga. Temple Bar,
and Frank Desprez was possibly a young English-
man ranching (i.e., as a rule, cattle-farming) in
Texas,
LASCA.
I want free life, and I want fresh air ;
And I sigh for the canter after the cattle,
The crack of the whips like shots in a battle,
The melley of horns and hoofs and heads
That wars and wrangles and scatters and spreads ;
The green beneath and the blue above,
And dash and danger, and life and love.
And Lasca!
Lasca used to ride
On a mouse-grey mustang close to my side,
With blue serape and blight-belled spur ;
I laughed with joy as I looked at her 1
Little knew she of books or creeds ;
An ‘ Ave Maria ’ sufficed her needs ;
Little she cared, save to be by my side,
To ride with me, and ever to ride,
From San Saha’s shore to Lavaca’s tide.
She was as bold as the billows that beat;
She, was as wild as the breezes that blow;
From her little head to her little feet
She was swayed in her suppleness to and fro
By each gust of passion ; a sapling pine,
That grows on the edge of a Kansas bluff,
And wars with the wind when the weather is rough,
Is like this Lasca, this love of mine.
She would hunger that I might eat,
Would take the bitter, and leave me the sweet;
But once, when I made her jealous for fun.
At something I’d whispered, or looked, or done,
One Sunday, in San Antonio,
To a glorious girl on the Alamo,
She drew from her garter a dear little dagger,
And—sting of a wasp I—it made me stagger 1
An inch to the left, or an inch to the right,
And I shouldn’t be maundering here to-night;
But she sobbed, and, sobbing, so swiftly hound
Her torn reboso about the wound,
That I quite forgave her. Scratches don’t count
InJTexas', down by the Rio Grande.
Her eye was brown —a deep, deep brown ;
Her hair was darker than her eye ;
And something in her smile and frown,
Curled crimson lip and instep high,
Showed that there ran in each blue vein,
Mixed with the milder Aztec strain,
The vigorous vintage of Old Spain.
She was alive in every limb
With feeling, to the finger-tips;
And when the sun is like a fire.
And sky one shining, soft sapphire,
One does not drink in little sips.
The air was heavy, the night was hot,
I sat by her side, and forgot—forgot;
Forgot the herd that were taking their rest,
Forgot that the air was close opprest,
That the Texas norther comes sudden and soon,
In the dead of night or the blaze of noon ;
That, once let the herd at its breath take fright,
Nothing on earth can stop the flight;
And woe to the rider, and woe to the steed,
Who falls in front of their mad stampede!
Was that thunder ? I grasped the cord
Of my swift mustang without a word.
I sprang to the saddle, and she olung behind.
Away 1 on a hot chase down the wind 1
But never was fox-hunt half so hard,
And never was steed so little spared,
For we rode for our lives. You shall hear how we fared
In Texas, down by the Rio Grande.
The mustang flew,'and we urged him on ;
There was one chance left, and you have but one—
Halt, jump to the ground, and shoot your horse ;
Crouch under his carcass, and take your chance ;
And if the steers in their frantic course
Don’t batter you both to pieces at once,
You may thank your star ; if not, good-bye
To the quickening kiss and the long-drawn sigh,
And the open air, and the open sky.
In Texas, down by the Rio Grande.
The cattle gained on us, and just as I felt
For my old six-shooter, behind in my belt,
Down came the mustang, and down came we.
Clinging together, and—what was the rest T
A body that spread itself on my breast,
Two arms that shielded my flizzy head,
Two lips that hard on my lips were prest;
Then came thunder in my ears,
As over us surged the sea of steers,
Blows that beat blood into my eyes,
And when I could rise—
Lasca was dead.
I gouged out a grave a few teet deep,
And there in Earth’s arms I laid her to sleep;
And there she is lying, and no one knows ;
And the summer shines, and the winter snows.
For many a day the flowers have spread
A pall of petals over her head ;
And the little gray hawk hangs aloft in the air.
And the sly coyote trots here and there,
And the black snake glides and glitters and slides
Into a rift in a cottonwood-tree ;
And the buzzard sails on,
And comes and is gone,
Stately and still like a ship at sea.
And I wonder why I do not care
For the things that are like the things that were.
Does half my heart lie buried there,
In Texas, down by the Eio Grande ?
FRANK DERPRE[?]
C CONSIDERATION of Alex. Montgomery’s
j book of stories (published by Geo. Robertson
and Co.) brings the reflection that no one among
local writers has a better talent for crisp, clean
narrative, with the words cunningly chosen and
placed to give greatest effect in least compass, and
the balanced sentences ringing like anvil-strokes.
A weary editor, one-half whose time is employed
in linking relatives to antecedents, gathering up
sprawling sentences, and digging a half-expressed
thought from a mass of clumsy verbiage, is perhaps
inclined to welcbme too warmly the man who
writes like this—
Across the long perspective of the mangrove flats the
sun glared fierce and crimson. From the black mud-
banks, white, pestilential vapours rose upon the torpid
air. A subtler miasm quivered over the long stretches
of mephitic ooze, against the sickly green of which
gleamed out the hideous reds and yellows of fantastic
crabs that scuttled-as the boat came near—into pools
prismatic with stagnation. Beneath the slimy surface
the banded water-snakes glanced to and fro ; from the
reed-beds came in hungry swarms the tiger-mosquitoes ;
and from the mangrove roots the sword-flies darted out
in swift detachments upon the half-nude rowers—swelter-
ing in a bath of vegetable fetor.
> » * *
This is not the best prose : it is too hard and
metallic, too lacking in softness and sympathy;
and the hardly-varied cadences grow monoto-
nous. English prose is becoming, on the whole,
less musical than it was for Sir Thomas
Browne and Sir Walter Raleigh. The Macaul-
ayan wave has submerged it : it is as glitter-
ing as you please, but too terse, too regular,
too staccato, to give the finest stimulus. Mont-
gomery wants the luxury, the melody, the magic
of writers like (say) De Quineey or Scevenson.
His pages fall as stiffly as a carven toga : theirs are
often a garment closely woven to the thought. De
Quineey’s sentences ripple like water now in
shade and now in shine ; Montgomery’s are fixed
in the glare of an unwinking noon. But there is
no mistaking their illumination ; his is an ideal
journalistic style. The lucid rhythm of, e.g.—
Across the long perspective of the mangrove flats the
sun glared fierce and crimson—
is a craftsman’s joy. To write so you must have
clear thought, a knack of words, and a good ear
(not necessarily delicate). Try it in other ways—
Over the long perspective of the mangrove flats the sun
glared fierce and crimson.
Across the perspective of the mangrove flats the sun
glared fierce and crimson.
Across the long perspective of the mangrove flats the
sun glared crimson and fierce.
Across the long perspective of the mangrove flats the
sun shone fierce and crimson.
Re-combine or alter these words in half-a-hundred
ways, and each time you tall short, in cadence or
meaning, of Montgomery’s result. Yet originally,
doubtless, the phrases shaped themselves in his
mind almost without effort. There was possibly
verbal emendation —little or much —but the form
and swing of the sentence came as if by instinct.
This is what it is to be able to write.
* * * »
But study the sentence further, and see why,
e.g., “over” will not do instead of “aoross” in
the first alternative given. It will not do, first,
because it has not the direct, sweeping meaning
of “ across ” in relation to the rays of the level
sun. With “over,” the glare hangs in the air;
with “across” it is down touching the ground,
lighting up the mangrove flats, as they must be
lighted up if the mind is to absorb the whole pic-
ture. And it will not do, second, because “ over ”
is a trochee and “across” an iambus, and the
rhythm of the sentence is markedly iambic.
Acrdss the 16ng perspective 6f the mangrove flits the
Siin glared fierce and crimson.
The iambics are not perfect; but this is the way
in which the average person would unconsciously
accent these words —stressing “ of ” and slurring
“grove’’and “glared.” And on examination a
well-marked preference for iambic rhythm will be
found running through all Montgomery’s prose.
Here is a sentence from “Her Father’s Head,”
describing the interior of a Dyak head-house—
Around the circle of the roof, in close and curving
rows, the monstrous trophies hung—dim blots of greater
darkness in the shadow, but starting out, in patches
where the white light fell, into hideous caricature of
humanity, with huge blank orbits where were one time
eyes, and shark-mouths grinning to the moon in ghastly
travesty of laughter.
Accented —
Aroiind the circle 6t the r6of in clAss and cdrving rAws
the grisly trAphies hiing . . .
but Stirling Aut in pitches wh Are the white light fAll.. .
with hhge black Arbits whAre were Ane time eyes . . ».
These fragments would pass in iambic verse, and
even the omitted phrases are iambic in character,
peppered with a few weak syllables to prevent
them from falling into the affectation of prose
verse.
* • * *
It is interesting (and quite unprofitable in the
absence of evidence) to speculate on the physical
reasons of Montgomery’s marked iambic prefer-
ence. Possibly his heart is weaker in systole
than in diastole : instead of a regular lub-dub it
beats a biassed lup-dub—quaver-orotchet in lieu
of crotchet-crotchet. Thus in a slight valve-weak-
ness, making a permanent change in the ear-
pulses, one might find the cause of the strong beat,
the monotonous music of his prose.
« * * *
The bias is unconscious, of course ; for it is nob
consistent with the best effects for which Mont-
gomery strives. The treasury of prose is far too
rich to open to a single key. The regular rhythms
which the popular ear demands in verse are in
prose inadmissible —not because mere dogma bars
them, but because in the result—which is the test
of their artistic value—they spoil the prose. Some
passages held as exceptions are of doubtful
authority. The familiar death of “little Nell,”
for example, is in parts effective writing, but it is
not good prose. Ingersoll tells why. (He is
speaking of poetry, but the dictum applies a for-
tiori to prose.)
I do not mean by rhythm a recurring accent at accur-
ately measured intervals. Perfect time is the death of
music. There should always be room for eager haste and
delicious delay . . .
Yet through much of Ingersoll’s work—and he
has composed some of the most notable rhetorical
prose in English—the regular iambic rhythm
beats strongly. The speech “ At a Child’s Grave ”
begins—
My Friends: I kn6w how vdin it is to gild a grief with
words, and y<st I wish to take from 6very grave its fdar.
Here in this wdrld, where life and d6ath are 6qual kings,
all should be brive enoiigh to mbet what All the dead have
mAt.
Dickens also runs to iambics when he purples.
As in the passage referred to —
Of Avery tAar that sdrrowing mortals shAd onsiich green
graves, some go6d is bdrn, some gAntler nAture cdmes.
So that Montgomery errs in good company
And curious readers who examine further may be
surprised to find what a grip the rhythmic iambus
has on modern English prose, when the prosers
wish to be especially forceful. The cause ?
JANUARY Cosmopolitan prints an interest-
ing article describing the making of an ideal
newspaper by an enthusiast to whom a millionaire
gave a free hand. Says the millionaire’s agent
to the enthusiast—
‘ You are like a great many newspaper writers that I
have met. You have in your mind an ideal newspaper,
while your lives are spent in writing for anything but ideal
newspapers.’
• That is true. I never saw a man yet who thoroughly
admired the newspaper for which he worked, except the
proprietor.’
An obvious retort is that this is inevitable, since
ideals differ widely, and even if the man with the
best ideal became proprietor his subordinates
would not thoroughly admire his ideal newspaper.
But this retort is not fully satisfactory. There is
among intelligent journalists a certain unanimity
of opinion as to what is a good paper, just as there
is among intelligent readers a certain unanimity
of opinion regarding standard authors—though
one may have an individual preference for fiction,
another for history, and so on.
* * * *
The enthusiast proceeds—
•ln plain words, I have not been able to treat myself to
the luxury of expressing my own opinions in black and
white for a number of years. As a reporter and cor-
respondent I have been obliged to color my facts, more
or less, to suit the newspaper which employed me. No
matter how impartial an editor may think he is, and
whatever may be the directions given by him to his re-
porters or subordinates, they thoroughly understand that
their popularity and their prospect for advancement
depend largely upon their collecting the facts and views
which will have a tendency to substantiate the actual
views of their employer. Aside from this personal ques-
tion, the average newspaper has a fixed set of ideas.
Whether they relate to mere partisanship or mere assump-
tion of independence, the suboidinates have but the
freedom of men engaged in working on a chain gang.
The person who seeks to make his individuality too
marked soon becomes an object of criticism, and the
moment the readers of a newspaper begin to complain to
the editor, through that wonderful column known as the
people’s column, there comes a warning, and if the warn-
ing is not heeded, loss of employment.’
‘You put yourself in a very critical attitude toward
the newspapers. Is it true, then, that you have found in
the exercise of your profession neither pleasure nor intel-
lectual profit ?’
‘ Neither. The practice of modern journalism has no
tendency to bring out the best qualities of the individual.
Men are moulded after a certain pattern, according to
the newspaper employing them, and individuality is dis-
tinctly discouraged. Journalism is a trade ; no one
should call that a profession which eats up the best life
of a man. When he should be arriving at the years of
his greatest value, in almost any other occupation of life,
' in this one exacting, nerve-exhausting calling he finds
himself growing less and less valuable, and is soon pointed
out by the youngsters as only fit for the purpose of writ-
ing reminiscences.’
Many journalists will endorse this to some extent.
* » * •
And there is truth in this, from an intellectual
standpoint :
‘You have doubtless studied art, and therefore compre-
hend the technical meaning of the term called ‘values’
—that is, the scale of proper proportion of things. This
constitutes the chief merit of great pictures. It seems
to me that the modern newspaper lacks this quality more
than it does anything else. There is a lack of the proper
sense of proportion. The subjects which are thrust to
the front in the order of their publication often are ex-
aggerated beyond the power of their true perspective
Let me give you an instance. Let us suppose that a
janitor, a highly esteemed janitor in charge of some res-
pectable up town flat, becomes weary of the routine of hit
life and deserts his family to elope with some interesting
chambermaid in the neighborhood. Now there is a story
which in a great many modern newspapers would be pre-
sented with about the same spread-eagle display that
should be accorded to the news of a foreign war. In my
judgment, infractions of the law are made altogether too
conspicuous. By this I mean that stories of crime and
stories of exposure should have their place in the news-
paper, but it does not seem to me chat they should occupy
the front place ; and where the people are of humble and
ordinary life, their peccadilloes, misdemeanors or crimes
should not entitle them to furnish more than their men-
tion, and then only when they fall into the hands of the
law and are actually dealt with by the courts.’
* * • *
Intellectually, this is correct; journalistically,
it is fallacious.' The average newspaper is issued
to make money —chiefly through advertisements.
To secure advertisements it must have ‘circula-
tion’; to secure ‘circulation’ it must please its
patrons ; to please its patrons it must give them
what they like to read. In America, Aus-
tralia, and elsewhere, the number of persons who
see events in their true human perspective is
small; in the area which, e.g., a single daily news-
paper can expect to cover it is very small. Ninety-
five per cent, of female readers, perhaps 70 per
cent, of male readers in Sydney, say, are pro-
foundly impressed by events which happen near
them, and not at all impressed by infinitely
more important events (to the human race)
which happen a long way off. The educated
mind may be cosmopolitan, but the unedu-
cated mind is parochial; and probably four-
fifths of the people among whom the average
daily newspaper finds its purchasers are almost
wholly uneducated. If the paper would prosper
it must “ hit them where they live’’—give them
Butler and bicycles instead of bimetallism and im-
aginative Art.' The Sydney and Melbourne even-
ing papers, which have some sensational reputa-
tion, are possibly as intellectual as they can afford
to be. The pressure to heighten intellectual status
is small in proportion to the pressure to lower it.
Every fresh intellectual step in advance would
probably mean a shrinkage in subscriptions. It is
to the credit of Australian editors that, as a class,
they probably make their papers quite as intel-
lectual as their public will permit. Probably the
papers are rather above than below the standard
which the mass of the public prefers. And pro-
bably the average public standard—for a variety
of interesting reasons—is in Australia a little
higher than in America or even than in England ;
whence the compliments frequently paid by
foreign visitors to the Australian press.
gOMB LITTLE ITEMS.
They say Hubert Crackanthorpe’s exit was by way of
suicide. Certain letters found on the body have been
suppressed. Last seen with ‘a woman at Bordeaux.*
After that the Seine.
Kipling’s new long story, ‘ Captains Courageous,’
now running in a couple of the magazines, is readable—
nothing more. It is a diary of his experiences on a New-
foundland cod-fishing schooner, hung on the slightest peg
of plot—little more than a boy’s story, in fact; clever
and vigorous as Kipling’s work must be, but imperman-
ent and not (on the high plane) artistic.
Quiller Couch succeeds Zangwill as Pall Mall Maga-
zine commentator. ‘Q.’ is a pretty prattler, but he has
not the pith and force of Zangwill; and he is no critic—has
no sense of proportion. Too good-natured, perhaps ; for
he is friends with everybody, and no sooner does a
chum’s rushlight splutter than he all-hails my lord the
Sun. Your conscientious critio is almost of necessity a
sour, malevolent fellow with an evil eye—like Javert or
the incorruptible Robespierre—who pitilessly damns,
when it deserves it, the book of the man he has just
dined with.
EHEU!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ashes to ashes ! dust to dust!
Sound is my darling sleeping.
Gone are Passion and Strife and Lust
(Ashes to ashes 1 dust to dust!)
Deep in the mould and the graveyard rust,
Never he hears my weeping.
Ashes to ashes! dust to dust!
Sound is my darling sleeping.

Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
HARVEST TIME.
When the cranky German waggon,
With its ten or fifteen bag on
Gomes a-jerkin’ and a-joltin’ down the dusty,
limestone street,
And the “ Norther’s ” blowin’ blindin’,
j ro .^ ers are a-grindin’,
And the agent jabs his sampler thro’ the sackin’ to
the wheat,
Let em slide along the plank ! slide along ! slide
along !
Sixty bushels for the Bank ; slide along !
When your back is fairly breakin’
And your very fingers shakin’
With the heavin’, heavin’, heavin’, in the blarsted,
blazin’ sun ;
And the agent finds the spots out
And takes all his sample lots out
Where it’s rusty, pinched, or smutty—knockin’ off
five pound a ton ;
Sling ’em over with a jerk! slide along ! slide
along !
Sixty days of wasted work ! slide along 1
Sixty days a-ploughin’ mallee
In the God-forgotten valley
Of the creepin’, crawlin’ Murray, with the dingoes
for your mates !
Sow and harrow, roll and reap it,
But you get no show to keep it,
For it’s “Boom and bust yer biler” when the
cocky speculates !
Let the bankers take the lot: slide along ! slide
along !
Farmin’ mallee’s bloomin’ rot—slide along !
S.A.
NIL.
IRON ROOFS AND COUNTRY SCHOOL-
BOYS.
In a lonely backblocks school-house with the fierce
sun’s blood-red eye
Looking down in sullen anger from a smoky,
brazen sky,
With the bush-fires raging round us, and the only
breeze that blows
A sirocco from the north-west hot enough to singe
your clothes.
Here, amid my two-score charges, I an instant
meditate
On the blessings of instruction, and the wisdom of
a State
So extremely persevering in its zeal to educate
That it crowds some forty youngsters (sometimes
less and sometimes more)
In an iron-roofed slab shanty fifteen feet by
twenty-four,
With three pigeon-hole-like windows and a narrow
crazy door,
And the mercury just threatening to run over on
the floor.
Oh, those cursed iron roofings ! How they frizzle
up the brain
Of the schoolboy generation, making education
vain:
Surely Satan in his cunning and determination
fell
To blast Young Australia’s genius must have
sent them up from hell,
And our legislative Solons —ever Satan’s ready
tools —
Took them meekly, and pronounced them “Pro-
per roofings for our schools.”
And I fear me that the schoolboy, tough and
thick-skulled though he be,
Must go under in the struggle, and I very plainly
see
That, with broiling him in summer, and with
freezing him in June,
If these iron roofs continue there will be no
schoolboy soon.
He’ll be frizzled up and frozen off the surface of
the earth,
And the “ sylvan glades ” no longer will “ re-echo
with his mirth.”
But ’tis not for me to grumble, or your sympathy
implore,
For the doomed Australian schoolboy—l’m his
teacher —nothing more.
And “the standard” hangs before me, and free
standard measure must
Be poured into every youngster if old Fahrenheit
should “ bust.”
PEDAGOGUE.
WHERE THE LOST MEN GO.
O land of desire ! 0 land of a dream !
Where the rivers fret their gold ;
We see in the morning your waters gleam
With the light that tempts the bold ;
We gaze at noon-tide and dream o’ nights
On your mountains tipped with snow ;
There are dark recesses and dazzling heights
In the land where the lost men go.
There are shining plain-lands and tangled shades,
And echoing caverns cool,
And stretching forests and sun-lit glades,
And many a lilied pool ;
There are windy ridges and tropic calms,
And the long tides ebb and flow
Over coral reefs, by island palms,
To the land where the lost men go.
But to us who follow the brave lost men
This land seems faint and far ;
The sun sets o’er it each eve again,
And above it throbs one star.
Shall we reach them ever, those men who led—
We who follow and follow so ?
It is always a long day’s march ahead
To the land where the lost men go.
Q.

SATURDAY, MARCH 6, 1897.
The Federal Railway Problem.
The story told by the Prudent Federationists
-—otherwise the Mangy Dog party—about the
inevitable loss of traffic on the New South
Wales railways if the lines are put under
Federal management, and the awful results
that will ensue thereupon, receives a bad
shock from some recent articles in Sydney
Telegraph. The railway problem has now de-
finitely asserted itself as the biggest stumbling-
block in the way of Federation. The princi-
pal reasons why the lines should be put under
Federal management and be bossed by one
central authority are these three. In the
first place, it will result in the establishment
of a uniform gauge, and thereby reduce ex-
penses, facilitate commerce by saving much
useless loading and unloading, and economise
rolling stock, Secondly, it will cause new
railways to be constructed so as to lead to the
nearest port instead of to the capital of the
province, and do away with the insane sys-
tem whereby the producer is denied access
to the port 200 miles distant in the next pro-
vince, and compelled to send his goods to the
one 450 miles distant in his own province.
And, thirdly, it will do away with the ruinous
war of rates on the borders, and thus effect
another much-needed economy. And against
this the Prudent Federation element in
three provinces tells a disastrous legend of
how/Federal railway management will take
trade away from every place and reduce
every place to utter ruin. In New South
Wales it is alleged that the Riverina
trade will go to Melbourne and the
Western trade to Adelaide, and Sydney will
become a desert. In Victoria it is ajleged
that the Western trade will go to Adelaide,
ail'd that the trade of New South Wales
beyond the Darling, much of which is now
dragged by great labor and ingenuity to Mel-
bourne, will revert to Sydney. In South
Australia it is pointed out that that province
has the Barrier trade already, but a Federal
management will probably complete the
Cobar line to Broken Hill as part ©f a new
railway policy, and then this business will
go overland to the east coast, where coal for
smelting purposes is plentiful, and South
Australia will be lost and ruined and de-
serted. The principal trouble, however,
lies between New South Wales and Victoria ,
and the third province may, for the moment,
be left out of consideration.
* * * *
Under the present conditions there is a
constant struggle on the part of each railway
management to drag trade to its own big
port at almost any cost. The competition
began in a small way, and grew by degrees
till it became a mania. At first the exports
of New South Wales south of Junee, or a
point somewhere thereabouts, were mostly
shipped via Melbourne because it was the
nearest port. Then specially low rates were
offered to induce the trade to go via Sydney,
and Victoria offered still lower rates to bring
it back to Melbourne. After that larger
and larger reductions wer§ offered on both
sides, till each party was willing to carry the
goods of the people of this Debateable Land
at a dead loss, out of pure cussedness, and
solely to prevent the hated individual
across the border from getting the job.
The Victorian railways, as the Telegraph
points out, will carry goods of a certain class
from Melbourne to Wodonga in Victorian
territory, a distance of 188 miles, for £.5
10s. per ton. It will carry the same goods
from Melbourne to Wagga, in New South
Wales, a distance of 268 miles, for £3 3s. 4d.
per ton. This rate is a dead loss, therefore
the tremendous charge of £5 10s. has to be
levied on the miserable Victorian to help
make up the deficiency. If the Victorian
Department abandoned the effort to drag
New South Wales trade to Melbourne at any
cost, and ceased carrying the goods of outsiders
almost gratis for the sake of pure aggrava-
tion, it could convey the goods of its own
people at a lower rate and relieve them of a
serious burden. New South Wales pursues
the same frantic policy. A ton of wheat
goes by rail from Albury to Melbourne for
15s. Bd., so the New South Wales Depart-
ment carries it to Sydney —fully twice the
distance—for 13s. 4d., and on a rough esti-
mate it loses 10s. on the deal. Machinery
is carried from Melbourne to Albury (190
miles) at £5 12s. per ton, while it is carried
from Sydney to Albury (387 miles) for £5
ss. 3d. Some goods are carried from Syd-
ney over that same 387-mile journey, at as
little as one-half the rate charged for the 190
mile journey from Melbourne. To make up
for this loss enormously heavy rates are
charged of necessity to the New South Wales
inhabitant in localities where there is no
outside competition. A great deal of the
prosperity of the Riverina is due to the fact
that each province carries its goods for next
to nothing in order to get ahead of the other
fellow, and taxes other people to make up
the loss. Riverina is a blessed place that is
subsidised at the expense of both New South
Wales and Victoria, and a good part of the
railway deficit in both provinces is due to
the scramble for a trade which is of no
earthly value to either at present rates.
* * * •
In the Western district of New South
Wales the struggle is of an even more
desperate character. The New South Wales
lines from Bourke and Cobar carry goods at
unprofitable rates to drag the trade to
Sydney. The Victorian Department carries
them at still more unprofitable rates to drag
commerce Melboi r )e-wards. The local in-
habitant, however, gains very little, whereas
both Victoria and New South Wales lose
heavily. The shortest and cheapest outlet is
by river towards South Australia, but the
lower reaches of the river are systematically
left unsnagged and more or less unnavigable
to prevent trade going out of the province,
and there are no railways leading westward.
Therefore, some of the annual crop is
laboriously and expensively conveyed to
Bourke, and carried thence to Sydney at
a specially cheap rate. And another part
is taken to Swan Hill and carried thence
to Melbourne at about one-third the re-
gular charge, the other two-thirds being
knocked off to make it worth the
owner’s while to get his wool to the Vic-
torian border, therefore that province may
be said to carry it gratis half the journey
in order that it may enjoy the privilege of
carrying it at a reduced rate for the other
half. The Swan Hill line doesn’t pay in-
terest on cost, but by charging heavy rates to
the luckless Victorian producer it is made to
cover working expenses and a portion of the
interest. It would probably pay a good deal
better without the trade from western New
South Wales, and it is almost certain that
one at least of the New South Wales lines—
the ruinous Hay extension —would pay
better without it also. If, under a Federal
management which took no account of pro-
vincial capitals, the far western pastoralists
were furnished with the most direct possible
communication with the nearest South Aus-
tralian port, they could pay freights which
would cover the cost of transit and yet be
cheaper than the desperately-reduced rates
now charged to Sydney and Melbourne ; the
New South Wales and Victorian lines would
only lose a traffic which costs more than it
yields;,by losing it they would be able to
reduce freights to the population wdthin their
legitimate sphere and also reduce the annual
deficit; and there would be a gain to every-
body concerned.
A good part of this debateable western
region, however, is nearer to Sydney than to
either Melbourne or any South Australian
port. And as a Federal management would.
necessarily put an end to differential rates,
and establish a uniform tariff according to
distance, it would emphatically shut down on
the absurd Victorian competition over a
large part of this area. A good deal of trade
which now goes to Melbourne at ruinous
rates would go instead to Sydney at payable
ones. Also, a good deal of trade which now
goes to Sydney, at rates which don’t cover
expenses, would still go there, only at rates
which would cover expenses. Until a
railway is built running northward
from Swan Hill, which isn’t likely to
happen for many years, Victorian competition
would be abolished over something like half
of the West, and the trade of almost all that
district would coine Sydney-wards. When
such a line is built, Victorian competition
would still be shut out from about one-third
of the country now disputed by the two pro-
vinces. Sydney would gain a great deal in
the immediate present, and Victoria would
be all the richer for what it would lose. In
the South, Sydney would lose some trade,
and as the trade is carried at a dead loss its
railway system would be much better off for
losing it. Under provincial management the
business of the two debateable lands in the
South and West profits absolutely nobody
save the people whose goods are carried
largely at the expense of their neighbors.
And it doesn’t always benefit even them,
as is shown by the situation out West,
where in the struggle to drag certain
unprofitable bales of wool to two ports,
neither of which is the natural outlet of the
country, egress to a third one, much nearer
than either, is practically barred. When the
producers around Sydney and Melbourne
realise that they are paying exorbitant
freights to cover the loss incurred in the
scramble for the border trade—that they, as
a matter of fact, are carrying at their own
expense the wool and w'heat and machinery
of other people whom they don’t know, and
never saw, and take no manner of interest in
—then the demand for Federal railway
ownership, or at least a Federal railway
tariff, will become peremptory. Nothing
else, apparently, can end the trouble. Where
two rival managements meet they can no
more refrain from reaching out for each
other’s traffic than the fighting Indian of the
old picturesque days could from reaching out
for the scalp of the other Indian across the
border. And now each management has got
the scalp of the other and has lost its own,
and the situation is mutually unsatisfactory.
The Great Life Insurance Mystery.
It is vaguely rumored that the Victorian
Government intends next session, or at some
other early date, to pass an enactment for the
regulation of life insurance societies. If it
carries out this purpose, and if its law fills the
requirements of the case, it will do a great and
much-needed work for which it will get
very little thanks and inevitably much exe-
cration. The managers, directors and other
officials of those existing insurance offices which
can’t stand too close inspection, and whose
liberty must needs be seriously interfered with,
will supply the execration ; the absence of any
adequate thankfulness will come from the
public which doesn’t understand the danger it
is in, and will be but little grateful to the
Government which rescues it therefrom. In
some respects the people, as regards insurance
matters, are in much the same position as they
•were as regards banking matters seven years
ago. Had the Turner-Isaacs Government
been in power in 1890, and passed its Com-
panies Act then, and if it had thereby averted,
so far as its jurisdiction went, the crash of ’93,
its legislation would have been merely regarded
as a piece of officious meddling. It could never
have proved that a crash would have come but
for its interference, therefore it couldn’t have
proved that its measure was really required.
The situation with regard to life insurance
business is something similar just now. No
serious crash has come, therefore no one can
prove to the multitude by ocular demonstration
that legislation is needed. When it is proved
it will be rather late for interference, but not
too late for a great sensational advt. There-
fore the Government which acts in time is likely
to get little support, whereas the one which
acts too late, with events as evidence on its
side, will probably get a great deal.
* * * *
Life assurance, to the average individual, is
as profound a mystery as the nature of things
at the back of Jupiter. Even with access to
all the affairs of an insurance office—with all its
records at hand and with unlimited time to
inspect them—only an expert in this particular
art can tell, in 99 per cent, of cases, whether an
office is solvent or not. Such experts are very
few. Of that few one or two are paid to inves-
tigate the affairs of each office and report on
the condition to which the directors have
brought it. The directors, as a rule, are men
of great influence in business circles—men of
the class on whom the expert depends for his
livelihood, and it is by no means his interest to
quarrel with them. There is no regular over-
haul by men who do not depend on the direc-
tors and people of their class for a livelihood—
in other words, there is no State audit. If the
private auditor does report that things are
wrong —w'hich he never does—he has no power
to put them right; he can only appeal through
the press—if it will publish his appeal—to the
great, scattered, helpless, unwieldy mass of
shareholders to rise up against the directors
and put things right. Also, he can only appeal
to them by explaining highly technical matters
in language which 999 out of every 1000 don’t
understand. Worst of all, except in some ex-
ceedingly glaring case, he can’t say himself that
there is anything certainly wrong. There is no
recognised definition of right and wrong in the
matter, and the law prescribes no standard of
solvency. Every office makes its own standard
of solvency, within certain limits, and it
naturally employs one by which it is solvent.
Therefore it is solvent.
There are still people who regard a bank as a
place where you insert your money, to be
stored in a safe until you want to draw it out
again, and who have a vague idea that a banker,
in some incomprehensible way, earns interest,
rent, working expenses, dividends, &c., simply
by keeping the money in a safe. Also, there
are people who vaguely imagine that a solvent
life office is one which has, say, a million
pounds of insurance in force, and a million
pounds to pay them with. The full incom-
prehensibleness of the business is a mystery to
the great majority, and even the fact that a
balance-sheet, however accurate, is, beyond a
certain point, no guide at all as to the solidity
or otherwise of the office is known to very few.
The question of solvency mainly depends on
the following considerations. The funds on
hand at a given date are so much. The
insurances in force, all of which, it must
be assumed, will ultimately have to be paid
in full, are so much. The annual premium
income thereon is so much. The ages
of the persons insured are various. The
average number of years each will live is a
matter to be decided by certain estimates
based on long experience. Therefore, those
whose premiums are payable for life will live
and pay premiums for a certain number of
years on an average. Those whose policies
are payable on the endowment plan will
also pay premiums for a certain number of
years which have to be estimated in
the same fashion. These premiums will ac-
cumulate, less a certain estimated outlay for
working expenses for the average number of
years already alluded to. The accumulation
will yield compound interest at a certain rate,
which is fixed by striking an average among
certain known and assumed circumstances. A
certain margin must be allowed for losses
through bad investments. So much in life
insurance to be paid a certain estimated number
of years hence, under all these conditions, is
equal, as ascertained by certain profound calcu-
lations, to a certain liability payable now. If
the assets in hand equal that present liability,
then the office is solvent, provided the assets-
are real, and sufficient allowance has been made
for bad investments. Otherwise, it isn’t.
These, with several dozen other complications
based on annuities, tontine policies, trust and
debenture policies, children’s endowments,
mortuary dividend policies, &c., thrown in, are
the basis of the question of solvency in life
insurance business.
* * * *
Every life office that The Bulletin ever
heard of is—and always was—solvent, by its
own showing. Only a stupendous fool of an
qffice would not be solvent, considering the
latitude allowed in Australia. The estimated
duration of life varies considerably, according
to different authorities, and, by taking the
most favorable table, the estimated amount to
be received in premiums before the bill has to
be paid can be considerably expanded. It can
be assumed that the accumulated funds will
bear interest at almost any rate. One English
office estimates future interest at 2 per cent,
only. Australian offices estimate and 4 per
cent., as a rule. One Australian office is re-
puted to have once filled an awkward gap
by estimating future interest at 6 per
cent., and the difference bet.ween these
extremes is so wide that one way an in-
surance office might be shown to be insolvent
with £1,000,000 in hand, while the other it
might show itself to be perfectly solvent with
the same liabilities and only £700,000 or so to
meet them. The amount to be allowed for
working expenses may be put at a much lower
figure than the real expense rate, if it is neces-
sary to make ends profess to meet somehow.
Also, the present assets may be swelled by not
writing off bad investments, or £50,000 worth
of bricks and mortar may be written up to
£IOO,OOO on the ground that “ values have gone
up.” If £50,000 is wrongfully added on to
property-values on the ground that an alleged
rise has taken place, and £50,000 more that has
been lost by bad investments is not written off,
the apparent assets are swelled by £IOO,OOO of
non-existent money. And when it is estimated
that the funds will yield 4 per cent, interest,
this means taking credit in the future for an
income of £4OOO a year that will never be real-
ised. And, through taking for granted this
£4OOO a year income for the future, a bankrupt
office may show a beautiful state of solvency.
By assuming assets that don’t really exist, and
assuming a higher interest rate and a lower ex-
pense rate and a longer duration of life than
are at all reasonable, any insurance institution
can persuade the public that it is sound, and
do a big business, and declaresensational bonuses
out of profits which seem to be there but are
not, and have a good time generally.
* * * *
The public, being utterly unable to fathom
the mystery of the business, takes an insur-
ance office mostly on its own valuation.
One Australian office which is now getting
along rapidly charges the lowest pre-
miums in Australia, uses a larger propor-
tion of them in expenses than any other
office, and pays about the biggest bonuses
going ; and the public judges it solely by its
low charged and its big bonuses, and knows
nothing about the interior working of things.
Another which is now booming handsomely got
round an awkward corner some years ago by
enormously writing up the nominal value of its
city property, and, so far as all the visible
information goes, thereby just saved itself from
showing a very heavy deficit. A third, which
kept itself absolutely solvent and honest by
heavily writing down the value of its assets to
cover depreciation arising through the bursting
of the boom, and sacrificed most of its bonus
to do so, fell into such disfavor through its
obtrusive soundness and honesty that its new
business diminished very badly. The present
system attracts business to the office which is
run on a reckless policy that points towards
ultimate disaster, and to a great extent scares
it away from the one which is conducted
on absolutely sound and cautious principles.
What is required is that the State shall pre-
scribe the rate of interest to be assumed (which
should not exceed 3£ per cent.) and the method
of valuation, that a State officer shall periodi-
cally audit the accounts to see that its rules
are strictly adhered to, and that he shall als?


periodically examine and value the assets, and
report on the surplus or alleged surplus. In
short, what is wanted is a system by which all
offices shall be solvent or insolvent according
to the same fixed standard. At present every
office is solvent according to its own standard,
which is quite a different matter.

Henry Button.
Several people—quite a lot of people, in fact—
have sent round since the above ticket was
first announced to point out its many defects.
It has been objected to because it didn’t contain
enough Catholics, and enough Protestants, and
enough Protectionists, and enough Freetraders,
and enough Labor men ; also, because it in-
cludes three Ministers in some provinces and
only two in another ; because Sargood is in it ;
because Purves, Lyne, See, Moran, Cope-
land, Crick, Brunker, Heydon, -MTntyre,
Gillies, Solomon, Copley, and three dozen
others are not in it, and because some weak
little men are included and many big strong
ones left out. To all these objections The Bul-
letin replies in a very few words : It has
certain distinct convictions as to what a Federa-
tion should be, as set forth in its “Federal
Constitution” published in the issue of January
23 —its main ideas being that there should be
two Houses both elected on the basis of one
adult one vote, the Referendum, a system
whereby deadlocks can be averted, elective
ministries, that the Commonwealth should take
over the public debts, railways, posts, tele-
graphs, defences, military forces, &c., bodily as
they stand, that provincial borrowing shall be
absolutely prohibited, and many other things. Its
forty candidates are the men who, so far as it
could gather from their speeches and writings,
are most likely to bring about its ideal Com-
monwealth. The theory that the Convention
should consist of the 40 biggest men in Aus-
tralian politics, regardless of their opinions, is
absurd. Ho one wants an army of 40 generals
and no privates. If the Convention contains
ten leaders who can draw up a really sensible
Constitution and 30 other men who will back it
with a reliable vote, it is much better than if it
contained 40 big men, who held w'idely diverse
opinions, and who were all too big to follow
anybody’s lead. Moreover, the big men are
not necessarily the men of brains. They are
mostly the old Parliamentary hands, the men
with money and influence, wdio know how to work
the road and bridge vote, the sectarian vote, the
Orange, or Masonic, or National Ass vote, who
advertise themselves deftly at banquets and
functions, the great borrowers and Dead-house
builders, and the men who whoop for the
Queen and get up loyal displays, and buy news-
paper support with timely concessions. The
Bulletin considers that, in individual intelli-
gence, its nominees compare favorably with
most of the large, disappointing political me-
gatheriums who are left out. Consider a few of
them : Speaker Abbott, who has been going to
do something great for 15 years, and has done
nothing but stand over 6ft. high ; Lyne, Cope-
land and See, the three heavy-going failures
of N.S.VV. Protectionist party; the unspeak-
able boomer, Gillies ; the commonplace
Downer, who never did anything but exist in
a large Tory orthodox way ; and the rest.
Anyhow, this paper, in its own self-satisfied
fashion, is content with its selections, and is
prepared to defend them against all-comers.
They are not an ideal 40, but Federal candi-
dates are not made in heaven, and it is neces-
sary to be content with the most likely speci-
mens to be found on this earth.
To the Ghost of the Dead Australian.
In four of the Australias the Federal Convention
election takes place this week, and, by present
signs, a very moderate vote may be expected.
The question at issue is so largo, and toe area
of the five provinces-interested is so vast, and
the individual voter looks such a very small
speck in the centre thereof, that there is a
more than usually strong tendency on every-
body’s part to remark that one vote doesn’t
matter either way, and that one fellow needn’t
trouble himself. And the fact which The Bul-
letin desires to lay down just here with a thud
like that of a falling Alp is that every individual
vote does matter. Also, that everything else
matters more or less. Some profound thinker
and dreamer, whose name the present writer has
somehow lost hold of, remarked truly that one
man cannot shift one grain of sand without
altering the world’s centre of gravity, and
through it the centre of gravity of the whole
universe, and by so doing he brings new in-
fluences to bear on the unborn generations, and
turns them into new paths, and in all the
billions of ages yet to come the Cosmos will
never be quite the same as it would have been
if that grain of sand hadn’t been shifted.
Everything is related in some way to every other
thing, and nothing that is would be exactly
wdiat it is if anything that was had been just a
shade different from what it really amounted to.
EvenFRANKI arnell wouldn’t havemade exact-
ly the exhibition of himself that he recently did in
England if Mithridates had downed Pompey
instead of Pompey getting on top. The Zealots
had to go under at the siege of Jerusalem over
1800 years ago, and Cleopatra had to be de-
feated at Actium, and all the battles and wars
and plagues and famines and murders and other
events that ever took place from the times
long before early Sodom down to the other day,
when 1 urner decided to go to England and
grovel—all these things had to happen to cause
John Norton to be fined £5O in Sydney last
week for contempt of court. If only one of
them hadn’t happened, he might have been
fined £75, or riot been fined at all, or he might
have never existed, or he might have been a
Czar or a cannibal instead of a Sydney jour-
nalist. And the point which all this leads up
to is that if any individual voter doesn’t vote
at the Federal election the world will never be
quite the same again as it would be if he did.
The man who doesn’t vote is missing a chance
to get his little lever under the universe and
start it in a new' direction. He is throwing
away an opportunity to make history ; his
suffrage, or the absence of it, will start some
impalpable change, which will widen and spread
till it capsizes empires or keeps them from
capsizing, till it causes or averts wars and con-
vulsions and the making of new states and the
upheaval of old ones, and shifts futurity in
general from its basis. The man who gets to
the poll too late can stop outside and look at
the closed door, and consider within himself
that his descendants and other people’s de-
scendants down to the remotest generation will
never quite get aw r ay from the effects of that one
insignificant episode. Even if one obscure per-
son named Smith is too tired to take any
interest in the matter, he will thereby make
the beginning of a small stream of circumstance
which will never stop flowing, and which will
gradually widen out until it turns the destiny
of Australia and the world into new channels,
and bursts up things that might have been, and
makes all manner of new things which would
never otherwise have occurred. And when the
reincarnated ghost of Smith returns to this
earth thousands of years hence to live over
again, as it possibly may, it will be quite a
different world from w r hat it would have been
if he had acted differently on this occasion—
possibly a worse one, possibly a better one, but
a different one anyhow. No one can tell in
advance whether the change which will be
effected by one individual citizen adding his
ballot to the heap will be for better or worse,
but it will be a change anyhow ; it will help to
prevent the universe getting mouldy, and the
solar system from stagnating, and" the fixed
stars from growing fossilised, and it will prod
the Cosmos into some new sphere of activity.
Therefore, it is incumbent on every Australian
citizen to vote, and in 60,000 years, or some
other period, his astral being, if it is around,
may be able to trace the consequences and know
that it at least tried to keep things moving
while it was in the flesh, and didn’t lean on a
gate quite all its life with an unseeing eye fixed
on a passing dog.

Going West.
Last year, according to the estimates of the
local statistician, the population of Victoria
decreased by nearly 7000. The population of
N.S. W. for the same year showed an estimated
increase of 19,000, which is much below the
usual average. There is a slight discrepancy
in the modes of computation, it being the custom
in Victoria to add 10 per cent, for possible
departures not recorded, but the figures are
near enough for all practical purposes. Accord-
ing to the actual records, the cause of the
trouble was that 14,000 more people left Vic-
toria and 800 more left N.S. Wales than arrived
in these provinces, and the bulk of them were
bound westward to the alleged Land of Gold
about Kalgoorlie and Coolgardie. This persis-
tent “ trek” towards the sunset is only another
evidence of how distant fields keep on looking
green because of their distance, long after all
reasonable excuse for their greenness has de-
parted. The gold-yield of Westralia is growing
smaller in proportion to population instead of
larger, consequently the prospects of the indi-
vidual miner are becoming steadily less. The
gold now. obtained comes mostly from half-a-
dozen big mines owned by companies ; the pro-
portion which remains for the individual
fossicker is miserably small. A year or
two ago he could make money - not by
digging, but by selling any reasonably pro-
mising show to the credulous J. Bull, but
now J. Bull is sick and full-up and disgusted,
and a mine is almost as difficult to float as a
ton of loose bricks on the sea. The prospects
of employment on the companies’ mines grow
worse rather than better, for many of them are
abandoned,and many more are underexemption,
and many that were going to start never got
started at all ; also, the shadow of Italian
bond-labor is coming o’er the land. The
demand for labor on public works has di-
minished greatly ; most of the large ones
recently on hand are completed, and there
are no prospects of more being undertaken
on a large scale till the new loan floats,
and it seems to be indefinitely postponed. The
Coolgardie Miner thus describes the situa-
tion :
We can point to nothing but distress and disaster should
the population continue to increase at the present rapid
rate. The prevailing slump has led to the suspension of
work, and in many instances the total abandonment of
promising prospecting shows held by local owners, while
the tremendous number of exemptions which have been
granted all over the country have had the effect of throw-
ingl some thousands of men upon the labor market. The
field, therefore, now offers no inducement to those about
to leave their homes for' this colony, unless idleness and
starvation can be accounted such. Actual distress is
already becoming prevalent in the outer centres, and the
day of unemployed demonstrations is not far distant.
Here in Coolgardie are hundreds of men seeking work and
finding none, and what to do with these vast idle throngs
will soon be a problem for the authorities, who in their
solicitude for capital have brought about such evils and
are still engaged in aggravating them day by day.
. . . But be this as it may, the fact remains that
we are face to face with a serious crisis, that the question
of how to feed an idle populace already looms large and
dark upon an horizon rendered closer to our senses by the
signs of approaching catastrophe, and that the Ministry
in its folly seems determined to make worse and worse,
day after day, the evil straits in which we are placed by
their lack of wisdom. In these circumstances we are per-
forming a simple duty in endeavoring to palliate the tribu-
lation we cannot eradicate by warning others against com-
ing here to share the gloomy fate which awaits our own
working men.
Almost all the private communications which
come from the West tell the same tale, but
nothing seems to check the exodus, and it will
probably continue till it is swamped by the
backward movement, which must set in shortly
from the busted Land of Promise. And mean-
while, before the rush to the dead-broke El
Dorado of the West has begun to decline, a
new one has started Northward to fever-
haunted, trackless New Guinea, where the
gold found up to date hasn’t paid expenses,
and which is almost more unpromising even, so
far as is known at present, than Westralia.
Every orator and every Dan O’Connor in the
three Eastern provinces has talked for years
about their “boundless resources” and “illimit-
able natural wealth,” but somehow they seem
unable to support in comfort an average popu-
lation of one male adult to 640 acres, and the
desire to get anywhere and anyhow out of
“ this great country ” is a wonderfully unani-
mous sentiment.
The Little Nigger.
There is plenty of law, but no great superfluity
of justice for the little nigger in Westralia.
Recently in Roebourne district, in the Far
North, where the chances of the small nigger
are even worse than they are in some other
parts of the province, a diminutive aboriginal
aged 14, and bearing the name of Friday, was
charged with being “ unlawfully on the pre-
mises ” during the night. The only person
who saw the individual who was said to have
been on the premises was a very young girl,
and her evidence was simply to the effect that
she couldn’t recognise Friday, and didn’t know
whether he was the person or not. A tracker
swore that he identified Friday’s footprints
about the premises, but being questioned he
said that all the tracks went “ straight fellow,”
and as Friday had a badly-distorted foot he
didn’t fit the description. Therefore the evi-
dence really amounted to this : that some
tracks had been discovered, not at all like
those of the prisoner, and that a girl didn’t
know whether he was the offender or not.
However, the scared Friday was inserted in the
dock, with no lawyer to defend him and no
witness, and was tried in a strange language,
and, in his ignorance and anxiety to propitiate
the court, he answered “ Yes ” to every ques-
tion put to him. All through the case he said
nothing but “Yes.” When asked if he was
guilty he said “ Yes ; ” if he had been asked
if he was innocent he would have said “ Yes ”
also. When he had said “Yes” a sufficient
number of times he was convicted on his own
confession, and Magistrate Brockman sentenced
him to 12 months’ gaol and 12 lashes. All the
questions put to the miserable little nigger
were heaved at him in the technical lan-
guage of the court; he was asked if he
wanted to “ cross-examine ” the huge police-
man, and if he desired to summon witnesses,
and it was taken for granted that boy Friday
understood all these matters, merely because he
said “ Yes ” all the time. A local paper,
Northern Public Opinion, thereafter came
out with a severe article on boy Friday, the
bloodthirsty editor regretting that he didn’t
get a larger flogging, and pointing out the
necessity of putting down such desperadoes
with a strong hand. The only individual who
attempted to interfere on Friday’s behalf was
his employer, who desired to act as his counsel,
but was snuffed out with an intimation that he
couldn’t appear unless Friday called him as a
witness. Friday didn’t call him, probably
because he didn’t know how, and that de-
molished his last chance. For that matter,
however, boy Friday never has any chance,
last or otherwise, with the hog-like administra-
tors of W.A. justice.

Doomed!
When the supernatural explanation of things
was the only explanation, the priest—pre-
dominant and arrogant—was the great fact of
human society. But ever since the prostrate
mind of man began to struggle to its knees, and
in proportion as for phenomenon after pheno-
menon there has been discovered a simple and
inevitable natural causation, sacerdotalism has
been steadily forced backwards towards extinc-
tion. The process is slow, but the end is cer-
tain. When the natural explanation of things
in general shall have entirely superseded the
supernatural, the priest will have to go, because
there will then be no place for him in the order
of things. Humanity will have no use for an
expounder of gratuitous assumptions about
what cannot possibly be known. To this goal,
it is true, we have yet far to travel. The mind
of man aforesaid is far enough yet from a per-
fectly erect position ; but, amongst the proofs
that it is slowly attaining that desirable atti-
tude, it is to be noted that not a single
priestly voice has been raised to proclaim that
the existing bubonic plague has been “ sent ”as
a physical punishment for the moral offences of
the afflicted peoples. This is wisdom, of a
sort ; for, though the ultimate decease of sacer-
dotalism is but a matter of time, nothing kills
so quickly as ridicule, and, in the light of
modern physiological research, the ancient
“ visitation ” gag would be to every man who
does his own thinking simply a subject for con-
temptuous laughter.
M illiam Fuller was fined 16s. 3d. and costs
at a i Red fern (Sydney) police-court, one day last
week, for neglecting to pay the State-school
fees levied on his children. In default of pay-
ing fees and costs he was to be sold up, and in
the event of enough not being raised that way
YV illiam was to be sent to gaol. The prosecu-
tion was directed by Minister Garrard. It is
unnecessary to work in agony about Fuller’s
poverty, or the why or wdiereforeof his neglect
to cash upthreepennies as the law directs. The
prosecution is a valuable incident solely because
it rubs into the public intelligence the fact that
after years of agitation and irrefragable argu-
ment N.S. VV. still stands as a doltish anomaly
which with one breath makes primary educa-
tion compulsory and with the next makes pay-
ment for that education compulsory and
collectable under penalties of fine and
imprisonment. Those acquainted with the
political movements of the last ten years
can recall numerous occasions when strong
majorities in Parliament favored free education,
and when even the opposing Parkes faction
admitted that it had no logical ground to stand
upon, but on every test occasion occult in-
fluences were brought to bear, and the three-
penny aggravation still stuck to the statute
book. In its last session N.S.W. Parliament
was similarly minded to destroy the impost,
and destroyed it would have been had the
Labor members risen to the expectations formed
of them. In the hurried days at the close of
the session, when a Minister of the Crown was
threatened with a demand for bills for the
imposition of newspaper postage and for free
education, that political duke, fearing the
wrath of the press, replied that the parties
at the back of these bills had, by claim-
ing that their own official letters should go
franked, lost their moral influence on the
questions named. So always, by one strata-
gem or another, the public will is burked.

Kish the buke ” has just been abolished by
announcement from the bench in Leeds, Eng.,
police-court, and the present Scotch oath with
uplifted right hand substituted—“l swear by
Almighty God that I will speak the truth, the
whole truth, and nothing but the truth.” The
only real value of the oath is as an impressive
hypnotic dose ; in fact, the notion is to frighten
the average witness into getting as near the
truth as possible. The old Scotch oath was a
magnificent ceremony and tremendously im-
pressive. The sheriff would rise up in gor-
geous trappings of office, raise his right hand
heavenwards, and sonorously announce clause
by clause, the witness, with right hand raised,
following him—
I swear by Almighty God,
As I shall answer to God,
At the great Day of Judgment,
That I will tell the truth,
The whole truth.
And nothing but the truth.
Just imagine the effect of that so delivered by
George Joseph of Canaan Rignold, in trap-
pings like a Cardinal’s, on the average witness 1
It would make even a bronze lion wriggle.

“ One of the undoubted results of Federation,”
writes “ Lex,” “ will be the establishment of a
Federal Court of Appeal. This will be a new
charge on the Federal provinces, and the ques-
tion arises how it can be efficiently and economi-
cally arranged. At least three, but more likely
five, Judges will be needed, and, if special ap-
pointments are made, and the best available
talent secured, a salary of not less than .£3OOO
will have to be paid. But appeals are not
.numerous, and are not likely to much increase
in number for years, so the Appeal Judges
would be idle three-fourths of the year. Why
not appoint the Chief-Justices of the federating
provinces—six eventually—without increase of
salary? A Chief-Justice does not arrive there
without possessing extensive legal knowledge,
and his impartiality is seldom questioned. The
work could easily be overtaken by a ten-days’
sitting once a quarter, and the Judges could sit
in batches of three—thus giving to each an at-
tendance of, say, 26 days, allowing for journeys.
Besides the saving of from <£lo,ooo to £20,000
a year, the Chief-Justices themselves would
largely benefit by rubbing up against each
other.”
A passage in Justice Hood’s summing-up of
the gruesome Rosebrook murder-case went
without comment by the Melb. press, although
it was the most extraordinary feature of a
phenomenal trial. All that the public ever
knew of the Rosebrook horror, as a horror, was
communicated by detectives to newspaper
reporters, who promptly put it into print. In
this way the statement of Mrs. Snell, the self-
alleged accessory to her father’s crime, and all
the facts tending to substantiate that statement
came to be published. The Lyfield shown by
the police was a dull, methodical monster of
iniquity, and as such it was expected that he
would have to stand his trial. The Lyfield
shown in the criminal court was merely a
suspected murderer and possibly model
parent, against whom nothing was proved
except that he talked like a person suffer-
ing from delusions. Some medical witnesses
gave Mrs. Snell a certificate of insanity
which put her out of court and thereby
cancelled, in a judicial sense, all the “orful
revelations ” that had been laid to the prisoner’s
charge. The trial of Lyfield produced so
little real evidence against him that he must
have received the benefit of a jury’s honest
doubt had not the 12 men in the box formed
their conclusions on the case from the police
stories they had read, or heard of, and which
their common sense told them to believe. But
Justice Hood told them—“ I want to express
publicly my regret, not that the newspapers
publish these things, but that the officials of
the Crown Law and Police Departments permit
the detectives to go straight to the newspaper-
reporter directly they have discovered any-
thing good.” His Honor chose an appropriate
time for expressing this regret. In ordinary
cases police prattling may lead to injustice. In
this case it was, on paper, solely responsible
for a murderer being classed under his proper
heading and sent to gaol for an indefinite
period. Lyfield’s trial, per se, left him quite
open to an acquittal.

humorous, provide more entertainment than all
the numerous imitators put together. Other

Tlxe above burst of verse explains, in part
At least, how The Bulletin feels concerning
“ Matsa,” which is now running at Her Majesty’s,
Sydney. There is less attempt at a story in this
Egyptian spectacle than even the usual panto-
mime can boast of, which is probably a good thing.
Too much story only encumbers a pantomime,
and is as superfluous as a top-hat on a mummy.
As a series of pictures and dances and a mass of
whirl and color “ Matsa ” can hold its own against
everybody and everything. Also, it can race any
other spectacle on record in the matter of mechani-
cal effect, the Rising of, the Nile being an item
that could give Bilmartin ten yards start
any day and beat him badly in the first lap.
There are some clever performers in the cast,
notably Little Gulliver, who is a wonderfully
bright and original infant, or dwarf, or a mixture
of both ; also Lauri, Howard Vernon, and Misses
Florence Young, Juliet Wray, and May Pollard.
As for beauty and youth and legs and music, they
are present in abundance ; and the presence of
Knowles telling over again in a hjirry all the old
jokes he formerly told in a hurry at the Palace
also counts for something. But “Matsa” is essen-
tially spectacle, and it fills its mission as such in a
great, lavish, generous way that appeals to the
soul. If it doesn’t boom with a great boom, then
the spectacular business is played out.
4)6 4)6 * *
On Saturday the Westmacott management
At Sydney Royal judiciously 4 threw the “Mother’s
Sin ” into the place where the dead mothers go,
and put up “Shall We Forgive Her?” in its
stead. The Bulletin’s impression, after con-
sidering this production in all its five acts, is that
she might as well be forgiven, but that the person
who forgave her in the drama is beyond forgive-
ness. The new play is a combination of many old
episodes. The heroine has a past and a sin—the
same old sin ; the stage heroine never has any
other. She had been a I’anqueray in a hut in
South Africa, where she Tanked with a scowling
ruffian as her paramour, and lived on bread and
dust; there was no water, for the drought was
very bad. Then, being moved by a kind curate,
she gives up Tanking, and goes to England and
reforms, and beeomes the wife of a noble-hearted
man, and almost forgets that she ever Tanked out
on the Karroo. Naturally her sin is found out
after a time, and her husband throws her out.
He becomes blind, like Pygmalion, and is led
about by a sour female relative of aggravated
Christianity, who acts as his dog, and ought to
bark to keep up the illusion. For two acts his
ejected Magdalen keeps calling round in a state
of despair, and trying to explain how circum-
stances drove her to Tank in Africa. She never
gets through her explanation, however, for the
sour female relative, Joanna, always shoo’s her
off the premises before she is half-finished. The
Christian virulence of Joanna, and the way she
always hunts the repentant wife off the grass
just when she is getting to the interesting part
of her story, are deeply objectionable. Even the
curate is moved to profane language, and the good
old motherly woman of the play calls for tenders
for the murder of Joanna and the secret removal
of her body. Then things end suddenly, for the
husband gets back his eyesight, and forgives
his Magdalen publicly in the garden, while she
kneels on the damp grass in the presence of all
his relatives. The present writer regards the hus-
band as a pious and moral hog. Any decent in-
dividual would have forgiven his wife unobtru-
sively in the house, or round in the back yard, or,
if he must forgive her publicly in the front garden,
would have sent away the crowd first of all and
offered the lady a chair.
* * * *
With all its old conventionality the clum-
sily-named drama has a good deal of human
interest about it. The lady who went astray, and
who is ultimately forgiven in that large fashion in
the garden is nicely, if not very strongly, played
by Miss Fitzmaurice Gill, and Miss Conroy, as
Joanna, is an artistically sour Christian made up
mostly of vinegar and moral front teeth. Plimmer
is a fine, manly curate ; Cecil Ward is a moderate
—a very moderate —hero ; Sweeney drifts by for a
moment as a pleasant comedy ruffian, and Hans
Phillips is a depressing failure. Stanford is
another highly picturesque ruffian, and he slinks
so ably through the drama that his disappearance
without first butchering Joanna is deeply regret-
able. A very gorgeous patch of desert scenery by
Howden, with rocks and bones and sun-glare in
it, calls for three cheers and a bit of National
Anthem all to itself.
* * 416 416
August Juncker’s new opera, “ A Romance
of Corsica,” which is doing a trial sprint at Sydney
Opera House, leaves a distinctly favorable impres-
sion on The Bulletin’s intelligence. Whst it
really knows about the play isn’t much ; the writer
tried to take it in on Monday night when a deluge
was pouring on the roof of the hollow-sounding
old barn in King-street, and most of the opera was
drowned in the din ; also, there were no copies of
the libretto available, and under these conditions
no one is expected co know what a song is about.
But when the play did come to the surface to
breathe every now and then there was generally
something interesting on deck, and scraps of
bright and pleasant music rose here and there
above the row that the rain was making on the
roof, and where the words were audible bits of
clever rhyme asserted themselves at intervals.
The two very tall men in disguise also seemed to
know what they were doing; and the co. in
general, whenever the writer could disentangle it,
appeared to act and sing rather cleverly ; and the
prima donna, Miss Anna Petersen—if it was she
—panned out very well indeed. This scribe
intends to tackle Juncker’s opera again, on a dry
night, and find out in real earnest what it is ail
about.

fourth week at Sydney Criterion that the “ Book-
maker,” which is to succeed her, is still in the far,

Very much the same crowd as last week
is in possession of the Palace and the Tivoli. They
have possibly been shifted about a little, but it is
difficult to follow the wanderings of Rickards’ stars
from one street to another. Rickards, having
three theatres on hand, finds it necessary to go to
Europe in search of more talent, so a fresh consign-
ment of novelties will be due shortly.

Henry Bracy’s opera co. starts business at
Sydney Lyceum on Saturday with the “Beggar
Student.” The cast includes Mrs. Bracy, Miss
Nina Osborne, Bracy, Knight Aston, Rebottaro,
and others, and the list of operas to be produced
covers several good old pieces that have been buried
so long that they should now be almost as new as
the newest. If the co. only swears off “Mari-
tana,” which is a hackneyed evil that almost all
opera companies fall into sooner or later, it should
do well.

The Lumiere Cinematographe leaves Syd-
ney this week, but as MacMahon’s show and the
Edison Electric Parlor remain indefinitely, the
city has probably still as much cinematographe as
it can assimilate. The last-named, which hangs
put at 162 Pitt-street, is a comprehensive show,
including cinematographe, phonograph, kineto-
phone, kinetoscope, x-ray apparatus, and a whole
lot of other mysteries.

“A Woman’s Reason,” now at Melbourne
Bijou, received a certificate of merit from The
Bulletin a few weeks ago, when the unreasonable
heroine was going astray at Sydney Cri. It is a
sound, sermonic sort of comedy-drama in three
acts, and its principal moral purpose is to warn
the dashing militaire against eloping with married
ladies under doubtful circumstances. In this case
a tame captain waits seven years for his chance to
annex the female property of an estimable Jew
speculator, the lady being an unsettled, un-
mother ly person who objects to the practical vir-
tues of her sister-in-law, Leah. Her noble parents
sold her to Leah’s brother in the first act, and for
seven years afterwards they live upon that patient
Hebrew like a plague of locusts, devouring his
colossal income, and belittling his respectable char-
acter, and calling attention to the fact that
his grandmother sold oranges. All this while
the) lady doesn’t trouble to think whether she has
acquired connubial happiness or otherwise, but as
soon as the front-door bangs behind her hasty
elopement she discovers a wild yearning for her
husband, and loathes, positively loathes, the cap-
tain 19 times more than he deserves. Conse-
quently, the patient lothario gets left like his law-
ful predecessor, and the lady bemoans her sin in a
cottage until the husband (divorced) returns to
her arms. Then the narrative dies away into
silence, leaving a tearful audience to suppose that
the happy pair will get their decree nisi overruled
by the Privy Council.
* * • *
A greasy vicar, played by Carey, provides
some irreverently comic relief to this serious enter-
tainment. The shepherd ambles in and out of the
story, attended, as a rule, by a sporting daughter,
who makes a soulful book on the principal handi-
caps, and gives a tithe of her winnings to the
curate’s sustentation-fund every time she “ skins
the lamb.” In the last act, he visits the penitent
heroine on his own, and exhorts her not to con-
taminate any needy sheep by supplying them
with coats and blankets. Brough, as the noble
locust-father, is a companion picture to Rev. Carey.
Either man might have been the other. They are
both the same humbug, bred and educated
under different conditions, and each is a con-
siderable artistic success in his way. Tithe-
radge and Mrs. Brough uphold the unthankful
characters of the married couple, but Ferris, the
colorless captain, intones his lines as though he
held a commission under Gen’ral Booth. Miss
Seymour, in the sister Leah capacity, fires off one
or two speeches with honest enthusiasm, and the
other people make useful contributions towards
the support of a promising show. “ A Woman’s
Reason ” should draw the women, and satisfy the
men who have to pay for seats. The construction
is rather clumsy, also some of the effects are
theatrical rather than dramatic, but the authors
have worked a lot of human interest into their
tale.
*« * *
“The Prisoner of Zenda” will run till
March 12th at Melbourne Princess’s, and the tail
end of its career promises to wag brilliantly. A
play steeped in self-sacrificial sentiment and fervid
devotion to His Majesty the King needs a little
time to grow upon the average Australian man,
but his average girl is a shrill barracker for
“ loyalty ” and blighted affection. Although not
personally unselfish (Marcus Clarke took the Aus-
tralian girl’s measure, years ago) she likes to think
herself in sympathy with stage characters who are
that way disposed. Therefore the popularity
of Princess Flavia and Rudolph Rassendyll
increases; also the villain Michael is adored by
stealth, so to speak, on account of his bold, black
eyes with rings of paint round them. A dark pirati-
cal glance across the footlights is probably the next
best thing to a noble character. Michael’s clothes
are a!ways satisfactory, anyhow, whereas Rassen-
dyll, for no conceivable reason, makes his first ap-
pearance—as a British johnnie—in a truly shocking
costume. The recollection of his reddish-brown
pants and brownish-red vest (to match his hair)
lingers through the next three acts, tarnishing the
gilt of his military buttons, and raising the ques-
tion whether Flavia would have loved the heroic
impostor if she had ever seen him in the
original sac suit. One very probable reason
why Flavia didn’t take kindly to the real king
was His Majesty’s tunic, which was identical in
color with the other fellow’s waistcoat. The
tunic, by the way, tends to damage the story in its
“ loyalty ” aspect. King Rudolph excites no
admiration. A drunken, squeaky young ass at
the outset, he crops up again at the finish as the
demented occupant of the same hideous tunic, and
it is hard for any beastly Democrat to understand
why all the good people in the play should put
themseles so much about to restore such an un-
lovely article to the throne of his auburn ancestors.
He is responsible for a sad waste of National
Anthem.
** * *
Bland Holt’s massive show at the Royal
has lost none of its massiveness since last week,
neither does the glory of Miss Watson’s “ damn ”
diminish. Miss Watson’s hearty performance is
much admired, even apart from her famous impre-
cation, and the ladies altogether are voted dream-
like as regards their dresses. In fact, “ The
Derby Winner” might lean against its drapery
display if there were not a dozen other and larger
considerations to rely upon for support.
