A bit of ancient story, full of glare and gas and
glory, ’neath the shadow of the pyramids
beside the dreamy Nile,
Where the sun sets red and golden, gilding many
a palace olden in Memphis, where the shores
are haunted by the crocodile ;
There old mummies resurrected by spells quite un-
expected, stream through marble halls, adorned
with barbaric gold and gem,
As the centuries roll back on their endless, dusty
track, and once again the sun is new o’er the
old, old land of Khem,
While “ Matsa ” weaves a spell in a gaudy, garish
hell, and scatters incantations and mysteries
in Khem.
Very small and trim and neat are the myriad
little feet which dance in bygone Memphis,
by the Pharaohs’ golden throne ;
A 1 as ! the feet are dust, and the jewelled gear is
rust, and the dancers sleep in Sais or Pelusium
or On.
But the ages going back take with them on their
track, like an atom that is carried on a
cyclone’s outer hem,
A weird Australian man in a suit of grey and tan,
who sells shares to Pharaoh Rameses in the
early days of Khem,
And, though we aren’t told, Pharaoh probably was
sold, and had to file his schedule by the
dreamy Nile in Khem.
There are gnomes and ghosts in plenty, and houris
ten times twenty, and palaces and odalisques
and harems in galore ;
And the desert land extending, like a tale that’s
never ending, with pyramids and sphinxes and
gods is littered o’er ;
And the waving, sun-kissed curls of the bright
Egyptian girls, like flowers that dance and
quiver on the sacred lotus stem,
Make a foreground to the trees, and shed fragrance
on the breeze, and make Osiris young again in
the musty land of Khem.
And the mummies that were hid in the dusty
pyramid wake to live and love and dance again
with the merry maids of Khem.
At eve the lotus closes, and the lilies and the roses
and the palm that droops its heavy leaves
upon the scented air,
Are reflected in the river where the ripples pass
and quiver, and the moon looks down upon
them with a silent, pallid stare ;
And as he writes these lines the writer half in-
clines to leave off modern politics and dreary
things like them
And wander gently back on the long-forgotten
track of the centuries, and run a paper by the
Nile, in Khem ;
To bring out his little journal among the shades
eternal, and get an “ad.” from Moses in the
ancient days of Khem.

Variety biz booms in Westralia. All the chief shows are
conducted in the open air during summer, indoor per-
formances being given to paper and empty cane-bottomed
chairs. George Jones and G. R. Lawrence run the
show in Perth on a large, sandy plot of ground in the
main street, which is nightly witnessed by crowded
audiences. The Lumiere Cinemat. is now the draw- and
backed up by a fairly good co., is bringing in shekels fast!
The Banvards and the muscular Lucifer are now due at
“Ye Olde English Fayre,” by which name the show is
know'n.

Hayes, the well-known circus-proprietor, died ot
pleurisy at Fremantle hospital, W.A., on 11th Feb aired
54. Hayes, who made and lost several fortunes during
his life, died very poor, but was given decent burial in
Perth by the Fitzgeralds and other friends, Gentile and
Hebrew.
A curious theatrical case was lately heard in
Perth. A lady, when under engagement to’King Hedlev
to sing at Cremorne Gardens, became noticeably in an
interesting condition, which so shocked the frequenters
of the Gardens that K.H., “yielding to numerous
requests,” terminated the agreemeht. A claim was made
for £55 for wages and the price of a boat-fare back to the
East, and a verdict was given for plaintiff. The plea for
the defence—that the condition of the performer justified
breach of contract—was not listened to by the Bench
Two well-known journalists gave evidence as to the cus-
tom of allowing performers to appear in that condition
and Regina Nagel unblushingly admitted that it was
quite the thing in the profession, but expressed the
opinion that it was “positively disgraceful,”
Ada Crossley speaks of the “ iciness ” of London morn-
ing-concert audiences. She is much pleased with her
success in “ Elijah,” and gratified with the appreciation
of the critics, who have a playful way of jumping on
every oratorio debutante in the Oity of Cliques. Although
the “sacred chamber in her soul” has not yet been
opened, yet I hear (writes a correspondent to Tub Bulle-
tin) that one or two knocks—not of the “ runaway ” type
—have echoed sympathetically in that hitherto cold,
irresponsive breast. This may sound poetical, hut the
plain prose is that if Ada attains full womanhood she will
sing as she should sing, and as she doesn't now, and
net treble the sized fees at present offered her.
Mrs. Fenwick has made her appearance in the law-courts
in quest of her stage earnings—and an advt,., writes Tim
Bulletin's London correspondent. When Sylvia Grey,
the Gaiety girl, married Mr. Fenwick, of Sydney, and his
gilded ornaments, the two-made-one occupied a fine es-
tablishment at Melton Mowbray, the pride-swollen, swell
centre of Hinglish ’unting. It is easier for a double-
humped, water-inflated camel to pass through the eye of a
just-born toy-terrier pup than it is for an actress, and a
g.g. at that, to enter the olique-y kingdom of the 'orty
'enters. No stone wall with a spiney cactus topping
could prove a more un-get-over-able obstacle than the
sleety, glacial opposition offered to the graceful gyrator’s
attempts to enter into that inner heaven of the nastiest
Nicest People, and the Fenwicks failed. Sylvia salved her
wounded pride in the warmth of the hazy footlights, and
her injured pocket with a favorable verdict against a non-
parting manager.
X. : Oscar Wilde’s name on Melb. Bijou streamers is
occasionally embarrassing. 'Tother day, the innocent
maiden of the theatre-party asked “hadn’t the author
done something dreadful? And, please, what was it?”
Every man present blushed ; the other girls laughed im-
moderately'.
Lemmone, The Bulletin hears, has determined to go
into the musical business in Australia as agent for various
instruments and Daniel Mayer's patent Resonator.
Mayer is the Jewish musical agent to whom Melba,
Crossley and others entrust their business.
Director Harrison, of the Patti concerts, has arranged
for a series of 20 concerts to be given in Liverpool, Man-
chester, Edinburgh, Glasgow, &c., during Oct. and Nov.
with Melba as soprano, Ada Crossley as contralto, and
Ben. Davies as tenor.
The portly Marie Elster, as prima donna of the Neilson
Opera Co. (writes a Bulletin correspondent), gives a re-
presentation of “ Santuzza,” which, “both dramatically
and musically, leaves nothing to be desired.” This week
she made her first appearance in London, and Australians
who heard her were simply charmed with her work. She
has lately had several good offers for America, and in the
part named can easily wipe out Del Torre.
“A Woman of No Importance,” says somebody, was
obviously suggested by' the ymunger Dumas’ “Le Fill
Naturel.” The little bit of plot might have been sug-
gested by “ Sweet Lavender,” or any other play in which
a man lights unexpectedly upon the illegitimate child of
a long-ago intrigue. Anyhow, the plot is but a very
minor consideration in Oscar Wilde’s play.
Julius Knight’s performance in “ Prisoner of Zenda ” is
said to be a faithful copy of the original, George Alexan-
der. In the matter of stage presence Knight is at some
disadvantage, however, and presence is about 99 percent,
of the battle for a hero who is mimicking a king. Herbert
Flemming, ten years ago, would have played Rassendyll
admirably, with a manner vigorous, yet debonnair, and
“the face of a god”—as a certain actress used to say.
A Bulletin correspondent enquires: “ What is the
trade secret of the scarcity of children in theatrical
domestic circles?” Well, the scarcity that he takes
for granted is merely an old public delusion which
actresses are careful not to explode. They don’t, as a
rule, wheel prams in public as proudly as mothers in
general, or walk about with children in arms, but in the
matter of maternity “professional” ladies get there all
the same, merely drawing the line (as a rule) at large
families. Mrs Kendal, Eilen Terry, and Nellie Farren
have two or three children apiece—the public only heard
of them when they grew up. Poor little Violet Varley
died practically in childbirth, within a year or so of mar-
riage. And doesn’t the average sketch of an actress’s
career begin with the statement that she comes of mum-
mer parents ?
Editor Bulletin, — Nicholas Charles Bochsa’s remains
lie in Newtown (Sydney) cemetery under an artistic stone
raised to his memory by his “grateful pupil, Anna
Bishop.” Lavenu found a grave in the same cemetery,
while Cutolo, I believe, is in St. Jude’s, Randwick, and
Packer in Rookwood.—Yours, F.

Tis said that because of this the performers feel ill at
ease. This was especiallv noticeable with even such an
experienced veteran as Chirgwin.

The Wild-Cat Column .
The City Mutual Life Assurance Society in
reply : “ Dear Bulletin, —That annuity-cum-fully-paid-
policy does seem anomalous. I will have it seen to.
Thanks for drawing attention to it. Not much harm has
resulted, however, as we have only issued in 18 years one
fully-paid policy and three annuities—one of which fell in
last January, three months after purchase. But we
haven’t a penny in trust at 5 per cent., and, what is more,
we are not taking any. And we have not issued even one
tontine policy with an estimate. Following the example
of one big office, we did get a few estimates printed, but
they were withdrawn a week after they were printed.
And that was some time ago. And you are right in say-
ing we are taking a good many risks, but as the doctor
first passes them we are willing to take more.—Geo.
Crowley. 1/3,97.
* * * *
N.S.W. revenue for February was ,£18,370
more than for Feb., ’96. Revenue for the first eight
months of the financial year (£5,880,865) is £157,322 be-
hind that for the corresponding period of last year, but
as it was £228,000 behind at the end of December the
last two months have pulled things up materially. Ex-
penditure, so far, is £6,323,478, so the deficit is already
£442,000, and there is a lot of leeway to make up if the
next surplus is to pan out all right.
* * * *
The N.S.W. local 3 per cent, loan is getting on
steadily. The amount sold in February was £31,450.
Since the beginning of the year £301,000 has been sold,
and the total amount of the loan issued since it first
opened, about four months ago, is over half-a-million.
It cost 2s. 6d. per cent, less interest than any N.S.W. loan
ever floated in London, even saying nothing about the
saving in management, remittance of interest, &c., which
amounts to a good deal more. The only drawback is that
a loan which is always going off silently at the Treasury
attracts no attention, while the one that goes off with a
howl under the tender-system helps to draw public atten-
tion to the way the debt keeps mounting up. But, as the
public never does anything when its attention is drawn,
that matters little or nothing.
* * * *
“The fact of the matter,” writes Mr. R.
Griffiths to The Bulletin re a recent paragraph, “is that
three of the old directors of the Overflow * retired ’
because a sweeping majority of shareholders desired a
change and insisted on a progressive policy in connection
with the mine being adopted. The shareholders are
anxious to see their money expended on machinery and
erection of water jacket furnace, so as to enable the
yields of auriferous matte and bullion to speak for the
mine. The old board was going too slow for them, hence
the ‘ retirement.’ Again, the shares were not sold at half
former rates, and any offered were eagerly snapped
up. As one of the much-maligned ‘ squatters,’ I am
content to leave judgment on the wisdom of our
policy to stand over for another twelve months, fully
satisfied that the mine, and results therefrom, will
vindicate us. At least, we are free from the imputa-
tion of gulling the public and inducing them to put their
money into a wild-cat venture. We have developed the
mine quietly with our own money, and are going to work
it as a legitimate investment. If it fails, we lose our
money, as we have not attempted to unload on the public.
I see iio reason why men associated with the pastoral in-
dustry cannot manage the business affairs of a mine,
when guided by the practical experi-nce of a man of Mr.
John Howell’s standing in the mining world. At anv-
rate, we are not hampered by the prejudices of exploded
theories and obsolete practices of so-called ‘ mining men.’ ”
* * * *
The Vic. Railway Department proposes to close
the Lancefield-Kilmore railway. This little cross-country
line, 17J4 miles in length, runs from one sleepy little town,
that the inhabitants are too tired to leave, to another
similar town, which no possible individual wants to go to.
The earnings last year were £150; working expenses,
£lOO2 ; interest on cost, £852. The interest goes on just
the same, but abandonment means a saving of £852 in
ex’s. This is the second-worst line in Victoria. The very
worst is the Maffra-Briagolong railway, which last year
earned £3Ol, and cost £3046 in working expenses and
£2745 in interest.
♦ * *
The great practical value of the new Victorian
Companies Act is best evidenced by the balance-sheet of
the Standard Bank (Melbourne). Under the form of
balance-sheet prescribed by the Act the unspeakable in-
wardness of the Standard becomes really visible for the
first time. The half-year’s loss is £7992, against £7063,
£1513, and £4908 lost in three previous half-years. The
institution has apparently been losing more since the
interest on deposits was reduced to 2£ per cent, than it
did at the old rate. The interest payable for the half-
year is £9938, and the net takings out of which it was to
be paid were just £1946, so the Standard is really in
a position to pay its depositors only nine shillings per
cent, instead of £2 10s. The liabilities are £1,294,000,
and the “ assets” are almost the most weird and ragged
collection in Australia. They include amount lost this
half-year, £7992 ; amount lost previously, £16,394 ; debts
known to be bad and utterly irrecoverable, £347,413; debts
known to be doubtful and which may prove to
be almost wholly bad, £535,186. (These latter, under
the old Act, were simply lumped in with the “Ad-
vance.”) This makes £906,985 which is either lost
or probably lost. Of the remaining £386,990 of assets
£18,078 is “ Deferred Interest” apparently owing by the
bank ; and only £356,000 looks like real assets. Even
this isn’t wholly certain, for bank premises (£41,457) are
put in at their original boom value with no allowance for
depreciation ; but still this £356,000 looks more real than
the rest. Yet even it can’t stand much investigation. As-
suming that the £535,186 of doubtful debts are really bad
and yield no interest whatever, and that the whole of the
interest and rents are derived from the £356,000 of assets
that profess to be still sound, these yield only about
£1 17s. percent, gross per annum. As the sound advances
of any bank, even in these times, should yield 5 per cent,
gross, the evidence of the interest returns seems to be
that fully half of the Standard Bank’s small remnant of
apparently sound assets are very shaky, and out of
£1,294,000 of alleged assets only about £150,000 look
decently above suspicion. Forming a rough estimate
from its own figures, the Standard Bank has lost all its
capital, and is just now in a position to pay at the most
55., or possibly only 2s. 6d. or less in the £ to' its
depositors, but as it is paying interest out of principal
instead of out of earnings, this amount is steadily grow-
ing less. The public endorses this view by offering from
Is. to 2s. in the £ for the deposits. The great reconstruc-
tion system which allows this mass of financial dry-rot
to remain above-ground, and call itself a bank, and take
deposits, and have an office and a board and a manager,
is a truly wondrous affair.
The full extent of the collapse is perhaps shown
best of all in the “gross profits.” For the half-year
ended December, ’95, these were said to be £17,241; for
the next half they appeared as £10,962 ; for the half
ended December, ’96, they are set down as £3269. Does
the management allege that the Standard Bank is really
going to the bad at this rate ; or that the old profits were
largely bogus, made by charging interest that couldn’t
be recovered on advances which were known to be lost,
and treating it as interest actually received ?
» • * *
The Australian Deposit and Mortgage Bank is
feeling rather better. In 1895 this boom institution
obtained a re-arrangement whereby three-tenths of its
deposits bore no interest at all and seven-tenths carried
only 3J per cent. It thus saved £lB,OOO a year in interest.
The last half-year before the re-arrangement the loss was
£7566, while for the half-year ended Dec., 1896, the profit
is £978. With £9OOO saved in the half-year the profit, cn
the 1895 basis, should be about £1450, so things have not
become much worse than they were 18 months ago, and
they are decidedly better than they were six months ago.
Last half the net profits were fourpence per cent, per
annum on capital; this one they are 4s. 6d. per cent.,
and that is something for a boom land bank these times.
Deposits have been reduced by £83,000 during the year.
The prospects of the shareholders are not much at pre-
sent, but something should be left for them after the
creditors have got 20s. in the £. Deposits have buyers at
125., so the market estimates the A.D. and M. Bank as
from six to twelve times as solvent as the smellful Stan-
dard.
* * * *
The Land Mortgage Bank of Victoria announces
that it has got through the half-year with a loss of
£10,494, against £9696, £8847, and £6616 lost in the pre-
vious three half-years. The trouble with the Land Mort-
gage spectre is that the entire takings are only £12,024,
while interest payable on debentures alone is over
£20,000. The institution owes £900,000 on debentures at
4J per cent., and it can only afford to pay 2 per cent.,
but so far its bowelless creditors have refused to grant any
relief. There are hopes, however, of a reduction to 3£
per cent., which won’t anything like fill the deficit. The
L.M. Bank purports to have £1,032,000 lent on mortgage,
but, as this sum only yields about 2£ per cent, interest,
the actual value of the lot is probably more like £400,000,
or less. It is admitted that the loans are put in at
their old boom value, and a million in boom paper values
may mean anything nowadays from £200,000 upwards.
An attempt is being made to call up £250,000 more capital,
but so far the shareholders have only responded with
£38,000. Evidently the Land Mortgage can’t raise enough
money in calls to pay off its debenture-holders, and it
can’t get the debenture-holders to reduce interest suf-
ficiently to cover the regular half-yearly shortage, and the
reduction which its hardened creditors are thinking of
making is of practically no use at all. Under all these
conditions, it would be interesting to know what the
bank proposes to do about itself. It is a matter of reason-
able certainty that its capital is all gone in depreciation
long ago, and it looks like a concern which might pay 10s.
or so, in the £ at present values on its liabilities to the
public.
• * » •
The West Australian Bank is having a good
time in the boom season. Whether the directors, being
unaccustomed to booms in the hitherto sober land of
Westralia, will know enough to take their bank in out of
the wet when the tension comes, remains to be seen. The
half-year’s profits are £16,703 compared with £12,284 last
half, and £10,972 and £10,315 for the two previous ones.
A year further back the profits used to average about
£BOOO. The W.A. Bank pays a dividend of 17£ per cent.,
and out of its accumulated profit balance it adds £25,000
to reserve. Also, it has sold £20,000 worth of new shares
for £45,000, the premium of £25,000 also going to reserve,
which now stands at £150,000. In the half-year, note
circulation has gone up from £95,000 to £156,000, and
deposits have decreased about £150,000. The W.A. Bank
has £945,000 in cash —equal to 48 per cent, of its total
liabilities. It has next to no interest-bearing deposits :
in the happy land of Westralia everybody leaves his
money with the bank gratis, and the bank keeps half of
it in the cellar and earns stupendous dividends by lending
out the other half. The institution earns 33 per cent, on
its capital, while in the East the banker grinds out his
soul wondering how to show a profit of 5 per cent, or
even less. It is opening new branches in all directions,
and growing daily larger, and its accounts seem to be
never audited ; in its own joyous Westralian way it trusts
to its regular officials, and publishes no auditors’ certi-
ficate whatever. And, judging by experiences in the East
with Ananias the auditor, it is quite as well without his
certificate.
* * * *
The United Australian Mutual Fire Insurance
Co. explains that the year’s profit is £2064, and a 6 per
cent, dividend absorbs £lBB4 of this. The balance, £lBO,
added to £2236 previously on hand, leaves, as the total
accumulation of 17 years, a sum of £2416. The U.A. Co.
has no reserve-fund, a capital of only £31,000, and a mort-
gage of £27,000 on its office property, and it is one more
of the many small Australian fire-offices which struggle
along year after year, eking out a humble dividend with
difficulty, and getting hardly any further forward. The
other lot consists mostly of big companies which are
either stationary or are steadily losing their foothold and
going backwards, and the companies that are making any
progress worth mentioning can be counted on the fingers.
The swarm of foreign institutions in Australia is steadily
eating up or crowding out the local offices, and scarcely a
year passes now without one more of the native companies
disappearing out of the list. Unless some Government
imposes a huge absentee-tax on the foreign companies, or
shuts them out altogether, this branch of business pro-
mises before very long to be almost wholly in the hands
of British and German institutions, and when the Aus-
tralian is burnt out guttural people named Hans Schmidt
will dispute the claim in a strange tongue under instruc-
tions from some place in Bavaria nr Bohemia
* * * *
The Sydney Permanent Freehold Land and
Building Co., which has done three reconstructions (the
one now in force is to the effect that the deposits
shall bear no interest at all and shall never fall due, but
shall be repaid by instalments of Is. in the £ at any time
when the money is available), states that it is still going
down hill. The loss for 1896 was £2864, against £3941 for
1895, and nearly £16,000 for 1894. Last year the institu-
tion with the long name was working under its second
reconstruction, by which interest on its £135,000 worth
of deposits was reduced to 2£ per cent., and as it couldn’t
make a living on these terms it remains to be seen
whether it can pay expenses when it gets its deposits
gratis. Another £58,000 is written off the alleged assets.
The S.P.F.L. & B. Co. has been the luckiest concern in
Australia in the matter of reconstruction, and it shows a
paper surplus of assets over liabilities of £85,000. The
public opinion of the said paper surplus is best shown by
the fact that the co.’s deposits are selling at about 7s. in
the £.
* * * *
Another tired small relic of the good times,
the Mortgage, Guarantee and Mercantile Finance Co.
(Sydney),"states that it has lost £3Bl in the last half-year,
which is about its normal condition for a very long time
back. The little concern with the long name tried to be
a land-bank in the old roaring days, but the tide ebbed,
and left it stranded with £44,000 of more-or-less unsale-
able property, and a big mortgage and other troubles.
Now it makes calls, which never seem to come in, the last
one being 2s. 6d. per share, in the hope of clearing off the
mortgage and starting fair. The capital is about £31,000
all told, and £2500 of it is lost, and the co. hangs out un-
obtrusively in a room somewhere, and tries to pacify its
depositors, who amount to £14,691. The M. G. and M.
F. Co. is a sad example of the way twopence used to
gather itself together and start a bank in the brighter
days gone by, and how mighty little was considered
sufficient excuse for a manager, and board, and all the
other paraphernalia. ,
“ Bananal ander ” : If a big reduction of deposit-
interest is obtained, it will almost certainly pull through ;
if not, it will burst, and the whole capital be called up.
Chances are about even. Can’t advise you without know-
ing your circumstances. If things go right, when divi-
dends are resumed you will probably get 50 per cent, per
annum on your investment. If they go wrong, you lose
your money and are liable for calls. The present writer
would go in—if he had the money to risk.
* * * *
At time of writing, the British depositors are
growling ominously about the proposed reconswindle of
the Q.N. Bank, and there is still no definite news about
the A.J.S. Bank re-arrangement. There is an ebb and
flow in everything. The first batch of reconstructions
came on J. Bull in one of his expansive moments, and he
agreed affably. The City of Melb. re-arrangement ar-
rived on the scene when his liver was out of order, and
there was a flat refusal and liquidation. Then the re-
arrangement of the Commercial and various others found
him feeling good, and things went through with little
difficulty. Now the Bull liver seems doubtful, and no
one knows what will happen. For that matter, the
Q.N.B. proposals are enough to upset all the liver in
creation.
• * * *
Montgomerie’s Brewery Co,, another of the for-
lorn beer-enterprises of Melbourne, goes from bad to
worse. For the half-year ended 3lst December the loss
was £3055. Last half it was £lllO ; at December, ’95, it
was £1660, so the downwardness is very conspicuous. The
balance-sheet is a weird affair. Assets include “ good-
will” £25,000 (think of the stupendous cheek which
reckons the goodwill of a business that is losing over
£4OOO a year as worth anything at all); “incidental
purchase expenses,” £1682; “expenditure on well,”
£1444 ; and amount lost £1485. As none of
these items are saleable it may be estimated
that, by its own showing, Montgomerie’s is over £29,000
to the bad. Further, the assets include £lOO9 lent with-
out security ; over £20,000 of advances at book value—
not net value; the overdraft, which is gradually in-
creasing, is now over £70,000 ; and the business looks too
small to be worth talking about, for the sales for the half-
year are set down at £4396. If this really means the
entire sales, there is something colossal in the idea of
having £140,000 paid-up capital for the purpose of selling
less than £9OOO worth of liquor per annum.
* * * «
The V.D.L. Bank liquidators have got the
creditors to accept 10s. in the £, payable in half-crown
instalments. This move successfully dodges £16,000 of
the shareholders’ liability in the shape of interest due to
Govt., Parliament having agreed to forgo the claim if the
creditors of the bank did their part towards pulling the
shareholders out of the mud. Thus £16,000 of the tax-
payers’ money goes as a subsidy to a few apostles of
“ Private Enterprise,” whose ability to run a bank shines
with painful conspicuousness in the history of Raspberry
Land’s flattest failure.
* * * *
Three Syrians were recently fined £lOOO in
Brisbane for defrauding the Customs by means of ficti-
tious invoices, undervaluing goods supplied. Investiga-
tion showed that the Asiatics were in the habit of cheat-
ing the Customs in nearly every port, and it transpired
also that many Sydney firms habitually supplied cus-
tomers with fictitious invoices.
* * * *
The Colonial Sugar Refining Co. is the great
fact in Coastal Bananaland. It owns large plantations
on the Herbert, at Geraldton and Homebush ; possesses,
also, large mills at the Isis, and a big refinery at Bulimba
Reach, Brisbane. The co. has bought Maeknade Planta-
tion on the Herbert for £65,000, and is also making over-
tures for other properties. Queensland at present sup-
plies about half the sugar required by Australia.
* * * *
The idea is to use E. W. Hawker’s favorable
report in tempting J. Bull to take over the Yuba. Hence
the otherwise mysterious firmness of the stock.

• * * *
While the chairman of the B.H. Water Supply,
Ltd., was assuring shareholders that the B.H. people
were satisfied with the water, the curses on the water,
the co., and the Govt, which permits the stinking fluid to
be retailed at 6d. per 100 (meter rent extra always) were
louder and deeper than at any time previous. Indeed,
8.H., feeling a little more secure than ever it did hitherto,
has come to the conclusion that the present position is
intolerable; and opposition, municipal or private, is
pretty well assured. Meanwhile, though, the demand by
the mining co’s for concentrating purposes increases
rapidly, and the 8.H.W.5., Ltd., has that business well in
hand.

A correspondent“ I think you are wrong in
supposing that if the main (Eastern) block of the North
B.H. doesn’t get the Junction North body, the other
block (the Western) must have it. If the lode doesn’t
double back, but continues in its divergent course, and
persists, it must just about miss that Western block, and
the Sultan would have it instead. And, further, the
underlay is away from the North’s western block.
“ Proved at one point in Block 10, near the
Central boundary, that the sulphide body is 275 ft. wide.
But, then, at another it is only 35ft.”
* * * *
Nundle (N.S.W.)—“ Stringer and party are
working in the vicinity where a miner got £5OOO worth of
specimens in a few days.” Where ?—when ?—who ? Pro-
bably over 40 years ago, and it is well known that, once
out of the ground, gold grows rapidly with age. Nundle
is a warm place where specimens would multiply their
weight by at least five in 40 years.

The manager of the Great Western mine, Cobar
(N.S.W.), has resigned. Result of last crushing still
shrouded in mystery, and the leaving of the boss is by no
means a sign that it wasn’t an awful frost.

For years the public have been reading every
two or three weeks of the 5 and 10 ton crushings from
Mt. Dromedary mine, N.S.W., generally for about the
same number of oz. A mine with an output of less than
a ton of ore a day hardly deserves so much publicity.

The “Mt. M'Donald Gold Mines Co.” regis-
tered in London. Six or seven years ago it looked as
though Hongkong was going to be a good place to sell
N.S.W. mines. It started in by buying at a big price a
Mt M‘Donald gold-mine, once called the Balmoral, and it
has been cursing and kicking itself ever since. The man
who took a N.S.W. mine to Hongkong now would pro-
bably never leave that |sle alive.

* * * *
Bed-Log: “Showing how very careful Mel
bourne papers are not to mislead the poor miner, take an
instance from the Leader, which, in a recent special
mining supplement, recommends, amongst other specs.,
the Gembrook dykes as a field calculated to repay honest
effort As well recommend Collingwood flat for sluicing.
It is quite two years since these dyke formations at Gem-
brook were tried and found wanting—wanting in gold.
There is not one working now ; in fact, there are only
about three persons hunting gold in the district, and they
do not make tucker—that is, what a Melb. soup-kitchen
habitud would call decent feeding. There are batteries
on the field, but the sound of a stamp has not been h *ard
in the land for over a year. One of these days a deceived
digger will tramp to Melbourne and kill an editor or two,
and then perhaps there will be some attempt to verify
mining-news in newspaper-offices. I have heard scores of
miners express a homicidal yearning in the above direc-
tion.”
* * * *
Mt. Charlotte reported to have really struck
gold at last. It has been trying for nearly three years.
Time, too, that the good old Maritana really found some
gold. It was a newspaper-mine, and it is impossible to
say anything worse than that.
* * * *
What next ? The Coolgardie Chamber of
Mines!—an institution composed largely of mining
boomsters, and, therefore, distinguished foritscheek—has
petitioned Premier Forrest to erect State batteries for
the treatment of ores at the chief mining centres. For-
rest is said to have expressed sympathy with the idea,
and is going to consider. He might also consider the
number of batteries already around Coolgardie having,
like the boomsters, nothing to do.

Whispered that the current Queensland Menzies
(W.A.) crushing will not be much above 3oz. per ton. A

* * * *
Much emphasis is novv put on the fact that
though the lode has been passed through at the 300 ft.
level of the Great Boulder, the South drive must go over
100 ft. before reaching good ore. The inference is also
that the lode is poor going North. At one time the
papers tried to lead the public to imagine that it was all
rich, that the gold did not run in chutes, but was one
continuous run right through this mine, and on through
several others. None the less, the G.B. has proved
itself a great mine.
• • * *
Another mine— this time the Lake View Con-
sols- has bought a new 20-head battery made by Fraser
and Chalmers, lying unused in Coolgardie. The advent
of batteries is alw-ays “ hailed with much satisfaction ” by
newspapers. The fact that they are often never erected
and sold for less money than they cost in freight alone
is of no public interest, and is, therefore, never
published except by The Bulletin, whioh occasionally
suffers from liver-congestion.
* * * »
Low as are the shares of W. A. mines in London,
let any Australian holder of average stock try to sell at
the prices given in the financial papers, and he will find
out his mistake. One tried lately to quit at 50 per cent,
lower than the quoted price, and yet was not able to get
out. Nine cases in ten the prices published are no
criterion of those obtainable.
* * » *
It takes a remarkably good Australian mine to
stand London management. Last Eng. mail brought the
directors’ reports and statements of accounts of four
English co.’s working mines in Westralia. All are
managed in the same office, the directors (with two ex-
ceptions) consist of the same individuals, the reports are
got up in the same style, the covers only differently
colored, by Waterlow and Sons, the fashionable printers.
The expenditure in London amounted for the four co.’s
to over £lO,OOO, and fully two-thirds of this went in
directors’ fees, halance in salaries and office exs. None of
the mines are div.-paying, and the prospect of their ever
reaching that stage is remote. Will poor—or rich —Bull
ever reflect that all the div.-paying mines of Bendigo and
Ballarat combined do not spend as much money in local
management as £10,000? The writer can picture the
weekly meeting of these directors —one meeting following
quickly on the heels of another, the chairman only chang-
ing seats, and the secretary busily engaged in delicately
handing around the fees in sovs.. The average London
guinea-pig likes to be paid on the spot in sovs. So appro-
priate in a gold-mine, dontcherknow.
« * * *
Murchison Gold Fields Co. meeting in London.
Chairman : “ The ventures this co. undertook have turned
out great disappointments. Our case is by no means
singular, the field has been a disappointment. Reefs
promising well, near the surface, have pinched out alto-
gether, or have become too poor to work.” A shareholder
remarked : “ The history of the co. was unfortunately
similar to that of three-fourths of the gold-mines which
had been floated during the last boom. Shareholders
were all lured by the splendid promises of the prospec-
tuses, and, in fact, thought they would have been fools if
they had not jumped at such an easy way of obtaining a
fortune ; and had taken no notice of the articles of associa-
tion, by which they were bound. The result, generally,
had been that the promoters and directors had got the
nuts, and shareholders the husks, and that was not a
satisfactory diet.” Chairman’s reply :“ He looked upon
the whole of West Australia as more or less a swindle.
He did not believe that 5 per cent, of the companies were
any good, and, as a director of this co., he would, have
nothing more to do with W.A. properties if he could help
it.”
* » * *
Prof. Krause’s first report after seeing the
General Gordon mine, W.A., has been described as
“encouraging.” It is a sick mine that won’t stand an
“ encouraging report.” To determine the extent of the
gold-bearing formation he started to open-cut the surface
near the prospecting shafts. Free gold was observed
along the outs, but it was very fine. At a greater depth
he thinks it will become coarser. When he has made
detailed surveys and examinations he will prepare a full
report “on the mining conditions of the property.” The
latest excuse for not crushing is that some of the battery
fittings have been “mis-sent.” It was going to crush
four months ago ; now whispered that it is not likely to
crush this month or next. If the G.G. is anything like
the mine it is boomed to be, Krause has been there long
enough to say so. A good mine is as plain as the nose on
a man’s face. Rumor has it that a somewhat disagreeable
surprise of a financial character awaits the G.G. share-
holders.
* * * *
The aftermath of the Westralian boom in Lon-
don. A shareholder at a meeting of a W.A. mine recently
held in London : “I bought my shares in this mine
through my bankers, while I wcs in Constantinople, and
I can assure you that, generally speaking, my feelings as
to mining co. directors are such that I honestly think it
would not have been a bad thing if half of them had been
in Constantinople recently and been mistaken for Ar-
menians.”

First clean-up Waratah Gold Mines, Croydon
(Q,), yielded 1020 oz. of poor gold—under 20s. per oz.
Fifty tons concentrates remaining; value about £7OO.
The bullion won locally should pay exes. ; concentrates
go to England, and should spell divs.
* * » *
Croydon King P.C. and the North Amalga-
mated, principally held in Sydney, struck gold at 200 ft.
vertical. As the precious metal was never found there
previously within 100 ft. of this depth, the development is
important. Some shallow ground workings previously
gave tens of oz. to the ton.

thousands oz. gold Govt, geologist Sands damned the
former, the Govt, diamond drill the latter. It would
have paid the people to have given Mr. Sands a thousand
or tw'o to keep away from the field.

COOLALTA
Admiral Bridge is very much averse to use-
less matrimonial engagements amongst his officers,
so the visit of the fleet to Hobart this season has
been remarkably unsatisfactory to ma’s who have
shoals of marriageable daughters. In one of the
commissions nearly all the midshipmen got en-
gaged ! This was beyond a joke, so the Admiral
said if any more such nonsense took place he would
despatch the ships to obscure quarters out of harm’s
way—for he “ wouldn’t have the boys’ schooling
interfered with.”
* * * *
La Revanche may be a long time coming,
but France and Germany don’t love each other too
heartily even now. The two nations differ in
most things, and, doing so, can’t even agree on
astronomical certainties, apparently. The French
warship Amiral Parsifal and the German Falke
are lying up at Mort’s Dock re-fitting, and it is
amusing to notice that each has a different sunset-
time. Each tries to get in first with gun, bugle,
and ensign down-haul, and the loser, in a dignified
manner, performs the bedding ceremony of the
sun a few minutes later ; as if to hint that the
other shop—ship, we mean—didn’t know the rules.

ship of the—haw ! —Union Club ?

A prominent resident of Lawler’s (W.A.)
has been fined £2O (or two months’ gaol) for
sportively flicking a fly off a barmaid’s nose.
Barmaids’ noses are precious at Lawler’s.

An attempted nurses’ strike is the latest
device of the disaffected Adelaide doctors, and
Govt, recently had to warn a prominent medico’s
wife to cease visiting the Hospital owing to her
deliberate attempts to cause trouble. These miser-
able medical disputes will actually affect the Con-
vention-election chances of several able politicians.
• * * *
From Murchison (W.A.) Times :
In prosecuting his search for the missing man, the con-
stable found it necessary to chain up some gins, who,
after being chained up for some time, gave him informa-
tion which enabled him to find the skeletons of three
men who, they said, had been speared by the natives
some time last year.
“Chaining up gins in order to make them confess,
is a not uncommon practice in the Westralian
back country ” (writes a Bulletin correspondent).
“ One case that came under my notice : Prospec-
tors were working a show some hundred miles
inland, and the nearest water they knew of was
25 miles distant, whence they brought it per
horse and cart. Round about where they were
working there were blacks, and so the whites
knew there must be water close by, but the niggers
would not show them where it was ; so they got a
gin and chained her up for 36 hours, gave. her
plenty salt food to eat, but nothing |to drink ;
finally let her go, and followed close on her heels.
She went straight away for three miles, and,
stooping down, drank from a small rock-hole.”
* » * *
“No mistake,” writes a Bulletin cor-
respondent, “black-gins are ideal Socialists and
equal distributors of wealth. I lately saw one of
our country-women suckling her piccaninny on
one breast and a half-bred kangaroo-pup on the
other. At the same time, she was drawing a
stumpy, black, clay pipe and blowing the smoke
through a bamboo into the mouth of her dusty
lord and master, whose head rested in her lap.”
Verily, the heathen retaliates. A little
while ago, The Bulletin hears, a young man from
the Glebe, Sydney, was shipped off to India by
Australia’s best-known camel-merchant, as a con-
vert to Islam. We can expect a ship-load of
Buddhist and Mohammedan missionaries shortly.
They’re sadly wanted.
* * * *
The defence of young Phillips, charged
with the Elizabeth-st. (Melb.) murder, seems cer-
tain to involve a violent attack on the character of
the man who’s dead. At the request of Phillips’
solicitors, the trial has been postponed, to enable
them to collect “ a great mass ” of evidence from
people who are either “ unwilling ” to speak, or are
not discoverable at present. The production of
this mass of evidence is very much needed for the
credit of the firm who worked up the prisoner’s
gaudy confession. As the matter now stands,
Phillips is practically on his trial for criminal libel,
as well as for murder.

That modern solecism, a school committee,
is fighting Queensland Educational Department
about the sister of a leper-boy, now in the
lazarette, who insists on attending the local State
school. The committee sends in report after re-
port urging that the girl shall be removed, and the
Department treats the committee in the old, well-
known way.

One Austra-
lian capital re-
joices in a female

whose transac-

cause the Supreme

after her health.
On such occasions,
she sends medical

gets excused from
attendance be-

a critical state of
health.” So it
was, months ago.
So it was, still,
last week. Yet
she hasn’t affected

Latest Maoriland census shows that to
3685 Chinese males in that province there are only
26 yellow females. Naturally, there is a great
demand for white-girl Bible-teachers among the
Maoriland heathen. The yellow vegetable-person
never takes his spiritual consolation from a bearded
elder. The elder’s plump daughter has proved to
be far preferable as a guardian angel.
The ravages of the cormo-
rant in Northern Victoria have
led the Government to offer a
reward of 3d. for each bird. A
very large cormorant-killing in-
dustry has accordingly sprung
up in the wild regions along the
N.E. borders. The cormorant-
killer pegs in manfully for a
fortnight or a month until he
has accumulated, perhaps, 3000
birds, and then he humps them
along to the nearest Customs
officer to be counted and in-
spected, and the heads cut off
as a guarantee against fraud.
The cormorant doesn't smell
nice at any time, and 3000 of
him that have accumulated for
a in the hot weather
smell something unspeakable.
Lately the hunters have been
complaining that the Customs
man is often a long way off, and
have demanded that the rural
postmaster or postmistress (it is
generally the latter) should be
appointed to overhaul the shud-
dersome mass of fow and pay
the bounty, but the Govt, has
decided that it can’t ask any
woman to inspect and count and
behead all these crates of de-
composed bird, and to run after
the deadest bird of the lot when
it starts off round the corner, so
the present arrangement must
stand. Besides, the present ar-
rangement brings grist to the
mill; many of the fowlers send
their birds along to the Customs
by rail, as may be seen by the
millions of blow-flies that scoot
fter the train—and thea freights
all help business along.
In connection with the recent robberies of
uncut diamonds from Sydney Technological Mu-
seum, why cannot the trustees take a hint from the
precautionary measures enforced by the British
Museum? In that institution, the moment that
any one of the air-tight glass cases is broken an
electric-bell loudly sounds the alarm, whereupon
all doors of egress are immediately closed, so that
the detective on duty can speedily locate and
follow the movements of those persons who were
m the vicinity of the disturbance.

Brethren, we are this clay face to face with a brighter
future. The tide of commercial prosperity is rising'; men

on the ene of a lengthened period of prosperity that will
it possible for us to do and dare for Christ.
Ihe collection will now be taken up. In token of
the brighter future, coppers are deprecated,
threepence more, and up goes the donkey !

Crowned heads of Europe have always
given their royal patronage to Ed. Pinaud, of
Paris, but never so much as lately ; his perfumes
are now recognised as incomparably the best in
Europe. The biggest hits of Ed. Pinaud have
been “ Yiolette Reine ” (quite different from
“Rhine Violet”), and “Preciosa,” the sweetest
and most delicate perfumes ever made ; in fact,
they are known everywhere as the quintessences
of the richest flowers grown in France, and are
sure to be rushed by everybody.*
Mr. Frank Senior, who has been chemist
to the N.S. Wales Governors for many years past,
has conferred a blessing upon the citizens of his
country by discovering a wonderful cure for head-
ache. The new remedy cures instantaneously, is
very palatable, and perfectly harmless to both
ladies and gentlemen. Moreover, it is perfectly
effectual-, no matter what the cause of the head-
ache may be. This cure, the result of years of
experience, can be had post free for 1/3. Senior,
chemist, George-street, Sydney.*

Factories Act. The latest contention (upheld by
Melb. City Court) is that an must
demand his half-holiday every week—or lose all
legal claim to it. Employers and Tories gener-
ally recognise that if this rule obtains no employe
dare demand his holidays for fear of dismissal.
Chief Inspector Ord, pending an appeal, is
strictly enforcing the popular idea of the Act.
• * * *
The other day, a Hawthorn (Melb.) Chow
laundryman was fined, under the Factories Act,
for working his “ washee ’’-mill after hours, and
Melbourne Conservatism fell upon Sam Yik’s
chest, and wept upon his shrivelled neck, declaring,
that he was a martyr to the cause. The fine-
money was raised by subscription, and Sam Yik
smiled right round his head, and went home, and
placidly broke the law more than ever. This time
his fine was £6, and expenses were added, and
Sam Yik, blue with remorse, sits amongst his
suds waiting for another subscription, but his
admirers, recognising suddenly that such a martyr
is likely to become expensive, have allowed him
to slip from their minds.

If the New Guinea massacre of 40 be fol-
lowed by arrests it will mean not only 40 or more
convictions but at least 40 executions. Gov.
Macgregor believes in teaching a “ great moral
lesson ” on all such occasions, and will show the
murderers and their friends that life will be
exacted for life. But won’t the executioner make
a rise if paid at ordinary rates ! The New Guinea
man-butcher is a member of the civil list who in
addition to “ keep ” gets £5 for each execution.

Tas. Benevolent Society—which runs the
biggest industry in the Land of Sleep a Lot—asks

Landlords will throw it out as an interference with

Up in. the far north
of the Piebald Land, a
freshly-arrived Mahom-
medan lately prose-
cuted a new - chum
Chinaman for some-
thing connected with
wood. It was a blazing
day, and the magis-
trate’s liver was out of
order, he having kept it
up late with the local
sub-inspector the pre-
vious night ; and the
flies and the mosquitoes
raged tremendously,
and a dust-storm blew
continuously. The local
interpreter was missing,
and the court tackled
the case single-handed.
The Mahommedan
couldn’t be properly
sworn for want of a
Koran, and the China-
man was only partly
sworn, because, instead
.of blowing out the
match, the dust-storm
came in and blew it
out for him. The
Mahommedan tried to
explain the charge to
the court and the court
tried to explain it to
the Chinaman. Neither
succeeded. Then the
Chinaman tried to ex-
plain something at
great length, ai d the
court, which didn’t un-
derstand him, tried to
convey his meaning to
the Mahommedan, and
failed badly. Finally
the court drew out of
the contest, and stood
aside while the China-
man and the Moham-
medan yelled in differ-
ent languages at each
other. By-and-byetheir
row lulled it to sleep,
and it dozed off leaving
the policeman to act as
peacemaker and bottle-
holder to the litigants.
It woke after half-an-
hour and found the
policeman gone and the
Chinaman and the
Moslem still harangu-
ing each other incomprehensibly with an idea
that they were getting justice. Then it dozed
off again, and when it woke an hour later both
litigants were gone, so an entry was made to the
effect that the matter had been settled out of court,
and the embodiment of the law went home.
Away down the street a cloud of dust showed
where the Mahommedan and the Chow were still
arguing, with a casual Afghan and a small boy
and a dog and a goat and a superannuated working
bullock looking on. And of such is the course ot
justice in the Piebald Land.

* * * *
Bumbledom is more than adequately re-
presented in Melb. by the local Benevolent Asylum
committee. Several members of this'body seem to
regard the unhappy old folk under their charge
as little better than” aged and useless animals, who
should be knocked on the head without delay to
save the expense of their keep. The asyluin supt.
suggested last week that, in order to enable the
inmates to enjoy a warm meal now and then, it
would be a graceful and charitable act on the part
of the committee if it allowed them to have hot
plates occasionally. The members nearly bit his
head off, and scouted the proposition as an auda-
cious piece of extravagance, which meant coddling
and pampering the inmates. Poor old inmates!
Then the humorous •. Jaffray arose, bubbling
with merriment, and delivered himself of an
original couplet to this hoggish effect:
Oh ! they’re all fat and flourishing,
Happy, warm, and nourishing.
Whereat his brother Bumbles laughed eonsumedly.
Here is a quatrain for Hot-plate Jaffray’s couplet:
The man who at adversity
Can jibe like this, with clumsy art,
He has, though fat his purse may be,
A pauper-head and pauper-heart.

Policy is the worst honesty.
* * * *
“Death equalises all,” as the long-suffer-
ing wife of a tyrannical husband realised when he
was called to take bis seat for the final stage.
There he lay helpless and yellow, his arms by his
side, his jaw slung to his head at last. It
was reported he had beaten her, and with a
woman’s weapon, his tongue. Their life together
had been one long, bitter, endless argument, and
he always had the last word. How the wife felt
this cruel wrong and robbery of her rights oidy
another wife can tell —and won’t. The last few
moments arrived, a rill of pity slowly coursed the
heart-husk of the watching wife, and she said
“ You’ll be better off shortly.” The “ about-to-
die,” with a heroic effort, steadied his quavering
eyes, flashed a world of meaning at her, gasped
‘‘rather ! ” and died like a coward ere she could
retaliate.

“Lady” Munro has again returned to
Melbourne after an absence of some years. As is
her custom, she attends Wesley Church on a
Sunday, and in the past her behaviour, with one
or two exceptions, has been excellent. A few
Sundays ago she attended a “ pleasant" Sunday
afternoon ” service. All went well until the
orchestra started to play a piece of operatic music.
This was more than her ladyship could stand, and
she at once loudly remarked that she “always
thought this was a church until now.”

Re policemen and their tempers. A man arrested a
few years ago in Bourke-street, Melb., for “ insooltimr be-
havure” didn’t take it kindly, and to ease his mind the
arresting constable banged the arrestee’s head several
times on the asphalt pavement. Next morning, usual
charge of “ insoolting behavure, resistin’ the po-laise,”
&c. Cross charge by accused of constabulary violence.
Denied by the handcuffer and a brother peeler who had
“ seen it all, your Worship.” Suddenly from chairman
Panton, P.M. : “ Case dismissed. I was in the crowd my-
self.” Blue fire; blue funk; two dismissals from the
force.

No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Same and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily subinitted, and in no case will MS. be
returned unless stamps (of any province) accompany.

an independent spectator alike of the calamitous blunders
of vanity-stuffed boss priests and their eggers-on and the
wild cavortingsof their enemies, the jealous and infuriated
parsons. Also, The Bulletin picked 10 N.S.W. Conven-
tion candidates, not as a political “tip,” but on their
publicly-expressed views concerning Federation, especially
concerning the railway and financial questions. The
Bulletin does not worship immediate success, else would
it have picked Cardinal Moran and the gang of well-and-
long-advertised old political hacks, mostly (as has here

of real Federation. As to your remark : “ Your paper

citizens, and yet urges the latter to vote for its own 10
nominees,” you carefully blink the situation, which is
that The Bulletin, in its selection, appeals to logic and
principle, whereas the parsons urge that their 10 men be
voted for because they are “ Prodistants,” consumed with a

A STRIKING
GIRL.
FOR THE BULLETIN.
BY THE BREAKER
She was a biking girl—a giddy creature,
Who ’round the neighbourhood had wheeled of late,
With figure angular and flinty feature,
An oldish girl—although quite up-to-date!
It was the mellow hour the sunlight lingers
Among the tree-tops-—just ere shadows pass ;
She held a cigarette between her fingers,
And rested, with her bike, upon the grass.
We don't, ourselves, give heed to empty rumors,
But we saw this: just as the sun took flight,
She took'a match, and on those shapeless bloomers
Struck it —quite man-like —and obtained a light.

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
. _ _ _ Sydney, March 2,1897.
Mr Dear Mtee— ’
_ _".Matsa ” opened on Saturday night at Her
Majesty’s in a loud hum of expectant excitement.
Among the audience, which was full-dressed half
way to the back of the stalls, there were so many
of the Ancient People that it was Egypt on the
stage and the land of Goshen in front of the house.
The Chosen went down into Egypt for the second
time on record and seemed to enjoy it as much as
their progenitors. “ Matsa ”is a wild, glittering
dream of oriental gorgeousness and profusion;
there isn’t any plot, or, if there is one, it is flooded
by the Nile rising in Act 11, but there are real-
looking mummy-cloths and bright-hued drapery
and Cairene silks and blue-gowned, red-capped
donkey-boys, and almond-eyed beauties enough to
set up a color-enthusiast with subjects for a life-
time. There is a gold-laced Khedive in the midst
of a varigated harem, though it is a libel on the
late respectable Tewfik to accuse him of as many
wives as that. Girls in flower-tinted frocks flut-
ter about the stage like animated roses, tulips and
peonies, amidst a shower of petals, in a gracefully-
designed floral ballet. The “ fire ” ballet consists
of six figures energetically shaking tablecloths in a
lurid glow, and, in the serial scene, Mary Weir
and others hang suspended from the flies like the
little china angels which some folk love to suspend
from their best parlor gas-bracket.
Florrie Young, as principal boy, has many uni-
forms, designed, like most of the other dresses in
Matsa,” by Mr. .Collins-street Barnes, the Aus-
tralian Wingfield in stage-costume designing, who
is leaving for New York shortly. He has the
true instinct for stage effect, and the costumes he
devises always show up well, but while his color
is all right, in form his designs rather need simpli-
fying. Juliet Wray, as an American heiress,
wears a gaudy, spread-eagle kind of frock in blue
and red trimmed with stars-and-stripes and
dollars. In the “ press review,” a girl dressed in
? L ? jETIN cover and a pair of black tights was
the brightest of the row. D’Arcy Stanfield, re-
placing Mrs. Lauri as the neglected Mrs.
M Lonely, wore some black gauze and carrotty
ringlets as a burlesque ancient Egyptian dress,
after the manner of Coleman’s caricature Jap.
costume in “Djin-Djin,” but failed to reproduce
the humor of O’Kiama’s inimitable bloomers.
If Miss Spence, who is standing in S. Australia
as the only woman candidate for the Federal Con-
vention, should get in she will make a good show-
woman for supporters of the female-publicist
movement. An elderly, grey-haired woman with-
out oratorical frills and hysterics, she speaks in
quiet style with shrewd common sense as long as
she is on her own pet topics, and nobody ever
heard her speak on any other. All her travels and
public addresses on her theory of voting have been
er pwq expense. Women have not yet become
enthusiastic enough to part with coin for the pur-
pose of. learning all about the representation of
minorities. _ The worst of the Hare-Spence system
of voting is the long time it takes to count the
votes. I remember a meeting where the lecturess
was enthusiastically recommending her system as
a panacea for all human ills. Its use would pre-
vent bribery and baldness, restore political purity,
the complexion to its youthful color, cure over-
drafts, insolvency, bad legislation, and leprosy,
and generally make this world a little heaven
below. At the end of the meeting there was a
trial ballot conducted by the chairman, a Univer-
sity professor, when it took half-an-hour to count
a couple of hundred votes. '
A heated roomful of seiious-iooking worshippers
met at the Y.M.C.A. Hall on Sunday night to
hear Mrs. Saunders, mother of the two girls
massacred at Ku Cheng, evidently expecting some-
thing sensational in the grim-and-gory line, and
quite prepared to gloat over sickening details.
But the speaker was a mild and motherly old
lady, who merely spoke gently of the iniquity of
horse-racing, sixpenny-nap, whisky, and other
familiar horrors, and never so much as mentioned
an infuriated pig-tail. The chairman, speaking
the address, alluded disappointedly to the
thrilling episodes which their “ dear sister ” could
have told if she had wished, but she had chosen
to give the Gawspil message instead. Then the
choir sang the hymn, “We shall be satisfied by-
and-bye,” while a collection was taken up; but,
judging by the few threepennies in the plate, their
day of contentment must be a long way off as yet.
fund-raising harbor-excursion to Cabarita, on
loth March, with band, moonlight, refreshments,
and all the other adjuncts, has been promoted by
the ladies’ committee of the Boys’ Brigade. Some
of the matrons objected strongly at first to the
expedition going anywhere in particular, and were
in favor of the steamer-load of revellers merely
cruising vaguely around the harbor without
coming to a full-stop. “No landing,” they ob-
jected sternly, “the girls will stray away ” —they
had evidentlybeen there” themselves. But,
after all, the frivolous party carried the day and
a ®u ee u t 0 ave siren give an extra loud hoot to
call the wandering sheep together before starting
on the return journey.
The moral smoke-concerts' held out by'the Rev.
Carr Smith, of St. James’s, Sydney, have been
superseded in favor of moonlight water-parties.
It appears that the womankind of St. James’s
strongly objected to the rev. gentleman mono-
polising their husbands and sweethearts; hence
the new departure. The more “ straight-laced ”
section was much shocked at the billing and coo-
mg of the. young people on the steamer at the
inaugural jaunt; but as there was a corps of
clergy and deacons present—well, Hallelujah !
Autumn and winter shows are made by most of
the drapers now, and from signs millinery matters
are going to be much mixed next season. Hordern
Bros, show hats with flowers, feathers, velvet,
chiffon and buokles all adorning 1 the same crown ;
most of the bonnets are minute, while hats are
large and topply with waving black plumes.
Chapeaux will be tilted still more over the nose,
the hair-knob, which has been climbing higher
and higher up the back of the head, pushing the
millinery forward. Women are revolting now
against savagely stabbing their hats with long
pins: they say that the weapons tear out the hair
and make holes in the straw, and are returning to
ingeniously-concealed elastic. Veils now float
loosely about the face as if they had come adrift
from their moorings, and it is darkly whispered
that it will soon be “common ” to wear the head
in a bag of net, and veils will be “out” altogether.
Recent color-contrasts are striking—red roses and
purple violets trim the same hat, an emerald-green
straw with bright red ribbon bows might have
been built for a supporter of the Cardinal, but
orange mixed with black is just as popular. You
pay your money and you take your choice.
New gown-materials are given some most out-
landish names, but they all amount to this, that
sackcloth in all sorts of glorified forms will be the
correct wear. As “matting” and “checks”
and “canvas,” it appears under many disguises
but always with the same loose texture, which is
such a bad dust-holder that a woman can wear it
for a whole_ day in town without looking like a
perambulating dust-bin, as shedoes in serge orcloth.
For a really high-toned gown there is nothing to
beat a shot-canvas cloth built upon silk, which
rustles opulently when you move. Imitation
rustles are now produced by stiff linen and other
deceptive fabrics, but the only genuine shivering
murmur is given by silk, and can’t be attained
under 10 guineas.
“ The Romance of Corsica” was staged at the
Opera House on Saturday evening, and Monday’s
thin and infrequent audience showed that the
public are not taking any foreign romance at
present. It comes rather stale and irrelevant to
see Jules Simonsen in white cross-gartering hurled
from his love in last-century apparel by assassins
in blue steeple hats and long macintoshes. Miss
Petersen, as the heroine, was stagily-nice in pale-
blue, and got herself up in appropriate white satin
for her wedding ; but the only change in his tog-
gery the bridegroom made was tying his lee's up in
clean white tape. The dresses in general were
new and fresh, and the only drawbacks to the
make-up were the wigs, for which amateurs have
a fatal passion. They will wear them, especially
if there is a scene with powdered hair; conse-
quently they all appear to be suffering from a bad
attack of swelled-head, and years multiply on
them. Some of the music is catchy and flavored
with “I was dreaming ” turned inside-out for
copyright reasons. Two arias, “ For love I fight ”
and “Whisperings,” stand out as being much
above the average, and the words of the second-
named are quaint and fanciful, but there is, on the
whole, too much bandit and big drum in the opera
to make it a success right off the mark.
. Yet more Sydney girls are off to the “ con-
tmong ” to have their musical education trade-
marked by a foreign brand, and, before they go,
are to make the usual farewell appearance.
Yvonne Leverrier, one of the cleverest young
piano-pounders here, with a distinct style of her
own, will play her good-bye to Australia for a
while next Tuesday week at the Town Hall. Con-
stance Evelyn is following in the footsteps of
Crossley, and will be off ’Ome to study ; her final
warble here will occur on 27th March. A new
soprano, Florence Schmidt, has sent out cards for
a vocal trial-trip next Wednesday. A newly-
arrived baritone from Maoriland, who is to make
a public appearance in Sydney this month, has
already been heard with great rapture by feminine
listeners at several sassiety functions. As a set-off
against old-world musician-factories, Prof. Mar-
shall-Hall issues the prospectus of his own har-
mony shop in Melb. for current year.
A remote mining-field in Westralia had its first
funeral lately over a young fellow found exhausted
from want of water in the bush. As there was no
clergyman, the local postmaster was chosen to
read the burial service. The godless collection of
tents had never a prayer-book among them, and
the degenerate citizens were getting desperate, as
there was no time to ride to the nearest township
and back with the necessary ritual. So they tele-
graphed to the place and the burial-service was
sent through per wire and read off the telegraph-
forms as received.
The South Australian Gov. was, the other day,
undergoing the usual loyal presentation of illum-
inated address at a back-blocks township, the
spokesman whereof constantly alluded to His Ex.
as Sir-Thos. -Fowell-Buxton-Bart., ” making the
abbreviation rhyme with cart. The bart. never
smiled —Governors are paid to take little things
like that as part of a day’s work. As to the cor-
rect way of saying “ Fowell ” : it should be made
aristocratically to chime with “Powell,” pro-
nounced “ Pool. ” Sir Thomas Fool-Buxton ! It
is indeed a heavy load.
Girls among the Nicest circles are contemplating
a farewell tea to the departing Lady Hampden
and daughter, and now breathe a sigh of relief at
the return from Hobart of the vice-regal victims,
who, for a time, were thought to be going off from
the southern island in order to shunt loyal
expressions of devotion. It is noticeable that
dear Australian friends” of ex-Grovernment
House contingents get the cold shoulder when
rubbing against the magnates on the other
side of the world. That “distressing cawlonial
familiarity ” doesn’t trouble them much at ’Ome.
Occupations after individuals’ names read oddly,
not only on lists of Convention candidates. The
list of aspirants for membership of Sydney School
of Arts committee last week revealed the entry
“ Name, Mrs. Blank ; occupation, wife of So-and-
so Blank.”
Writes my Melb. correspondent
“ Female Melb., with its usual warm, if short-
lived enthusiasm, is feting and making much of
the ‘ Zenda ’ people. Miss Elliott Page has been
taken up by the Govt. House folk, to whom she
brought ‘letters.’ Miss E.P. is a pretty Ameri-
can with a carefully-disguised accent and a dis-
tinguished manner of wearing her clothes. Miss
Ferrar, helped out by a remarkably becoming red
wig, makes a gracious-looking princess, particu-
larly in the fourth act, when she wears an artistic
white cloth gown with touches of pale-blue. Mr
Julius Knight is the most elegantly-tailored male
member of the co. off the stage. He affects linen
of unusual and vivid hues with ties and ‘ button-
holes to match, and vast enveloping frock coats.
Three additional members of the co. who are to
appear in the next production, ‘The Two Little
Vagabonds,’arrived last week. The two ladies
Misses Terriss and Neilson, are miniature dolls of
women, who look about as smart and fashionable
as if they had newly-arrived from Booligal instead
of that alleged centre of fashion, London.
“The new autumn modes were exhibited at
Flemington on Saturday chiefly on the backs of
the newly-arrived mummers. Melbourne women
with unusual economy, sported for the part
confections which had seen wear before. A
new blue of a rather glaring tone was favored by
the two leading Princess’ ladies. The beauty of
the co. wore a startling skirt of an almost audible
check and a smart roomy whole-back coat. Those
girls looked best who were smartly tailored m
tweeds of the prevailing skimpy cut, and apart
from these Saturday’s gathering was a dowdy
d6mod6 affair, with a distinct flavor of hard-
uppishness in evidence everywhere.
“ The most attractive exhibit at the Cycle Show
is the motor dog-cart which belongs to a patent-
pill propounder. Lady Brassey and Lord Neville
had an exciting sprint in the vehicle last week.
The motor dog-cart differs in appearance in no
wise from the ordinary cart, except that it has
large, heavy-looking wheels and a sort of colonial
oven under the box-seat.
“ Capt. Freeman Thomas and pretty little Mrs.
F. T. (Gov. Brassey’s daughter) go ’Ome for good
in Nov., after the Cup. They will go when the
Governor himself goes for a holiday; but they
won’t come back, and he (probably) will. The
rumour circulated that H.E. will go tg England
tor the June junketings, is, I hear from a direct
source, not true.
“ Guvmentouse people seem determined to
cause trouble by tumbling off their horses. Lady
Hopetoun set the fashion and broke her little nose.
Lord Richard Neville more recently did it, and
broke his collar-bone. Now Gov. Brassey has
followed suit, and damaged both his collar-bone
and his nOse. There is so much grovel at Govt.
House that even the horses are sometimes seized
with an irresistible impulse to fall down and crawl.
“Talking of the recent fashionable Toorak-
Tasmanian alliance, the bridegroom was at one
time universally petted as the beauty-boy of Melb.
society. As years went on he frisked unduly, and
learned how much ‘cold shoulder’ a prodigal is
called upon to swallow. Then one of the revivalist-
fraternity came along and worked a happy change,
with the result that a pacified Providence has now
given the handsome Penitent in marriage to a
highly-connected and rather well-gilt Tasmanienne.
“ Melbourne’s most hospitable hostess and popu-
lar society-leader also upholds the “ blood thicker
than water ” notion. Her gilded halls have ever
been open to the attractive daughters of more or
less impecunious sisters, cousins and aunts, for
whom, given reasonable opportunities, prizes
might be expected in the matrimonial lottery.
With one or two notable exceptions, the damsels
in question have returned to the parental roof-
tree minus lordling or visiting militaire, to score
nearer home. The fame of fashionable doings
and fine connections, in conjunction with their
own pretty faces, has generally helped the re-
turned wanderers aforesaid to the pick of the
baskets in their respective country towns, when
once they had dropped to the salutary doctrine
that half a loaf is better than none.
“ Parson Buchanan, of Brisbane, preaching in
Melb. on his way Londonwards, dispelled the last
vestige of admiration cherished for him in the
hearts of she-churchgoers. He was greatly beloved
of the sex when he first arrived from Baltimore,
but he was younger then, less substantial, more
‘ spiritual ’in appearance. Also, he set a watch
before his mouth and kept the door of his lips
thus guarded against his natural tendency to slip-
shod grammar and construction, misplaced h’s,
and similar atrocities. Now he speaks anyhow,
and his sermons are disjointed things of shreds and
patches. He always affected an occasional
‘joke’; now he goes in for being funny and
familiar, colloquial and autobiographical all the
time. ‘ I like to hear of Christ being weary,’ he
prosed complacently the other Sunday at Dr.
Bevan’s, ‘ ’cos I often feel that way myself.’
“ Fire has providentially driven the respectable,
truly estimable, and godly firm of Beath, Schiess
and Ca out of Manchester, or Flinders-lane, into
Little Collins-street, where arrangements necessi-
tate placing the lady-clerks at the windows amid
their ledgers, day-books, and what not—a most
instructive and inspiriting spectacle. These hand-
some ladies, out of the choicest circles of Goshen,
take not the pens from their lips to gaze through
the plate-glass at the passing flaneur. No;
Tottie tots in oblivion.

there is no demand for a high-class magic and
spells emporium in Melbourne. Even the shilling
fortune-tellers are often driven by necessity to
go forth and do a ‘ day’s washing ’ for a client.

Bendigo Evening Mail refers pathetically
to the “ dead-head campaign ” of Vic. candidates
for the Federal Convention. “With the exS
tion of Dr. Quick and Mr. Simon Fraser, no can
didate has published (at his own expense) even the
briefest statement of his federal policy.” In short
only two candidates have given electioneering
advts. to the hungry Mail. It’s soandalous. *
Tas. democrats are running four candi-
dates for the Federal Convention, and three of
the four have been approved by a joint committee
on which the Executive Council of the province
was represented, not to mention the University
the Chamber of Commerce, and other heavy
bodies. A unique result surely, but one which
indicates that the democrats of the Land of Lots
of Time don’t sleep quite as persistently now as
they have done hitherto.

First Premier Reid said he would not go
to England—it was impossible. Then he said he
would not go unless he was pressed to go by people
and Parliament. Now, he says he will go unless
people and Parliament prevent him going.
As to taking a title: —“ I have come to this con-
clusion-well, it is a pretty open secret that if I
had wished to be Sir George I could have had the
title—(cheers) —but I consider that as the repre-
sentative of New South Wales a plain ‘Mr.’ is
quite good enough.” (Loud cheers.) “Call me
George—plain George.” Alas ! we have all heard
that before !
Prof. Maartens, arbitrator in the Costa
Rica Packet case, has awarded <£3Boo to the
owners, <£3150 to Capt. Carpenter, and .£l6OO to
officers and crew-total, <£8550, as against £25,000
claimed. The amounts bear interest from Nov.,
Jl, and there are £250 costs against the
Hutch Govt. This strikes an outsider as
very fair justice. Capt. Carpenter was on
a whaling cruise with his Sydney - owned
barque in the neighborhood of the Moluccas
m JR and was imprisoned by the Dutch
on a bogus charge for four weeks, during which
tune hu. ship lay idle, and part of the anticipated
profits of the cruise were necessarily lost. For all
this owners, captain, officers, and crew were
reasonably entitled to heavy damages. But it
seems to have struck somebody that there was a
soft scoop in the business, and instead of proceed-
ing with the cruise when Capt. Carpenter was
released the vessel was sold at a sacrifice, and the
cute commercial gents of Sydney twisted G.
Republican Dibbs’ tail and made him roar a de-
mand for £25,000. The result gives the owners a
little more than the vessel’s value—apparently
plus sale-receipts and minus costs; and Capt.
Carpenter, his officers, and crew get a very reason-
able solatium though there must have been a good
(eal of trouble and expense in the interim. On
tne whole, the Costa Rica Packet case gives pretty
poor support to the policy of biting more than
you can chew.
COMMON PRAYER OF THE FEDERATED
PROTESTANT POPES OF N.S.W.
Outside, do keep the Cardinal we pray Thee, Lord
Divine,
And send Thy chosen one instead, with our own
chosen nine.
And, lest the ten’s to Thee unknown (there’s thirty-
nine beside),
We name the “ bunch ” and pray Thou wilt suffi-
cient votes provide ;
Tor well we know, tho’Thou may’st not, Australia
will have hurt
If Cardinal in Convention-room but gets his hat
and skirt.
For he has vowed a secret vow all heretics to burn,
And only to obey his Pope, and our good Queen
to spurn.
And, as “all’s fair in war,” Thou know’st, we’ll
use “ the Devil’s ’’ ropes,
And in our own Conventicles we’ll tyrannise like
Popes.
Big changes are impending in Q. Ministry.
Swelled-head Byrnes talks of accompanying Never-
mind Nelson to Queen Vic.’s unparalleled shivoo,
and Tozer is to be left at home—sitting like a big
spider in the centre of his web, gathering the flies,
Cunning old Groom has just been made chairman
of a commission on the land laws— he’s squared.
Glassey’s eyes are fixed on the dangled portfolio of
Minister for Labor—no harm in him. Philp is tract-
able, and Lilley is too tired of rusting in private
life to boggle about terms. There remains Mac-
donald-Paterson, still buzzing away at large.
Once he is caught Tozer doesn’t care much about
Drake, who has no following, and, besides, is on
the brink of marriage. Everything considered,
matters are coming on nicely. Apropos, some
people whisper that Byrnes’ intimacy with father
ISelson is not unconnected with circumstances of a
domestic nature. In a word, it is averred that
Byrnes has a heart.
THE BLOOD THAT JACK SPILT.
Having heard that the White Knight Fitz-
Gibbon has in hand another poem in honor of
Queen Victoria and pastor Fitchett, a Bulletin
bard, with a view of getting in early, pours out
thus:—
This is the parson lean and long, that wrote the Deed;
both steep and strong 1 ,
That built the yarns of blood and thunder,
That sent the Hosts of Europe under,
That made each tar kill twenty French,
Or a hundred Rooshians at a pinch ;
For the land whose great and glorious Queen
Has sixty years of salary seen,
And made a “ lifer ” everv bean ;
For the Crown that rules'a thousand creeds,
That squared the savage man with beads :
That worried the blacks
With rum and tracts,
That starved poor Pat,
That fed the Fat,
That robbed the poor,
That spilt the gore
That lies at the door
Of Hingland !

“ Rolf Boldrewood’s ” papa, Capt. Sylves-
ter Browne, in 1830 captained the convict transport
Proteus, likewise owned it, and in it brought some
200 convicts to Australia.
Premier Reid has begun to clandestinely
flirt with the bicycle. A Burwood resident found
him the other morning walking beside his machine
and giving sundry evidences of having played the
novice.
Gov. Brassev, when he had his collar-bone
broken and his chest damaged the other day, was
brought down by an Australian-bred horse which
he had just purchased. Omen—a tip for Federa-
tionists ?

one, “my name is Sir .”
