Governor Macgregor, of New Guinea, is
one of the first three suggested for appointment to
Fiji, vice deceased Thurston. Macgregor has
spent nine years at his present post and, taking
him officially, is considered about “as good as
they make ’em.”
John Trezise, the forgotten pioneer ore-
dresser of Broken Hill, died the other day in
retirement in S.A. Trezise 10 years ago (he was
then in the B.H. Prop.’s service) looked upon
jigging as the one possible means of profitably
handling the lower-grade Barrier ores.
Journalist: “I was on a large Australian
paper, and will call the machinist’s name Spiegel-
berg—not a bit like it. The sub-editor always
knew the state of Spiegelberg’s mind by the beats
of the machine. He would say ‘He’s angry,’
‘Sulky,’ ‘ Jolly.’ Then would come a rapid, ner-
vous beat, like flogging a buck-jumper, earth-
quaky, shaking the building. Sub. rushes down-
stairs, yelling ‘ Spieg" berg’s drunk ! ’ ”
One of the char-
acters of Perth in the
pre-mining era was
Tommy Narragut, an
aboriginal. Tommy
was an inveterate
draughts-player, and
would earn many a
big pfeller beer by
“taking down mugs”
who didn’t know his
powers as they talked
largely in different
bars. Another check-
er-board enthusiast
was then Colonial
Secretary Barlee, and
one day when he was
talking draughts a
bystander airily offer-
ed to produce a
blackfellow to beat
him. Barlee laughed
at the audacity of the
thing, and said that
if any nigger was good
enough for him, he
would take his hat off
to him for ever after-
wards. The challen-
ger quickly found
Tommy, and led him
to the Govt, offices.
.£lO a side was put
up, and black and
white took their re-
spective colors and
began. The darkey
carried off three games
running, and Barlee
paid over his tenner
more astonished than
riled. He was as good
as his word, though,
and ever afterwards
when he passed Tom-
my in the street he
would respectfully
doff his hat.
Melb Australasian borrows Phil. May’s
drawings from London Punch, but republishes

ciation with a man who made his name m the

Miss C. H. Spence, the S.A. Convention
candidate, will be 72 this year. Her father was
town clerk of the first Adelaide city council, and
he died more than half- a century ago. Her
brother was a Ministerial colleague ten years since
of Downer, Q.C., Education Minister Cockburn,
and “ Jim ” Howe, all of whom are candidates tor
the present Convention. Miss Spence is the Aus-
tralian apostle of effective voting.
Benj. Hoare, now principal leader-writer
on Melb. Age, was not only once a compositor,
but he for years also edited Geelong Times, a
Freetrade paper. Max Hirsch has unearthed
the amusing fact that it was in one of his leaders
that Hoare first styled the Age the “ Daily
Ananias. ”
An Australian abroad
to The Bulletin : — A strik-
ingly - handsome, magnifi-
cently-muscular man is San-
dow, the modern Samson.
The strongest man in the
world, said by anatomists to
be physically perfect. His
equal in muscular develop-
ment has never existed, un-
less history overlooked the
fact. It is grand to look
upon such a man and know
that he is the perfection of
muscle you might have been
had you but developed a
youthful inclination to sys-
tematically and in order de-
velope each individual muscle
as per Sandow’s simple rules.
One likens him to a Greek
god naturally, though, judg-
ing by the statues of the
Olympian athletes et hoc
genera, Sandow, with his
broad, expansive forehead
from which the short, crisp
hair curls up and away, the
clear, ready eye, and decisive
tone of command, would be
strong enough, clever enough,
and quick enough to “ out ”
half-a-dozen of those sleepy-
eyed, stunted - foreheaded
gladiators of the past. His
chest expands 14in., and
stands out bound, armoured,
and interlaced with muscles
around which sft. of tape
falls 2in. short of meeting.
The biceps, triceps, deltoid,
and trapezoid muscles stand
out on the upper arms and
shoulders prominently like
waves and dunes of sheeny,
pink-flushed marble. The
abdominal muscles at 300 ft.
distance show clearly as the
clean curves on the sea beach
when the tide has ebbed.
With his neck and heels supported, he upholds
four solid-looking men standing upright between
his shoulders and hips. Seated on the haunches
of a horse he swings back from his hips, and
grasping a 1301 b. dumb-bell in both hands
from the ground at the horse’s heels, svvings
upwards to a sitting position, with the bar
above his head, and 150 or 1601 b. of man is
treated in the same way. With his hands he
tears 52 playing cards en masse in halves and
quarters; then 104 and 156 are torn in halves
with but little extra effort. His tour-de-force was
the supporting on his chest of a bridge of 8001 b.
weight, over which a horse dragged a Roman
chariot holding two persons. Sandow would be a
very handy man to have round a bush homestead
to push falling trees off the bark roof, and chip
the jaw of gun-metalled-chinned book-canvassers.

A N.S.W. wool-king, who owns “ whips ”
of city property, and stations in at least three
provinces, and who is allegedly an enthusiastic
Protectionist, was asked, the other day, for a sub-
scription to the National Protection Union. In
response he sent a cheque—for 10s. 6d.
'Bus-boss Carey, chairman of Sydney
Daily Telegraph, is in future to be officially
known as “ President ”of the D.T. Co. At the
shareholders’ meeting at which this proposed in-
novation was assented to a popular lady, who is
one of the chief proprietors, attended and said she
had no objection to the change if it gratified
Mr. Carey’s vanity, but she (practical woman !)
strongly objected to any increase in the fees of
directors.

The Mayor of a certain Australian capital
is scornfully talked about as sure to come in for
knighthood next June. The magnate’s previous
sphere of usefulness is said to suggest punning on
the word “ knighthood.” For that matter, there
are ribald persons who declare that his Worship’s
guests ought not to be asked to banquet it la carte.
A coasting skipper, on the Melb. -Sydney
line, is a quaint old boy capable of assuming a very
pompous air. Occasionally on a hot day he
puzzles passengers at dinner by hailing: “Steward,
stop the punkah ! I am about to use the pepper.”
Then : “ Thank you, steward. I have used the
pepper—you may again allow the punkah to con-
tinue cooling the room.”
Kingston is the only lawyer on the
Democratic side in S.A. Assembly.
Dunedin Fish is a fine mob-orator, and
when he meets a hostile audience (which
is often), if he cannot beat it with argu-
ments he does so by brute force. Once, in a
suburban drill-shed, he addressed an awful
meeting which had sworn to “flatten him
out.” At the back of the hall, it was whis-
pered, were a dozen police and a swift horse.
Fish began—“ Mr. Chairin’’—when Sheol
broke loose, and the hall became dark with
the rain of dead cats, stale pigfish, and
mouldy eggs. When the rain ceased and the
gaslight struggled through the awful stink,
the chairman and supporters were gone, and
Fish, streaming with decomposed custard,
struggled from under the heap of mortality,
and recommenced his speech, only to be
stopped by howls, cat-calls, cats, and farm-
yard noises. Fish waited, and in about an
hour, when the audience had exhausted its voice
and ammunition, he rose above the wreck and
waded in. At 9.30 the audience began to feel
sorry for itself, and at 10.30 it voted the
speaker a jolly good fellow, and passed a
unanimous vote of confidence with the
scanty remains of its larynx. Then Fish
went home dirty in body, ruined in clothes,
but in soul triumphant.
Rev. E. G. Moberly goes from an incum-
bency in the'North (N.S.W.) to a chaplaincy in
Suva, Fiji. Moberly was a well-known figure on
most of the Q. goldfields about 30 years ago, but
somehow never had the worldly knack of collect-
ing stipends. He was starved out of town after
town, and when he shifted to N.S. Wales the old
curse folio wed him. He is scholarly and liberal,
and is a brother of the classical Bishop'Moberly,
of English University life. He has probably
married more bush couples and baptised more
bush children than any other parson.
A legal correspondent:—
Dear Bulletin, —Re Judge Windeyer and the Macleod
bigamy .appeal: Windeyer’s judgment wasn’t upheld
by the 1 ' Privy Council. The Councillors decided
the case on an utterly rotten point taken by
themselves, and didn’t even enter upon the con-
sideration of the question on which Windeyer and
the other Judges gave their judgment. So far as
that is concerned, Windeyer’s judgment still stands as a
dissent from the majority of the Court, and if the point
arose again it is the judgment of the majority which
would be binding. All the same, I think the men who
know most about it consider Windeyer’s judgment good
law. But it isn’t the law of N.S.W. until the Privy
Council says so on some future appeal.
A N.S.W. barrister always exhibits a con-
siderable degree of temper if questioned concern
ing the Great Seal of England. This is the reason,:
In his ’Varsity days he was one of the “ greenest’
youths that ever attended a temple of learning.
When he entered the Law School he was told,
and seriously believed, that the Great Seal 61
England was kept in a tank, and that the Keepei
of the Seal was the man who fed it every day.
A man, reputed from his correspondence
to be a millionaire, has been negotiating for
months past to buy most of the big properties in
Riverina. Several squatters and others went to
the expense of having their places valued and set
out in detailed description, believing trade immin-
ent. But the “buyer ” turned out to be a penni-
less lunatic, with the gift of a deft pen and royal
mind, who was a couple of weeks ago sent off to a
madhouse.
It seems to be regarded as pretty certain
that had Premier Turner been a bachelor he
would neither have bitten at the knightly hook
nor accepted the Chamberlain invite. Turner
risks shaking up his private practice by leaving
Vic. even for three months, as his clients are used
to his personal attention. He works in a dirty
little office at the top of a smellful staircase. The
plaster shows through the shabby paper where he
sits back in his broken-armed chair. When at
work he is always coatless.
At the conclusion of the case for the
Crown in a trial held at Sydney Central Criminal
Court last week, Mr. Wise, who defended accused,
rose and remarked that perhaps the jury did not
wish to hear any address. Mr. Justice Simpson,
the presiding Judge, said that it was not the cor-
rect thing for counsel to interfere in that way.
Mr. Wise replied that he had done the same thing
before Sir James Martin, who approved of the
course. Then his Honor : “No doubt, Mr. Wise,
you have done many remarkable things in your
time.” The prisoner was convicted.
Premier Seddon, while in Adelaide, made
a pilgrimage to the grave of the only child born of
George Grey’s marriage, which died more than
half a century ago. The mother was a daughter
of Admiral Sir R. W. Spencer, who in 1839, at the
time of the wedding, was Govt. Resident at King
George’s Sound. Strange to say, Brookes, super-
intendent of the cemetery, who pointed out the
grave to Grey on his visit to Adelaide in
1891 and to Mr. Seddon, was an official of the
Supreme Court when Grey was Governor of S.A.
from 1841 to 1845.
r A far Northern Bulletin correspondent
thus obituarises Christie Palmerston: —
Died the other day at the Straits Settlements Christie
Palmerston, perhaps the best bushman in the Northern
Territory, and one of the most successful of early prospec-
tors. Was on the Hodgkinson in the early days, and was
the first to blaze a track from Mourilyan to Geraldton.
In the early eighties Christie prospected the Daintree, and
became blind there through incautiously handling
poisoned spears and afterwards rubbing his eyes. Guided
by his blackboy, Palmerston regained civilisation and
ultimately his eyesight also. Had wonderful tact with
the blacks, with whom he hardly ever quarrelled ; was an
unfailing revolver shot, and never meddled in the conju-
gal concerns of aboriginals. Nothing pleased him better
than to ride into Herberton bootless, hatless, and _in
rags, with a troop of naked myalls at his heels. Married
late in life but could not pin himself down to civilisation;
visited the Raub, Borneo, and ultimately the Straits, where
he died
C. CHIN, UNDERTAKER

“ ARE UNDERTAKERS HUMOROUS?”
(A suburban sketch.)
The old-Jack-Robertson memorial fund,
which will probably be devoted to establishing
a Sydney University bursary, now amounts
to <£97o odd. The Robertson monument fund
amounts to about .£lll, and it is to be raised to
<£2oo.
George Ash, of S.A., the first Federal
candidate to die, passed away with his last libel-
action unfinished. It was no fault of his, for the
fighting solicitor shoved it along to the very la;st.
Ash was aged 38 —the age at which most journa-
lists seem to die. He had some exciting experi-
ences in the Kaffir war. Then he came to S.A.,
and was an editor for ten years till a huge slander-
suit ruined him ; and finally he became a lawyer
and a politician, and the business partner of his
political enemy, Premier Kingston. • He waS a
successful, though ineffably dreary advocate, with
an elocutionary style that was dust and ashes ; he
was a debater of insufferable length ; and he
afflicted with a Toryism that was past all remedy.
Ash died mainly through trying to work 20 hofirs
per diem. Another theory is that he did work
20 hours per diem without serious injury, and
that it was an attempt to extend his working day
to 22 hours which killed him.

POLITICAL
POINTS
“More Popery than Fiscal”—the Con-
vention election in N.S.W.
Some British Fatman now refers to them
spitefully as the “Coloanies.”
Present state of the Federation game in
N.S.W. :—Yellow on red, player green.
It will surprise The Bulletin if “ Toby ”
Barton fails to top the Convention poll in N.S.W.
Duncan Gillies : “I am in the hands of
the people.” Does he begin to feel the pressure on
his neck ?
It has come at last. Relief works have
been started at Coolgardie to save the unemployed
from starvation. .
Nicholas Fitzgerald’s list of Vic.’s Federal
certainties: Turner, Peacock, Sargood, Deakin,
Trenwith. To this list Murray Smith adds Fitz.
himself.
By working the free-postage dodge, more
than one N.S.W. Convention candidate has man-
aged to send an election-circular in the guise of a
newspaper to every elector in the province. N.S.
Wales gives free postage to newspapers—but
“ cannot afford ” (vide S.M. Herald) to concede
free education.
CELEBRITIES.
REVEREND ESCAPEE FROM PALESTINE (introducing his lecture):
“ With your permission, I shall now say a few words about
Jerusalem. ”
A VOICE ( devoutly) : “ Thank Heaven! We have at last hit upon an
original subject, anyway! ”
Population of Thursday Island (Q.) :
Europeans, 362 ; Polynesians, 156 ; Chinese, 61;
Japanese, 179 ; Manilla-men, 207 ; Javanese, 16 ;
Coolies, 11; Mexicans, 2 ; African negroes, 11;
Cingalese, 32; aboriginals, 203; heterogeneous
nondescripts, 28.
Softgoods Sargood’s business connection
gives him a canvasser in every Vic. town. He
has practically a better organisation than any
other candidate.
Premier Reid, if he goes to London, will
re-organise the N.S.W. Agency-General, and pro-
vide for the immediate retirement of Saul Samuel
—who will soon be 77.
H. : “ O’Shanassy said 30 years ago that
directly Australian Federation is on the cards in a
practical way Imperial Federation will be drawn
as a red-herring acrossthe trail.”
Vic. : “ The first thing every candidate for
the Federal Convention does is to declare vehe-
mently, and almost ferociously, that he never will
‘desert Mrs. Micawber! It almost looks like
Who’s afraid ? ’ ”
N. : “ Gillies was absurdly out of touch in
handling Federation, at his meeting in Melb.
Athenaeum. He had come red-hot off the steamer,
gobbled up morsels from M‘lntyre, Murray Smith,
and Sargood, and mixed a haggis.”
An electoral law-point of grave interest to
certain N.S. Wales Parliamentary candidates :
Half-castes, whose fathers are unnaturalised
aliens, are disqualified for the franchise. The full-
blooded black has a vote, but the half-caste is in
the peril stated.
Britishah : “I claim that my beloved
Hindoo fellow-subjects of Her Gracious Majesty
the Empress—whom God assoilise !—must be
allowed votes for the Federal Convention. Nay,
I would propose Tarboosh Abdallah, the man-im-
porter, as a candidate.”
Now that Vic. Premier Turner has become
a knight and has decided to go to England and
grovel, it is about time Isaacs and Peacock tossed
up for the reversion of the Premiership. Turner
can't go on this wild career long without leaving a
vacancy, and Isaac A. Isaacs ought to be his suc-
cessor when the vacancy comes. The Chosen
Race has been waiting a very long time for its
turn at the helm in Australia.
The influx -of - aliens
question from a Chow cab-
bage-grower’s point of view :
—“No more my countlymen
come, cabbagee thlippence !
All li’! welly good! Plenty
more my countlymen come
—cabbagee one penny!
No good, cabbagee one
penny ; no good ! ”
At a Melb. Federa-
tion meeting. Voice from
the body of the hall : “ We
will not vote for the candi-
date!” Candidate:
“ Why ? ” “ Because you
are an enemy of the
people.” “ But are you
not taught on the highest
authority to love your
enemies?” “Yes, but not
to trust them.”
Cairns (N.Q.) Argus
wears sackcloth because
Kanaka boys who only got
10s. a week in Geraldton,
Mackay, or Bundaberg are
rushing Cairns district and
asking £1 to £1 ss. per
week ; and says : ‘ ‘ As there
is no organisation among the
farmers here to fix the rates
of pay, they readily get
w hat they ask for. It is
time some step was taken
to fix the maximum pay
of South Sea Islanders.”
That is the difference bet
ween White Australia and
Leperland. The former is
worrying for a minimum
wage for whites, and the
latter about a maximum
wage for niggers.
J. T. Walker, of Syd-
ney, says Premier Nelson
told him “it was the young
Australian Native party in
Q. Assembly that opposed
the northern province join-
ing the Federal Conven-
tion.” This is of a piece
with the impudent declara-
tions of discredited Q. dele-
gates to the Federal Coun-
cil that “Queensland would
not have ” this or that. As
Brisb. Courier points out,
Queensland’s opinion has
never been asked, and Govt,
has carefully shirked asking
it. The only “young Aus-
tralian” in Q. Assembly
who deliberately opposes
Federation is Nelson’s pro-
tege, Byrnes, the apostle of
Tory reaction.
A few of the N.S.W. towns which claim
to have special advantages entitling them to be
the Federal Capital: Armidale, Albury, Bathurst,
Eugowra, Glen Innes, Goulburn, Hornsby, Kiama,
Liverpool, Newcastle, and Sydney.
The Westralian Government is wise in its
generation. By standing out of the Federal move-
ment till the last moment, and then coming in
with a rush, it leaves no time for objectionable
people to hold meetings, and draw up platforms,
and stump the country, and form democratic
organisations. There isn’t even time to find out
the views of candidates, and the ten best-known
men will get in without having to say anything in
particular about their intentions. In fact, W.A.
in the skurry has to “ go it blind.”
The Syrian sore ! Eighty-five more appli-
cations were received from Syrians and Indians
for hawkers’ licenses at Redfern (Sydn y)
last week. The D.S.M. granted all save four.
Quite recently over 200 additional licenses were
granted in the same court to members of the alien
horde. Nearly 300 in about a month !
The illustrious Mr. R. D. Meagher had
the front to bob up again politically—at Bremier
Reid’s Convention meeting the other night,
where he got a hot reception—and now his press
barrackers protest that it is cowardly to kick a
man when he’s down.” If Mr. Meagher is unwil-
ling to be metaphorically kicked let him stay
down. Dean stays down.
Population of
the Lower Bur-
dekin (Q.), 1500,
of whom 800 are
colored. Of the
population work-
ing on the fields
nearly 90 per
cent, are colored.
In one half-hour
the writer count-
ed half-a-dozen
different undesir-
able nationalities
in Flinders - st.
Townsville, to
wit : Italians,
Chinese, Japs,
Kanakas, Cinga-
lese, and Javan-
ese.
The Bogan
scrub-cutting, it
is believed, won’t
be worth any-
thing to N.S.W.,
after all—except
as a perpetual loss
in the shape of
interest on the
borrowed money.
Those who know
the country well
say it was origi-
nally timbered
with large trees
of yellow box,
pines and other
woods. Ring-
barking destroy-
ed these, but the
seed from the
large pines find-
ing a free outlet
sprang up in
thousands. This
is the scrub now
being cut down
by pauper-labor.
The land is still
full of seeds, and
when the place is
bare once more
another growth of
pines will take place. Already in the places first
cleared indications of this are noticeable.
The present Federal campaign isn’t many
weeks old, and already one candidate has retired,
one is dead, one has been oommitted for trial
and fined .£5O for contempt of court, and one,
at time of writing, was dangerously ill. None
of the four was on The Bulletin’s Federal ticket.
This paper begins to think that its 40 candidates
are the only healthy ones in the crowd.
Yic. : “Deakin has been reminding his
electors that his Parliamentary career extends over
18 years. An elaboration of how not to do it.
Another example of a statesman who thinks he
can govern with the fag-end of his mind. Deakin
and Shiels, rather superior Wrixons, with their
verbal magazine-articles get the reputation of
Barnave and Vergniaud in the French Revolution.”
Downes Carter, Treasurer of the Patterson
Govt., who sustained confidence by haunting
Colhns-street with a drawn smile, was put up to
do all the gushing over the recently-returned
Gillies, and he prated a great deal about Duncan’s
“ cool head.” Whereat David Syme : “ But cool-
ness is a characteristic which the public only asso-
ciates with the cheek of Mr. Gillies.” In point of
fact, the head of Gillies would appear to have
been reduced to the temperature of his heart.
One of the latest wild-cat political railways
is that which is being constructed from Hughenden
(N.Q.) to a point 35 miles in the direction of
Winton. Afton Downs and one or two other
squattages will benefit by the line, but nothing
else. Where the line is going to, eventually, no
one seems to know. If Winton is the objective
point, then the route takes a curve southwards
and looks fairly like jerrymandering. And just
won’t there be a row between the interests along
the Central line from Rockhampton, which line is
also aiming at an extension to Winton !
One of the humors of the Federal election
in N.S.W. is the candidature of John Bridge, of
wool fame. He has published one short address,
the only lucid statement in it being that because
his firm is well known his customers ought to vote
for him. He has not spoken anywhere near
Sydney or suburbs, but wanders around back-
block towns on the Northern rivers—where doubt-
less he thinks his audience will not be too critical.
His brand of Federation embraces military, tele-
graphs, and law purposes only; but his speeches
are mostly in favor of Protection, and agm the
Government.
It is expected that the four S.A. Ministers
who are standing for the Federal Convention
(Kingston, Holder, Cockburn, and O Loghlin) will
be among the front half-dozen when the contest is
over. Solomon, who aspires to the leadership of
the Opposition, is spending more than all the
other Federation candidates put together.
The admission of Sargood into The Bul-
letin’s Convention “ bunch ’ was admittedly a
sop to Cerberus, but the inclusion of Fraser and
Zeal in the Age “bunch ’’ can only be put down
to the personal interposition of Dawvid Syme.
Fraser is Scotch, ye ken. Moreover, Zeal was a
witness for Dawvid in Space v. Time, a legal
action of the stone age.
PROMISING FOR DE BROWN.
DAUGHTER: “So you do consent to my marrying Mr. De Brown ; I thought you
didn’t like him.”
MOTHER: “I don’t; hut I should like to he his mother-in-law!”
As in State functions the Speaker and the
President of the Council precede all Ministers,
some hair-splitting constitutionalists argue that
Chamberlain erred in passing over these officials
and inviting mere temporary Premiers.
Card. Moran’s address— had plenty
of time for the preparation of it—was perhaps the
most disappointing and humiliating step in connec-
tion with his candklature-sofar. Helucidly explains
his idea of an Australian commonwealth : “ That
each state shall continue to enjoy the most perfect
freedom of action consistent with the well-being
of the Commonwealth, while at the Same time the
Federal Government shall be invested with full
authority to weld all the states into a strong, united
Australia.” In other words, “ I believe in travel-
ling rapidly at a moderate pace so far as is con-
sistent with due velocity, yet without giving way
to what might be called excessive speed, and I
shall remain stationary with reasonable rapidity
while endeavoring to get over the ground at the
rate of a certain distance in a time to be hereafter
decided without committing myself,”
THOSE PREMIERS.
They might have stood to fashion here
A nation new and grandly free,
Have made their simple names how dear
To generations yet to be !
But, no, each servile spirit craves
To hasten to a land afar,
Where they may posture as the slaves
Behind a Queen’s triumphal car.
DANGERS OF DELAY.
A Flagman upon the railway delayed—the express
rushed on, and a score of lives were lost.
A nurse girl neglected the child in the park, and
the littls one was drowned.
A father was so busy he had no time to think of
himself, and so common symptoms ran into a
fatal disease.
A mother was so absorbed by her children that she
did not notice her own danger until it was too
late.
A million people neglect and delay, when, if they
only realised, their whole happiness and very
lives are at stake.
A pain in the back, Lassitude Melancholy Faint-
ness, Irritability, Prostration, all
serious kidney difficulty.
A headache, Coated Tongue, Irregularities, Cold
Feet, Weak Pulse, Constipation, unmistak-
ably prove deranged kidneys or liver.
A delay or neglect at such a time may mean
everything—may mean life or death.
A prompt use of the only standard remedy for
such troubles, Warner’s Safe Cure, is the part
of wisdom.
“A word to the wise is sufficient.” ‘Are you one
who can accept a timely suggestion, and hence
avoid the
Consequences of Neglect?

SPORTING NOTIONS .
Last year The Bulletin selected the New-
market Handicap winner (Maluma) in one try ; the guess
this year was pegged back to second place. Wait-a-bit
ran among the leading three horses from start to finish,
but when it came to a final dash chestnut Carlton fairly
beat the thicker-set colt from Ballarat. Twenty-seven
horses started for the Newmarket. Prom a batch of
eight “ fancies” named in this column, six finished among
the first ten —Wait-a-bit, Coil, So-and-So, Ayrshire, Sun-
burv and Reka. Also Carlton was picked as one of a
little lot of “ probable winners during the V.R.C. meet-
ing.” .
A decided “tip ” for Carlton could be gleaned
from his appearance at the post. Tom Hales, greatly dis-
tressed by the death of his wife the other day, would
have withdrawn Carlton right away but for the fact that
a lot of stable money had been invested on what was
thought too good a thing to scratch under any circum-
stances. Splendid chances are not generally sacrificed to
sentiment on the Turf, or anywhere else. Hales gave the
horse its “chance of a lifetime,” and won a big stake.
“ Unlucky in love, lucky in play.”
The story of the Leger surprise is soon told.
Newhaven wasn’t in first-class fettle ; Resolute, although
pretty sound on his feet, seemed rather short of work.
The Officer had been doing well, and appeared likely to
win if he were not bustled. W. R. Wilson and manager
Macdonald made a careful inspection of Sam Cook’s
champion before the race, and came to the conclusion,
presumably, that one or other of the two outsiders could
beat the favorite in a fast-run race. Resolute took New-
haven along at a killing pace, whilst The Officer lay off
within easy distance of the pair, until required to make
his effort. Then he rushed by them, and the race was
won.
The Officer’s Leger-time was Gfsec. faster than
the previous best on record, and about 53sec. better than
the average time for ten years past.
None of the Flemington touts uttered a warning
note re Newhaven’s condition. The matter with the Cup-
winner just now is a sore place on the “ elbow,” and
thereabouts, of his near fore leg, which he moved very
stiffly, not to say lamely, after the Leger gallop. Such
a nasty and noticeable sore that several lady critics as-
sumed an air of gentle sympathy and said “ poor thing ! ”
Before this week is out, the V.R.C. stewards
will possibly find occasion to cast their eyes back upon
the betting for the Essendon Stakes, last Saturday.
Fadladeen and Bloodshot, then favorites for the Aus-
tralian Cup, ran very much “unfancied” in the weight-
for-age race. Bloodshot was meeting Coil on 101 b. better
terms than their Australian Cup handicap, yet Coil started
at 3 to 1, whilst Bloodshot had no stable-backers at 10’s.
Little things like this set punters thinking. Bloodshot,
carrying Bst. 121 b., ran second for Melb. Cup, and was
now being backed at short odds to win the Australian
Cup under 9st. 91b. Yet he was a knocked-out outsider
for the Essendon Stakes, in which he had to carry 9st.
only.
A good deal of the joekeyship at Flemington
last Saturday was painful, but Morrison, on Manfred, in
the Hopeful Stakes, gave about the most excruciating
performance of the day. Morrison “ rode his mount for
all he was worth,” as horse-reporters put it. That is to
say, the boy sat askew on Manfred, just holding the reins
in his left hand and walloping monotonously at Manfred’s
quarter with his right. Meantime, little Redfearn, on his
“blind” side, was peacefully guiding Waraka to the
easiest of victories.
The Sydney horse, Valiant, lost a little ground
in Newmarket Handicap after the public had taken a
“ liker ”at him in the bird-cage. A fine, nuggetty
animal, he would be generally picked as a stayer rather
than a sprinter, and for that matter Wait-a-bit also has
the cut of a long-distance racer. Three of the speediest-
looking nags in the crowd were Ayrshire, Carlton and
Coil, the two latter being nearly related by the blood of
good old Chester.
Cydnus, as The Bulletin had suggested, was a
goner for the Newmarket, The St. Albans stable, after
threatening to be very dangerous in the race, fell back
upon Pilatus, who seemed out of sorts, and was not
backed for the proverbial shilling.
* * * *
The Bulletin waits with breathless interest for
cable-news that another Carbine foal has burst upon the
scene.
* * * *
Gollan’s Norton, who seems likely to start a
warm favorite for the Liverpool Grand National, is an
exceptionally big horse, but a very moderate performer
in England so far, judging from his handicap weight.
How long is it since Norton went ’Ome? The writer re-
members him making a farewell appearance at Fleming-
ton when the Hopetouns were about, and they have been
gone nearly two years.
As regards Daimio and his wasted appearance
when he ran at Lingfield (Eng.) recently, the question is
raised—“ Will a nine-year-old horse, who has gone light
on change of climate, ever regain his old form under the
new auspices ? ”
The steady decline in the stability of Ikey Mo
is quite explained by betting-market reports. Thus—
“ For the Newmarket Handicap Valiant finned to 8 to 1
after 10 to 1 had been taken for £5OO, which was taken to
£5O about Cydnus, who came with a rattle again.” An
investment of £5O on either horse shortened their prices
by two points and six points respectively ! And yet the
awe-stricken horse-reporters continue to prattle about big
bookmakers and leviathans of the ring 1
• * * *
A symmetrical yearling to be placed under
the hammer next Easter is a filly by Niagara out of old
La Belle, the dam of Marvel. As far as looks go this
filly is “ the show of the shop ” at Hobartville.
Marvel is now located at Hobartville, where he
has been doing stud duty. The old black horse is as
30und as when a four-year-old, and looks as if he had
only just come out of work.

Rumored that the Kensington Club, Sydney,
intends trying its luck, ponies v. horse-meetings, on
Saturday afternoons.
* * * *
The carnival of N.S.W. Wheelmen’s League,
on Sydney Cricket Ground, is a big sporting event with
many aspects. As a speculation on the League’s part it
will either make or break that squabbling institution.
Ever since the great Zimmermann commencement, when
the League scooped close on £2OOO, it has had to draw on its
funds to pay for the meetings which have been failures.
The League now makes a supreme effort for patronage by
offering the public the unrivalled accommodation of the
Cricket Ground, and the best collection of cycling talent
yet gathered together in Australia. All circumstances
augur a big success, but should disaster come there is
nothing left but for the League to bury itself, and for the
name of Gen. Sec. Hunter to be shorn of the fine, large
Christian name of Percy.
This meeting comes as a boon to the trade.
Jan. and Feb. are dead slow months with the industry of
wheels. The carnival will give stimulus to the public s
cooling enthusiasm. The last few weeks the cycling
firm’s manager has had nothing to do but sit in his office
and grow bald through incessant staring at the pictures
of French girls a-wheel pasted round the walls.
The League’s meeting has an aspect for the
Sydney press. The S.M. Herald and Daily Telegraph
are laying themselves out for the advertising boom which
is already manifesting. The D.T. publishes the photos, of
people who were never heard of outside the little circle of
a score of ultra cranks, and the S.M.H. shows equal readi-
ness to bow down to the wheel-god. The recent leading
articles on Percy Hunter’s proposal to make Sydney P-O.
clock-face a poster-hoarding for the League’s advts. were
done as solemnly as if the pushing young man were a
leading spirit in Federation. If the editors of the dailies
take Percy Hunter with such awful seriousness, his next
move will be to offer editors Curnow and Brient £2OO
each for the vacant space upon their shirt-fronts.
» * * *
Only one or two of the Melb. cycle agencies have
risen to the occasion at the cycle-show in the Exhibition
Building, and the majority of the stands consist merely
of several rows of bicycles on a raised dais ; with no points
to distinguish the exhibit from the ordinary every-day
shop-window. The advertising of the races in connection
with the show was a triumph of imbecility. As a result, the
attendance was poor in the afternoon, and only mediocre
at night, whereas, if properly announced, Bilmartin and
Porta’s names would have been good enough for a 10,000
crowd. Martin followed up his Adelaide successes by
collaring the £25 for the First-Class Mile after a desper-
ate battle with Don Walker all the way up the straight.
Walker has done practically nothing since he beat Zim-
mermann, but as he ran Bill to a few inches on Saturday
night his form must be recovering. Megson is another
man who showed one of his old-time sprints in the. heat
of the Five Mile Scratch, when he beat'Porta badly in the
run home. Martin won his heat of the classic event as
he liked, and as Ken Lewis has also qualified for the final
next Saturday the meeting of these two will be looked
forward to with interest. The Bulletin wonders
whether but on second thoughts The Bulletin
won’t wonder. The second and third-class handi-
cap final was a succession of crosses, bores, and
jostles from start to finish, and the surprise is that only
one man, Peryman, was brought down. A number of
protests have been directed against Symonds, and that
gentleman will probably be warned from his wobblesome
habits. A few words to Berry (the son of the Speaker, as
the Argus always has it) on the subject of passing on the
inside would also not be out of place. The races were
very nearly not coming off at all, for with the resignation
of the officials who acted at the Martin-Lewis match, the
L.V.W. has nobody left willing and competent to conduct
the sport.
* * * *
Martin’s Adelaide feat of putting up a world’s
record for a mile (unpaced) of 2min. 2sec. is all the more
meritorious when it is considered that the thermometer
showed 102 in the shade.
* * * *
The inner history of the reinstatement during
late years of one or two disqualified athletes would be
interesting. Such things have been worked in this way :
a man disqualified for alleged crookedness applies for re-
admission and threatens slander actions against all who
asperse him. The final deliberations are private, but the
applicant takes his job to a lawyer, who happens to be a
member of the disqualifying committee or council.
Having no legal privilege, everybody on the committee is
meek as milk, and the disqualified person crawls back to
the racing-path “without a stain.”
» * « «
Dear Bulletin,—Concerning bowler Roche, I
fancy the loss of index and second fingers and part of
thumb must be just what a bowler wants. The break
must be put on from above or below, just as a screw in
billiards. Now, the depointed thumb can work the one
side and the tip of the little finger the other, from the
bottom, whilst the third finger can do top work. All this
while the “ vis viva ” can be given from the hollow of the
hand. Whole men must lose force by attending too much
to the tips of the digits. That’s my idea. Not worth
much, perhaps, and neither is— Scotty the Wrinkler.
* * » *
The meeting to raise funds to send the promis-
ing young Hunter River sculler, George Towns, to Eng-
land is to be held directly after the Federal elections. If
successful at ’Ome, Towns will visit Canada and pull
Gaudaur.
* * • *
“ Penny-Weight Sports ” were held at Pindinni,
W.A., lately. The events included the “Eucalyp-
tus Pannikin,” a “ Budgeree-fellow Bun Struggle,”
“ Dusky Virgin Plate ” (gins’ race in currajong-leaf cos-
tume), “Tin Dog trot,” “ Picanniny Tuckout,” “Never
Never Cup,” “ North Coolgardie District Scorcher,” and
“ Baal-gammon Hop.” Prizes consisted, in addition to
cash, of “ tin-dog,” preserved potatoes, tea and sugar,
jam, tinned milk, colored shirts, socks, handkerchiefs, &c.
* * » *
Whenever Charles Godfrey trills his descriptive
song “ Dreams,” at Melb. Opera House, he introduces the
name of a fat metallician, and the most amusing sight of
the evening is to see that booky enter the stage-box just
as the song starts and beam on the staring multitude as
Godfrey yells out his monniker. Who can maintain that
woman is more vain than man ?
* * * *
Monsieur Lesna, with his Manager of Finance,
his Master of Technique, his valet, his French cook, his
quintette, his quads, his triplets, his baggage, his sundries
and his fal-lals, arrived in Sydney on Tuesday last, and
was given a big reception by N.S.W. Wheelmen’s League.
The League, as a body, doesn’t often get out on the bust,
but when it does it has a very magnificent birthday
indeed. And Tuesday’s affair was the gayest razzle that
the League has yet put in.
* * * *
The V.R.C. St. Leger has for the past five
years been a race of surprises, such champions as
Camoola, Carnage, The Harvester, Wallace, and New-
haven being successively defeated. Excuses of various
kinds have in each case been offered, but it is passing
strange that Ikey Mo and co. seem to have anticipated
the result every time.
* * * *
The Queensland horse, Battalion, scored his
second win in N.S.W. on Saturday last, when he carried
9st. 51b. to victory in Rosehill Hep. Though not con-
sidered at the very top of the tree in his native land, he
is far and away the best horse that has crossed the border
for some years past.
• * * *
The Yic.-S.A. cricket-match resulted in an easy
victory for S.A. and Luck by an innings and 70. Vic.
went in first on a good wicket and retired for 279, with
Harry Trott’s 61 as the leading score. S.A.’s first innings
started with a fine exhibition of smiting. The fieldsmen
hadn’t done panting after Darling’s threes when they
were called on to chase Lyons’ fours, and when the two
mighty hitters were disposed of Clem. Hill took up their
parable vigorously.
Stumps were drawn on Saturday to the figure of
343 for three wickets. On Sunday rain came to help the
bowlers, and Monday saw the rest of the side go
-down for added 84 only. But 427 was more than
enough to settle the Victorians. Between a rotten
wicket and Carraher, who took 5 wickets for 24
runs, they melted like Monaro snow under the sun
of September. Jones, too, gave his demoniac aid to
the tune of 4 for 28. There was a monotonous and
draggle-tailed procession from the pavilion to the crease
and back again, and the small boys’ sarcasms drew no
flash from the spiritless victims. “ Victoria ” spelt crush-
ing defeat.

Townsville (Q.) Star offers “ a prize of £1
Is.” to the “ competitor who suggests the punish-
ment most fitting for the crimes ” supposed to
have been committed by Butler, the man arrested
in ’Frisco. This, because “hanging seems too
easy a death for a fiend of the Butler stamp.” ! ! !
The Townsville Star out-Butlers Butler. He (if
it was he) murdered with a prospect of gold in
sight. The Star isn’t game to commit murder
itself, isn’t game to suggest murder itself ; but it
offers a prize in order to induce others to suggest
murder. It hasn’t even the common honesty to hire
people to suggest murder, but hopes to cheat others
out of JllO-worth of “copy’’for the chance of a
guinea. Thelegalasphyxiation, or spine-cracking, of
a malefactor is none the less murder, when accom-
panied by deliberate blood-licking cruelty. What
is the Star’s proposal but a Raffle for Murder by
Torture? Entrance fee, fiendishness; prize, a
Kanaka’s drunk. Add this, please, to the argu-
ments for the abolition of capital punishment.
For, if these moods be the effect of Gallows on
the supposedly sane and uncriminal mind, what
sort of hardening will Gallows not produce on the
criminal temperament ? Capital punishment is
the heaviest curse that civilisation has attempted
to disguise as a necessity. It confesses failure,
and chains humanity to despair.
A wedding in the Westralian wilds is a
great social event. A Victorian who went over
there with his three unmarried daughters recently
gave the last of the trio away in holy matrimony
to a publican. The bridegroom kept open house
in honor of the occasion, and the whole neighbour-
hood got on such a woeful burst that seven drunks
were found in the bridal apartment when the
bride wanted to retire. They were carried out
and left in a row in the back yard. Then it was
discovered that the bridegroom was missing, and
the most vigorous search among the drunks failed
to bring him to light; the general theory was
that he had got sunstroke and wandered away
into the bush, and the widowed bride wept all
night. Next morning the servant girl gave notice
on the ground that a strange inebriate had taken
possession of her room during the night and she
had been forced to take refuge under the house.
It proved to be the bridegroom, and when he was [
awakened by an energetic process of dry-blowing
he apologised handsomely to the girl and made
things all right. Then the best man was rescued
out of the manger in the stable, and the happy
party sat down to breakfast. After that they
started on their honeymoon in a buggy with the
temperature at 116, but one of the horses dying of
sunstroke they returned on top of a passing ox-
conveyance and decided to dispense with the pro-
posed tour and settle down without ceremony.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page

SUCCESSFUL CAREER.
MARVELLOUS TESTIMONIALS.
FLATTERING PRESS OPINIONS.
- - - MIRACULOUS CURES.
A few words as to the progress and growth in
public favour of the Registered Alfahne Herbal
Remedies would not be out of place here, and
would be additional evidence as to their real
genuineness and great virtues as claimed for them
by Herr Rassmussen, their discoverer and sole
proprietor.
The great virtues of the Alfaline Herbal Reme-
dies are so well known that it is quite unnecessary
to say much in regard to them in this notice ; the
very fact of their having been before the public
for so many years, and continually growing in
popularity, is quite sufficient evidence that they
are all they are claimed to be. Taking into con-
sideration the fact that they have been awarded
the Parisian Gold Medal, and that so many thou-
sands of original and unsolicited testimonials have
been received, and may be seen at the Head Office,
some of which appear in his pamphlets, there
should be no difficulty in predicting a great future
for these health-giving Remedies unprecedented
in the History of Herbal Remedies.
About twelve years ago Herr Rassmussen intro-
duced these Remedies to the Australian public by
opening a Depot for their sale at 547 George
Street, Sydney, N.S.W., his present Head Office,
and although at that time he did not advertise m
any journal, the wonderful virtues of these Reme-
dies were known far and wide within a few
months. Orders for them came from every
quarter, not only from all the Australian colonies,
but from all over the world. In 1891 the clamour
for the Alfaline Herbal Remedies had become so
great that it became impossible to supply everyone
from the Sydney Depot. Letters arrived by thou-
sands, many orders came by telegraph, and some
people even travelled over a thousand miles to
make sure of obtaining some of these God-inspired
Remedies. What was to be done ? It was Herr
Rassmussen’s duty to see that every sufferer from
the Bffiod, Skin, Nerves, Liver, and other affec-
tions, whose very life and happiness depended on
these Remedies, should be able to obtain same
without undue delay. Herr Rassmussen had thus
no other alternative than to open Branch Depots
for the sale of the Alfaline Remedies in every
colony, and, if possible, in every country in the
world, as he desired to keep the sale of his Herbal
Remedies in his own hands entirely, and thus
avoid the agency of chemists, who generally have
a substitute which they prefer to sell instead of
the real article. A start was made at once in that
direction, and a very large and palatial Depot
was opened at 150 Bourke Street, Melbourne, in
1891. Immediately after, a fine Branch was
established at 154 Rundle Street, Adelaide, fol-
lowed by the opening of an extensive Branch at
91 Lambton Quay, Wellington, New Zealand. A
large Branch was then established at the corner of
Hay and William Streets, Perth, W.A., and
shortly after another Branch, next to Coolgardie
Hotel, Bailey Street, Coolgardie, and N 0.2 Lin-
dell’s Chambers, Hannan street, Kalgoorlie, W. A.,
not to forget the very extensive Branch opened
some seven years ago at 160 Queen Street, Bris-
bane which is now the Head Depot for Queens-
land, and finally a Branch just opened at Charters
Towers, North Queensland.
It is not unreasonable to ask, “Is it possible
such success should follow unless these well-known
Herbal Remedies are all they are claimed to be,
and is it likely that their popularity should keep
growing to such an extent, unless they contained
exceptional virtues out of the common?” The
evidence that Herr Rassmussen’s Herbal Remedies
contain wonderful virtues is simply overwhelming,
as he possesses thousands of Testimonials to that
effect.
The whole of these Branches are each doing a
large and flourishing business. There is not a
country on the face of the globe where these Re-
medies have not been heard of, and orders arrive
daily from the uttermost parts of the earth.

Mr. Buit.iN writes later:—“The Pills I had from you
were of great benefit to me and others. In one case,
when three hunters and trappers were travelling about
the ranges near my’ place on the Rose River, one was taken
very ill ; his mates intended taking him in a waggon to
Wangaratta (about 60 miles) next morning to see a doctor.
I gave him a few of your Pills. I am pleased to inform
you that, having to pass their camp next day, I found
him up and pegging-out his skins and looking well. This
speaks volumes for your valuable Remedies.”

A Sydney Miracle.
INVESTIGATION BY A REPORTER.
The Interesting Case of Mr. Thomas Jarvis,
WHO SAYS THAT A FEW WEEKS AGO HE WAS
Crippled—Partially Paralysed, Suffer-
ing from Locomotor Ataxia, and Help-
less. Doctors, Hospital Authorities,
AND EVERYONE SAID HE MUST DIK AS HIS
Case was Hopeless. But they were mis-
taken. To-day he is a New Man, full
of Ltfe and Energy, his Crutches he
has Thrown Away, his Dead Limbs have
life, his Appetite has Returned, and, in
FACT, HE HAS A NEW LEASE OF LIFE OUT
and Out.
So much interest has been shown by our readers
in this marvellous case that we have published the
facts again as they appeared in the Australian
Workman a few weeks ago :
One afternoon our representative made his way
to the home of Mr. Thomas Jarvis, 45 Crown-lane,
Ultimo. He was discovered nursing a two-year-
old baby, and his face declared him to be one of
the happiest men in Sydney. He was up and
about and able to answer the knock at the door.
In reply to the preliminary questions, he said :
“You want to know all about my case ? Well, I
am glad of an opportunity to tell you. The facts
may be of value to those who are stricken as I
was.”
“ How long were you suffering ? ”
“ Well, twelve months ago I got so bad that I
had to leave off work, and I have done nothing
since then. About five years ago, next Christmas,
I met with an accident. I was badly scalded
while taking a bath in Pitt Street, Sydney. Since
the accident I have been in failing health, and
after four years of gradual developement of the
malady,'l was forced to gi ve up my billet at the
Glebe Island.”
“ What were the symptoms ? ”
“ Simply indescribable. The pains were some-
thing terrible. They were just about as much as
I could bear. They were for all the world like as
if a saw were cutting my bones in two in every
part of me. I had no control over my bowels and
bladder, and was a nuisance to myself and every-
one else. I was incessantly shivering and shaking,
and could neither eat or sleep. On the 23rd
March last I saw a well-known doctor, and he told
me that my case was utterly hopeless. However,
he gave me an order to the Director of Charitable
Institutions.”
“Did you make use of the order?”
“No, I came home and went to bed, and have
been pretty well in bed ever since. Eive weeks
ago I could not walk across the floor; in fact, I
could nofc.get out of bed. I felt then that it was
all over with me, and only a question of days
when I would give up the ghost. My doctor Was
attending me, and did all he could, by opium and
morphia pills, to ease the agony I was in. I was
under him for five or six weeks. He came to the
conclusion that I was suffering from locomotor
ataxia, or progressive locomotor ataxy, a disease
of the spinal cord, characterised by peculiar dis-
turbances of gait and difficulty in co-ordinating
voluntary movements. He told me that I might
last for ten years, and that I might never get the
use of my legs. (Just then the patient kicked his
legs about with energy to show that medical
opinion is not infallible.) Well, iust as a drowning
man will grasp at a straw, I grasped at an idea
conveyed by the advertisement describing the cure
effected in the case of another boiler-maker in
England, which I read in the Australian Work-
man, by Dr. Williams Pink Pills for Pale People.
I read in the ad vertisement of a case which seemed
to fit my own. I determined to give the Pink
Pills a trial anyway, and sent for a box. The
effect was as marvellous as it was magical. I was
able to eat and sleep as though nothing was the
matter with me. It was the first appetite and rest
I had for many a day. It was incredible. I im-
mediately left the bed and was able to move about.
I have taken four boxes of Pink Pills during the
past four weeks, and am now taking the fifth box.
I can walk about with the aid of a stick, and am
gradually, but surely, recovering my former vigor
and vitality. You see these crutches here?
They were made for me before I got Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills, but I was too weak to use them, and
now I don’t want tliem. All I want is this stick,
and I hope soon to do without it. You see I am
rapidly regaining my strength.”
“ He is 100 per cent, better,” said Mrs. Jarvis,
who had come in and taken the baby on her knees.
‘ Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills have saved his life, and
made another man of him. ”
A friend who called in at this point, said that
Jarvis looked ten years younger that day than he
did a fortnight previously.
“I can now,” continued Jarvis, “have a cold
bath, and all the feeling has come back in my legs.
If I keep on going the way I am, I hope to be able
to resume work as a boiler-maker, and you know
I have to be in the very pink of health and
strength to work at that trade. lam better to-
day than I have been at any time during the past
five years. I ascribe all the change and benefit to
these Pink Pills. They have saved my life. lam
in no pain now whatever, and feel like a new man.
On the mantlepiece there is a box of opium and
morphia pills which the doctor gave me to deaden
the excruciating pain. But they can stay where
they are, for Ino longer need them. Everybody
who knows me has been amazed at the change in
me. They can hardly believe their eyes. My
being about again has caused quite a sensation out
here.”
Such is the remarkable history, and confirms
what is said in all the chief newspapers of this
country of the astonishing cures wrought by Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills for Pale People. They cure
locomotor ataxy, partial paralysis, and spinal
disease, also the many disorders which arise from
an impoverished state of the blood, such as
anaemia, pale and sallow complexion, general
muscular weakness, indigestion, loss of appetite,
shortness of breath, pains in the back, nervous
headache, &c.
These pills are a tonic, not a purgative. They
are genuine only with the full name, Dr. Williams’
Pink Pills for Pale People, and are sold by
chemists and storekeepers generally, or the Dr.
Williams’ Medicine Company, Queen’s Place,
Sydney, will forward on receipt of stamps or post
order one box for 2s 9d, or half-dozen for 14s 9d.

Balmain, Sydney.”

The use of cocaine as a local anaesthetic
is being condemned in the London hospitals,”
writes The Bulletin’s correspondent, “in favor
of eucaine hydrochlorate, called eucaine for con-
vienence. Dentists will welcome the new drug
and gladly discontinue cocaine, the injection of
which is so frequently attended by nervous dis-
turbances and actual danger. Eucaine is non-
ansemic, antiseptic, and less poisonous than
cocaine, a drug that very often developes an ac-
cursed craving for it almost as fatal as the desire
for morphine.”
A very hot, tired correspondent to The
Bulletin :
Went for a trip out West with a guide. Alighted at a
place. Some women with enormous sleeves and men
with flowing pants—camels, Afghans, Chows, heat and
“cuss.” “ Hello!” I said, “ a fashionable place?” “Yes,”
says my guide, “ Bourke.” Went on. Train stopped; I
wondered what for. Looked round; saw a horse chew-
ing rag, a dog, and a pub. “What place?” I asked.
“ Byrock,” he answered. On again. In an hour we
stopped. This time I saw a woman : she got into the
train—her first trip. All the family came to say goodbye
—three urchins, a man, and something that was once a
woman. “Well, goodbye, Liza,” says “Something”;
“ take care o’yerself.” “ What’s this?” I asked. “Oh,”
says my guide, pointing to the group, “ them’s Coolabah.”
On again, with Liza. “What are we stopping for?” I
asked presently. My guide looked out of the window —
so did I. Saw a William-goat and his Nancy. Woman
was holding out a piece of bread and calling “ Nan ! Nan !
Nan!” “ Girilambone 1” said my friend, shortly. Still
on. Passed bark hut (put your hand down the chimney
and lock the door). Two pigs and three children are
playing outside ; and a woman hangs out clothes. I look
up questioningly. “ Selector ! ” was the brief comment.
Five miles further on saw several huts together—a cow,
vegetable garden and a real woman. “ Squatter,” he
sighed, and somehow I sighed too. On till we heard a
whistle, felt a sudden bump, and stopped right in front
of a policeman and a station. Gazed around, and saw on
one side dust, on the other a horse bogged to its knees in
mud. “ Well ?” “ That’s Nyngan.” “Ohl ” I said, and
wondered whether the folks live in the mud or in the
dust. Half-an-hour’s sleep—wakened by falling on to the
opposite seat. “ Look out ! ” said my guide. I looked
and saw some standing posts, some iron on rubbish heaps,
empty bottles and jam tins, stiffened animals, and a Jew
rubbing his hands over flattened roofs. “That’s Never-
tire ! ” lam still Nevertired.
A Maoriland correspondent to The Bul-
letin :
One notable feature of the M.L. election was the
smash-up of the Liberals in the three electorates com-
prising the Hawkes Bay province. Napier and Waipawa
have been wrested from the Govt, by two of the wealthiest
land-owning mediocrities in the province. In Hawkes
Bay “Capt.” Russell, also a wealthy land-owner, beat
Govt, candidate Fraser by about 800 votes. Last election
Russell only beat Reardon by 70 votes, although the
Liberal vote was split, the Govt, candidate stone-broke,
and this election run by a couple of penniless clerks.
Strong men are scarce in H. 8., and good men avoid the
province because its alleged Liberals are the most cranky
and cantankerous lot in the land of aggressive atheists
and crusty Presbyterians. Geo. Hunter, who beat the
Govt, member for Waipawa, is a decent sort of fellow
who has spent about four figures trying to get into Par-
liament, and whose only revealed capacity for legislation
is his ability to flood the electorate with bad beer.
“ Capt.” Russell, leader of the last Opposition, is the sort
of gent who imports every thing—even his pyjamas—
direct from England, and whose hobby is to raise and
command a standing army and run M.L. with cheap
labor. R. D. M‘Lean, who drove the Govt, out of Napier,
is the wealthy owner of Mareakakaho Station, a good
employer and a believer in a milk-and-water form of
theoretical socialism ; the practical brand he considers to
be closely connected with nitro-glycerine. Altogether,
Napier, Hawkes Bay and Waipawa elections are beautiful
examples of the fact that when mediocrity armed with
little money is attacked by mediocrity and plenty of
money the moneyed mediocrity wins the day.

Forsaken .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I cannot blame you ; though a passing bell
Toll in my ears, and deaden other sound,
And light be dimmer as the years roll round •
I cannot blame you—you have chosen well. ’
I loved you—love you ; but lam not weak ;
You would not know it, looking in my eyes ;
My heart-ache is not shown by tears or sighs •
The colour has not faded from my cheek.
When first I knew I did not start nor cry
I think I smiled ! we did not stand alone ;
And though my heart seemed stiffened’ into
stone,
No keen-eyed watcher guessed my agony.
How can I blame you ? Never definite word
And never promise passed between us two ;
You never said you loved me, but I knew, ’
And what your own heart whispered my heart
heard.
Did you not love me ? Ah, the passionate bliss
Of sudden meetings when we feared to speak !
For strongest feeling ever makes us weak,
Being too strong for spoken word or kiss.
Did you not love me ? Ah, the joys and sorrows
Of those past days ! the walks, the silent rides
Down the long valleys, up the steep hill-sides—
Ah, friend ! the sadness now of these to-morrows !
I knew you loved me in those days of old,
But Prudence bade you pause a little while;
And so you waited—with your look, your smile
That still beguiled me when your love grew cold.
And then you saw my soul was made to roam
Above convention, on a wild bird’s wings;
I could not settle down to humdrum things,
Or be oontent to care for house and home.
You loved me—yes; but much against your will;
I was too bold, too modern, and too free;
Outspoken as a woman should not be—
You disappeared, although you loved me still.
One of the two must conquer, one submit;
Which would be harder, which more full of rack—
For me to rein my fiery spirit back ?
For you to spur your soul to roam with it ?
I see it all; the life-long fight for me,
The crushing of all bolder flights of thought,
To gain a place at last, too dearly bought,
By death of individuality.
Far better as it is. And yet, the pain !
Not even the Modern Woman can conquer
Fate.
It is so hard to work, and watch, and' wait:
I love you, dear, so dearly ! though in vain.
And you, you choose the well-worn path of life—
You wish no straying from the beaten track ;
Not even you could call my wild soul back,
Nor mould me to your will, the model wife.
I cannot blame you, for I love her too,
Graceful she is, and gracious, pure, and sweet;
True woman she, from dainty hands and feet
To shining hair, a fitting mate for you.
Go to her, and be happy ! Fare you well; —
Leave me ! You know that I shall always pray
For you and yours ; but on your wedding-day
I hear no sound but of a passing bell.
M.L.
DOTS EILCOX.
A West Coast Incident,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The tram from Queenstown to Tee-poo-kana was
3ust about to start. _ On the platform were the
local doctor, some children, a woman, two diggers,
and the inevitable Salvationist. The diggers
stood a little apart. The taller and younger of
the two seemed flushed with excitement, though
he spoke calmly enough. He had been lucky and
was going home. His friend—not his mate, for
they were hatters —had come to see him off.
Won’t they be glad to see me ! ” says the lucky
man, following up a train of thought.
“My word,” agrees the other a little sadly. He
has not been lucky.
“ Wish the old jigger was ready to start.”
t You’re in a hurry to leave us, Ben.”
“Well you know,” said Ben, apologetically;
then brightening : “ There’s the wife and little ’un
looking out for me.”
“Yes, I forgot.” This softly. The speaker
also has a wife and “ little ’uns.”
“ Wish you’d make your pile, too, Ned,” said
Ben. gently.
. Thanks, old man. I’ll hold on up at the Spur
till the train comes. It’s a good enough show.
Let s sit down.”
They seated themselves on the unfinished brick-
work of the new station-house.
“ Give ’em all my love, Ben,” said Ned, after a
while.
“ You bet I will, old man.”
“ Take your seats, please ! ”
“ She’s ready at last,” said Ben.
He half rose and slipped back. Ned has risen,
and stands waiting. Then he suddenly bends
over the other with: “Ben! Ben! My God
he’s in a fit.”
He calls out to the doctor, who is just getting
into the train. The doctor comes and examines
the fallen man.
“ Is it a fit, doctor ? ”
“No, he’s dead ; quite dead.”
Ned is supporting Ben’s head on his arm when
the Salvation person comes along and looks down.
“ Dead,” he ejaculates solemnly, “and quite un-
prepared. ”
“ How do you know that ? ” asks Ned, fiercely ;
“ answer for your own soul—if you’ve got such a
thing.”
The Salvation man smiles pityingly. He is a
very zealous officer. “I’ve observed the young
man’s way of life,” he said; “I fear he has not
found God.”
Ned allowed the woman’s arm to replace his
own under the dead man’s head, and rose to his
feet.
Very pale, he confronted the Army man, and
hissed out : ‘‘ He didn’t want to find your God,
anyway.”
The zealous officer would improve the occasion.
“I’m afraid, my friend, you are also following
on the broad path that leads to ”
The rest of the sentence was lost.
When Ned came back to attend to his dead he
said he “ didn’t feel so bad now.”
NANCY HOGAN.
In a recent Sydney daily paper there were
no less than eleven separate funeral-notices from
relatives of one corpse.
Two "Larrikins . "
[F OR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Y’ortee do something, Ernie. Yer know how
I am. You don’t seem to care. Y’orter do some-
thing.”
Stowsher slouched at a greater angle to the
freasy door-post, and scowled under his hat-brim.
t was a little, low, frowsy room opening into
Jones’s Alley. She sat at the table, sewing—a
thin, sallow girl with weak, colorless eyes. She
looked as frowsy as her surroundings.
“Well, why don’t you go to some of them
women, and get fixed up?”
She flicked the end of the table-cloth over some
tiny, unfinished articles of clothing, and bent to
her work.
. “But you know very well I haven’t got a shil-
ling, Ernie,” she said, quietly. “ Where am I to
get the money from ? ”
“Who arsked yer to get it?”
She was silent, with the exasperating silence
of a woman who has determined to do a thing in
spite of all reasons and arguments that may be
brought against it.
“Well, wot more do yer want?” demanded
Stowsher, impatiently.
She bent lower. “ Couldn’t we keep it, Ernie?”
“ Wot next?” asked Stowsher, sulkily—he had
half suspected what was coming. Then, with an
impatient oath, “You must be gettin’ ratty.”
She brushed the corner of the cloth further over
the little clothes.
“ It wouldn’t cost anything, Ernie. I’d take a
pride in him, and keep him clean, and dress him
like a little lord. He’d be different from all the
other youngsters. He wouldn’t be like those
dirty, sickly little brats out there. He’d be just
like you, Ernie ; I know he would. I’ll look after
him night and day, and bring him up well and
strong. We’d train his little muscles from the
first, Ernie, and he’d be able to knock ’em all out
when he grew up. It wouldn’t cost much, and
I’d work hard and be careful if you’d help me.
And you’d be proud of him, too, Ernie —I know
you would.”
Stowsher scraped the doorstep with his foot;
but whether he was “ touched,” or feared hysterics
and was wisely silent, was not apparent.
“Do you remember the first day I met you,
Ernie ? ” she asked, presently.
Stowsher regarded her with an uneasy scowl:
“ Well —wot o’ that ? ”
“You came into the bar-parlor at the ‘Crick-
eters’ Arms ’ and caught a push of ’em chyaeking
your old man. ”
“ Well, I orltered that.”
“ I know you did. You done for three of them,
one after another, and two was bigger than you.”
“Yes ! and when the push come up we done for
the rest,” said Stowsher, softening at the recol-
lection.
“ And the day you come home and caught the
landlord bullying your old mother like a dog .”
“ Yes ; I got three months for that job. But it
was worth it ! ” he reflected. “ Only,” he added,
“the old woman might have had the knocker to
keep away from the lush while I was in quod. . .
But wot’s all this got to do with it ? ”
“ He might barrack and fight for you, some day,
Ernie,” she said softly, “ when you’re old and out
of form and ain’t got no push to back you. ”
The thing was becoming decidedly embarrassing
to Stowsher. Not that he felt any delicacy on the
subject, but because he hated to be drawn into a
conversation that might be considered “soft.”
“ Oh, stow that !” he said, comfortingly. “Gi
on yer hat, and I’ll take yer for a trot.”
She rose quickly, but, recollecting that it was
not good policy to betray eagerness in response to
an invitation from “Ernie,” she restrained her-
self.
“ But—you know —I don’t like to go out like
this. You can’t —you wouldn’t like to take me
out the way I am, Ernie ! ”
“Why not? Wot rot ! ”
“ The fellows would see me, and—and ”
“ And . . . wot ? ”
“ They might notice ”
“Well, wot o’that? I want ’em to. Are yer
cornin’ or are yer ain’t. Eling round now. I can’t
hang on here all day.”
They walked towards Flagstaff Hill.
One or two, slouching round a pub. corner,
saluted with, “ Wotcher, Stowsher ! ”
“ Not too stinkin’,” replied Stowsher. “ Soak
yer heads.”
“ Stowsher’s goin’ to stick,” said one pi-ivately.
“ An’ so he orter,” said another. “ Wish 1 had
the chanst.”
The two turned up a steep lane.
“ Don’t walk so fast up hill, Ernie; I can’t, you
know.”
“ All right, Liz. I forgot that. Why didn’t
yer say so before ? ”
She was contentedly silent most of the way,
warned by instinct, after the manner of women
when they have gained their point by words.
Once he glanced over his shoulder with a short
laugh. “Gorblimme!” he said, “I nearly
thought the little blank was a-follerin’ along
behind ! ”
When he left her at the door he said: “Look
here, Liz. ’Ere’s half a quid. Git what yer
want. Let her go. I’m goin’ to graft agen in the
inornin’, and I’ll conre round and see yer to-morrer
night.”
Still she seemed troubled and uneasy.
“ Ernie.”
“ Well. Wot now ? ”
“ Sposin’ it’s a girl, Ernie.”
Stowsher flung himself round impatiently.
“ Oh, for God’s sake, stow that! Yer always
singin’ out before yer hurt. . . There’s some-
thin’ else, ain’t there—while the bloomin’shop’s
open ? ”
“ No, Ernie. Ain’t you going to kiss me? . , .
I’m satisfied. ”
“ Satisfied ! Yer don’t want the kid to be arst
’oo ’is father was, do yer? Yer’d better come
along with me some day this week and git spliced.
Yer don’t want to go frettin’ or any of that funny
business while it’s on.”
“ Oh, Ernie ! do you really mean it ? ” —and she
threw her arms round his neck, and broke down
at last.
“So-long, Liz. No more funny business now—
I’ve had enough of it. Keep yer pecker up, old
girl. To-morrer night, mind.” Then he added
suddenly: “Yer might have known I ain’t that
sort of a bloke ” —and left abruptly.
Liz was very happy.

The Track to True Eyes.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Tracks there are in plenty from the Old and from
the New,
But wherever they may lead to, if they do not
lead to you,
Their terminals are vain, and grey and hodden
Are their skies ;
All their trees are cypresses, and all their flowers
are rue ;
All their throstles curlews are—a-wailing in the
dew ;
Hungry is the heart on the tracks that lead from
you,
True eyes !
Tracks there are in plenty from the Roper to the
Bight—
Hellish tracks men only tread within the deeps of
night—
Tracks where the earth-crust splits and shatters,
Like fire-worn delf.
But these to me are flowered with tend’rest hue,
If but the time to walk You-wards is due,
And gay are all the tracks that lead to you—
Sweet self !
Plains that are green with corn, and sun-kissed
mountains
Gushing a song from throstle-throated fountains,
If they lead not to you are full of ashes
And bitter sighs ;
But brimmed is Thirsty Land with grace and dew,
And the condenser track is Golden New —
So long as it may lead to love and you—
True eyes !
When the bloom of thorn bloods Derwent hedges
And the nether lapping of the river sedges
Leads not to you, dear —all the joys of Spring
And I must part!
But, if the greatest gale that ever blew
Should shriek its tempest, I would never sue
Por quiet if its course led unto you—
Dear Heart!
Believe me all I say—believe me true !
I wish to see no sight but thee anew.
Sweet are Life’s griefs, while Life’s tracks lead to
you,
Chromed are black skies !
But, when you die then shall I hasten to
The Doors of Death in sorrow’s sable hue—
Sweet are Death’s ways when Life’s tracks lead
from you—
True Eyes!
Widgiemoola, W.A.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.

lionaire must go.” To which millionaire Carnegie :
