“ F.C.” to The Bulletin •
The Argus sneers cheaply at the recently-formed ser-
vant-girls’ union, but what class of workers needs union
and organisation more? lam staying at a township
hotel kept by a very nice family, including two grown-up
girls. It is a big and scattered public-house with a large
private house adjoining; the whole of the work of these
two places—which have from three to six regular boarders
and from 12 to 36 at dinner—is done by two servants, ex-
cepting the bar, which is run by the two daughters afore-
said. These two servants are kept continually going between
6 a.m. and 9to 10.30 p.m. Their wages are 15s. per week,
and food— barely 2d. per hour. The owners are very
decent people, and it would be a sin and a shame to call
it sweating; but—what would you call it ?
A Bush Singer .
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Thebe would be some dishonor in it if she were
caught here with Fenton. But since she came
only to listen to his clever talk, it were better to
crouch behind this well-grassed tussock and let
her husband pass. Fenton had already dropped
to the earth, and slowly, very slowly, he brought
his chin to the level of the ironstone reef. A
sludge-covered man blundered across the gully
below; the wind was against him, and the two
ends of a scarlet rag whipped his bull throat. A
Martini rifle rested on his left arm; the uplifted
right was against the sun.
“ Do you think he can see us ? ” Her lips hardly
shaped the words.
“Don’t know; but there’s so much of him
brute that his smelling powers are abnormal.
How’s the wind ?” Fenton watched her lips.
“ He’s taking the lagoon track, and he’ll come
close in here.”
A sudden gust flung her loose hair across the
wet fronds. A whimper of fear broke the silence.
Fenton glanced at her sharply.
“I object to signals; keep your damned hair
back, or the bull may see the flag. Some jobs
come about, others are put up—keep your hair
back ! ”
The woman flinched and stood erect, stung by
a thought that his words only half conveyed.
Already the husband had blundered into the line
of sight, but his head was down and the sun was
in his eyes.
“Do you mean that, Fenton?” He raised
himself slightly, and the suspicion in his shifty
eyes died out. This trembling, passion-wrung
woman was rather convincing, therefore the gibe
on his lips changed to a wheedling note.
“ I apologise ; _ perhaps he’s after ’possum ! ”
She crouched again, flushed and sulking, and the
swelling curves under the wind-blown tunic made
his lean throat parch. Big boulders at the gully-
end shut out the thick-booted husband ; and the
tailor-perfect man poised himself a little valiantly
to ease his conscience with a few remarks.
“ A slip of the tongue only ; let’s drop it.” The
woman covered her face.
“ I could never see much in your Bill; he works
in a mine, shovels down his food, and whistles
‘The Alabama Coon.’ He hasn’t a peg in his
brain to hang a decent thought upon. Why did
you marry Bill ? ”
“Don’t! don’t ! for pity’s sake. . , . He
carried me out of a building once—they said the
shearers had fired it —and the lead spilled from the
open roof on to his naked hands, and he laughed—-
my God ! he laughed ! —because he held me in his
arms. I couldn’t help marrying him, and I’ve
paid for it—l’ve paid for it! ”.
She rocked forward gently, and Fenton peered
through the dusky nook of ferns, but could not
see her face.
“ Partly the fire, and partly his captivating
bulk ; eh, Nell ? I’m sorry for you, indeed. Long
evenings with Bill will put the frost in your lovely
hair. He carves wooden images on Sundays, and
attends the local spitting-match, and everyone
stands back respectfully when your Bill spits ; he
beats all comers, he beat Nick Jones last Saturday
by three inches ; Nick’s training hard for the next
event, and if the beer isn’t faked he reckons that
he has your Bill under the whip.”
She was standing before him, quivering from
heel to throat.
“ You devil! why do you drag in that ? I could
laugh with you if he were dead ; but death misses
him always, as it missed him yesterday in the
shaft! ”
She watched Fenton dawdle from shadow to
shadow, until the dark myall-belt blotted him out.
She had offended him, and henceforth she was to
be shut up with the man who spat. Fenton was
a singing-master, and he had been teaching her
the diva’s score of “La Sonnambula: she had
sung it in the clear solitude of the ranges and in
the silent bush; sometimes the magpies helped,
and, indeed, they chorused appropriately. And
yet a big stage was just as fine. Fenton had led
her to think of Paris ;he had praised her voice
and compared it with Melba’s ; and Fenton was a
clever dyspeptic who never permitted himself to
say nice things. Astride of these castles was the
bull-throated man who spat. Hands locked across
a boulder, she waited for the coming darkness.
Here and there an overflow had flushed the
jungle floor, and the waters creamed over chan-
nelled slopes of clay to fill a hundred cups in the
rocks. A flapping of wings came above the full
sound of many pools—a wonga-pigeon fluttered
across the windless dusk. She groped into a
lighter space, and her coming sent a flock of teal
uptrailing to the edge of a cloud.
She listened, but her furious pulse shut out
sound. The hut door was open; she stepped in
smiling, and stopped. The sludge-covered, thick-
booted man lay across the table with a gun slop-
ing from his knees. Outside, a sapling thrashed
the window, and the room was grey with the un-
certain light that comes before the dark. He lay
very still; a red splash at his feet sent her back,
stiff-limbed and agape.
“ At last! —at last! ” Her head went up; she
closed her eyes so that her brain might receive the
full measure of esctasy. The bull-necked man
named Bill had gone out of her life; oh, the
sweet pain of it! Her nimble feet lifted to the
tune of her pulse; she whirled across the damp
floor, and the mad motion ended in a pirouette.
Monsieur Delaunay, the dancing master, had
taught her the step—excellent Monsieur Delaunay !
The music had come back to her voice ; she
laughed that she might hear it, and the laugh
ended in a cracking discord. The man stirred,
stretched himself, and stood upright.
‘ ‘ Summat’s frightened yo’, Nell ?—summat’s
frightened yo’ ? ”
His huge body moved nearer to hers. Then he
patted her shoulder, heavily and kindly, because
she had grown suddenly limp in his arms.
“ Easy does it, my gel!—easy does it ! Yo’
drink too much tea, and yo’ think too much.
What’s frightened yo’, Nell?”
“I thought—l thought—oh, let’s think ! ” She
stooped cautiously as if she intended to dabble
her fingers in a pool.
“I thought there was something here-some-
thing red and wet; and it’s only your handker-
chief.” She laughed weakly, and steadied herself
against a chair.
Slowly her mistake shaped itself in his sluggish
imagination, and it flattered him exceedingly.
What a devil of a fright he had given this superb
creature !
The silly tears on her cheek gave him a pang of
pride. He straddled across his chair.
“What’s the use o’ frettin’ about it, Nell ? I
ain’t such a bloomin’ fool as to go flyin’me kite
over the valley of the shadder. Gimme life, an’
plenty of ’ard graft, an’ a bit of innercent enji-
ment on Saturdays. Don’t cry, Nell !—-don’t
cry.”
He touched the hem of her sleeve, it was so
near to his mouth.
ALBA DORION.
Johnson’s Fate,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We were swamping back from Lake Darlot rush
last winter with Billy Mills’s camels.
Our pockets were empty, and we had only a few
’weights of gold apiece. My mate, Johnson,
though a tiger for work out back, always de-
generated into a public-house prospector in town,
and I knew that when he had spent his dust he
would chew my ear for my balance in Coolgardie,
so I racked my brain for a plan that would part us.
At Granite Creek, a country of buck-reefs and
barren quartz, I got hold of some porous and
honeycombed stone and artistically stuffed it with
fine colors. This was easy, as I had graduated
with honors in this line, readying up Coolgardie
wild cats for the London market. Sauntering
over to Johnson’s door, I sat down with him on
his rug, quietly dropping the specimens near him.
Ten minutes’ smoking and spitting on the nearest
one disclosed the color to Johnson, who left me
abruptly. Next morning he had decided to camp
at Granite Creek for the next camel-team, as his
feet were too sore to do the 30-mile stage that day.
I departed chuckling.
A week afterwards there was a big rush back.
Johnson had struck it heavy, and within three
months had pegged and sold 200 acres on his
famed Godelphus line—in London.
He is now a representative Westralian in Eng-
land, and in his new role of mining-expert received
50 guineas from a johnnie for telling him what a
winze was.
Oscar Wilde is released, and off to South
America to join his family.
Tom Finney, M.L.A., of Brisbane (Q.) :
“ I met all the Premiers of Australasia in Hobart,
so that now I have the honour of knowing all the
Australasian Premiers personally. I think them
a fine set of fellows, especially that Maoriland
chap, Hick Seddon : he’s one of the finest and
grandest men, speaking as a man, that I ever
met.”
In U.S.A. politics may yet blast futures.
In Indiana two neighbors christened their rival
babies “ Abraham Lincoln Ulysses Grant William
M‘Kinley ” and “ Thomas Jefferson Andrew
Jackson James Munroe William Jennings Bryan.”
Australia may now expect “Frederick Sargood
George Reid Duncan Gillies Henry Parkes Julius
Vogel Smith” and “Graham Berry William
Trenwith Richard Seddon Undesirable Immi-
grants Jones.”
